THE RED PAGE
THE PALE WORKER.
(From the Yiddish of Morris Rosenfeld.)
Lo I yonder I see the pale worker,
Stitch, stitch, without pause, without stay,
Since first I remember him, stitching,
And paler and weaker each day.
The slow months roll on in their courses.
The years are as days that have been,
And still the pale worker, bent double,
Fights hard with the cruel machirif.
But from dawn till the sunset and darkness,
The tear-drops fall heavy and slow.
Till the seams of the cloth he is stitching
Are wet with the vintage of woe.
I pray you, how long must he drive it,
This wheel that is red for a sign l
Can you reckon the years of his bondage,
And the end—that grim secret—divine J
Too hard (i/re such questions to answer,
But this I am bold to declare .-y
When Death shall ha ve slain the pale worker,
Another will sit in his chair.
B. PAUL NEUMAN (in The Spectator).
ISRIEL ZANGWILL, ZIONIST
ISRAEL ZANGWILL has capitulated
to his race. No man can escape his
nationality for long ; his individuality
is pitted against the overwhelming
mass of his ancestry, and the tragedy
of genius is the struggle of one man
against the many in him. Zangwill suffers
from the fact that his race is of all races the
most aggressive, the most isolated. He was
born a Jew ; for a little while he was him-
self, an artist in words; but now his people
have claimed him, and he becomes a Jew
again. He has written great novels of the
universal, like The Master ; he has frolicked
among the absurdities of The Batchelors’ Club;
he has written eight plays of the type of
Merely Mary Ann ; he has exploited the
humor of the Hebrew in The King of
Schnorrers, and his pathos in Dreamers of
the Ghetto and Children of the Ghetto . Then,
suddenly, he felt the crushing weight of his
heritage; he doffed his art to put on his
ancestry : the author became the Zionist,
the artist the pamphleteer. He saw his
race exchange the ghetto for the world, and in
the conquest of that world lose its own soul.
The Jew, so strangely preserved without a
home for two thousand years, is beginning to
betray his tradition; he is winning the
world by merging with it. Once he had no
home ; now in finding a home he is in danger
of losing his homogeneity.
Israel Zangwill saw all this, and it sad-
dened, yet spurred him. He saw no way of
stemming the Anglisation or the Americisa-
tion of the Jew save in Zionism. Writing
books was mere futility when his race was
jlying. He became a Zionist. So now he
writes his Ghetto Comedies (Heinemann),
wherein, a little wearily, he traces the
weaknesses and the strengths of his race,
without much hope, it seems to us, of the
ultimate triumph of his dream of a separate
nationality. Probably it was not the will of
Providence, but the walls of the ghetto, that
have preserved and sharpened the race so
long. And the walls of the ghetto are
down. The Zionists propose rebuilding
them in Palestine. But whence are the
occupants to come ? Zangwill does not dis-
guise his disillusionment; yet he is still a
Jew. So these Comedies are touched with
pathos and a little pain ; the humor in them
is bitter ; he cannot jest without a sigh.
For surely here at last was the true tragedy of
the people of Christ—to have persisted sublimely,
and to be as sordidly perverted; to be king and
knave in one ; to survive for two thousand years
the loss of a fatherland and the pressure of" per-
secution, only to wear on its soul the yellow
badge which had defaced its garments. For to
suffer two thousand years for an idea is a privi-
lege that has been accorded only to Israel—‘the
soldier of God.’ That were no tragedy, but an
heroic epic, even as the prophet Isaiah had pre-
figured. The true tragedy, the saddest sorrow,
lay in the martyrdom of an Israel unworthy of his
sufferings.
Yefc interesting most of these studies are ;
in them are delightful bubbles of the old
wit, outbreaks of rollicking fun, and the
clever character-drawing of The Master.
Zangwill draws for us the bluff English
Sir Asher Aaronberg, the collector of pic-
tures, who nevertheless barred Italian art.
“In Italian art one is never safe from the
Madonna, not to mention her Son.” . . . He
cherished a contempt, softened by stupefication,
for the arithmetical incapacity of Trinitarians.
Christianity under any other aspect did not exist
for him. It was a blunder impossible to a race
with a genius for calculation. “ How can three
be one?” he would demand witheringly of his
cronies.
It is, however, in “The Hirelings” that
Zangwill’s saddened irony breaks most
pathetically out. He sees his race but the
servants of the heathen world it equally
despises and uses.
Jewish geniuses! Don’t talk to me of our
geniusesj it is they that have betrayed us. Every
other people has its great men ; but our great
men—they belong to every other people. The
world absorbs our sap, and damns us for our
putrid remains. Our best must pipe alien tunes
and dance to the measures of the heathen. They
build and paint; they write and legislate. But
never a song of Israel do they fashion, nor a
picture of Israel, nor a law of Israel, nor a temple
of Israel. Bah ! What are they but hirelings ?
Some day when Zangwill is convinced of
the futility of his dream, he may slough his
race and return to himself. He is a hireling
.worthy of his hire.
THE TREND THAT DIDN'T.
Chapter III. —From the Frying-Pan into
the Chimney.
“ Doomed ! ” exclaimed Alice.
“No, Darling, saved !’’whispered the pseudo
telegraph-line mender, rushing to her side.
“ Charles ! ” exclaimed the beautiful girl.
“I overheard the plot,’’ whispered her lover.
“ In this disguise I cut the wire before they could
telephone for the bloodhounds.”
He was interrupted by thunderous knocking at
the door,
“ Here’s my plan. From that telegraph post
the wires sag close to a cottage roof. I’ll grasp
the post and cling by my feet to the window sill.
You will walk over my body, get on the wires,
and slide to safety. It is the only way,”
His sternness stifled opposition.
She crossed his straining body.
“ Courage !” she heard him gasp.
Then her eyes opened in horror.
The three pursuers had dashed in, and had
foolishly rushed together upon the human bridge.
The doomed wretches, and the intrepid Charles,
crashed screamingly down into the abyss.
“John Blackadder, this is your work! Be-
ware!” exclaimed Alice, when she recovered.
Confidently she began her perilous slide.
Suddenly someone below shouted a warning.
Too late!
The wires snapped, and the brave girl fell feet
first into the dark maw of a huge smoke stack.
It proved her salvation.
(More, please !)
[Alice is now falling down a chimney. Her
situation, it seems to us, calls for pathos. Authors
will kindly turn on the tap of tears. Weeps
(marked “Serial”) must be received by this office
by Tuesday next.]
»
Among the most interesting of the contents of
Steele Rudd’s Magazine for August is Edward
Dyson’s review of the reviews of his Fact'ry
'Ands. He finds that Sydney papers are, by far,
the most generous of Australasian critics, and
Melbourne reviewers much the least willing to
give play to the kindlier impulses.
T. D. Mutch comes up smiling, and, incidentally,
has a dirty left:—“John P.” commences a long
boost-up of himself in the Red Page (8/8/’07) by
perpetrating a D pun; in fact, a T.D pun.
That pun on my name is just on twenty-two years
of age. It was made the day I was born, and I
believe my father was the culprit. If “John
P.’s ” stuff is as out of date as the pun, or if I may
deduce its quality from the fact that The Bulletin
man has “scratched the sign of the cross on
the back of nearly all the verse ‘ John P.’ has sent
him,” I am not at all surprised that he has to send
it to America to get rid of it. By deduction,
also, I deduce that those weird, woeful, and won-
derful tales of alleged Australian life we some-
times glance through ip English and American
periodicals are not written by ignorant English
and American writers, but by “unknown” pen-
men (or women) of the “ John P.” Stinking Fish
type, here in these States, or over the way in the
Dominion. “John P.” loses sight of the fact
that those remarks were intended for the literary
aspirant, not for the man who has “bumped
against every big editor worth his salt in
the States.” (Note the American skite.) But
the cap seems to have fitted him very well.
If a literary aspirant has anything in him, getting
an axe and doing something with it Won’t
stop him worth a wallaby’s whiskers. And
when “John P.” says that he has never lost a
manuscript, and never had an important M.S.
marked in any way, well he is stating a happy
experience that is not enjoyed by any Australian
writer I have spoken to on the subject. And how
many Australians are living—mind, living— on
literary (as apart from journalistic) work? Why,
the very best of them clear out as soon as they
possibly can (e.g., only the latest instance, Albert
Dorrington). It’s just about time that an Author’s
Union was formed in Australia, for the purpose
of : (1) Securing a prompt decision from editors
regarding MSS. (2) Payment on acceptance.
(3) Payment at reasonable rates. However, I am
glad to have afforded “John P.” an opportunity
of having a few lines printed in an Australian
paper.
London publishers issue a new sixpenny edition
every day of the year.
William De Morgan, whose Alice-for-Short and
Joseph Vance are, if extremely long, quite read-
able, has “arrived” as a writer of fiction at the
age of 67. Reviewers have hailed him as “a
coming novelist.” There is a moral lurking
somewhere about for the young Australian author.
Nowit is, by Oxford’s interference, Dr. Mark
Twain. Also Dr. Rudyard Kipling.
The sort of book to throw info your bag as you
start for a holiday in the country is Rambles of an
Australian Naturalist , written by Paul Fountain
from the notes and journals of Thomas Ward
(J. Murray). Ward is a Queensland stock-farmer,
and Fountain is a writer, and regards this book as
his foster-child. There are statements in the
book with which we cannot acquiesce; but
generally it is well written. Specially interesting
is his summary of the native blackfellow, and his
description of the submarine life of the Great
Barrier Reef.
WHAT THE AUSTRALIAN WORKER
READS
The three months which I spent, incognito,
among Australian workers, sharing their life and
their labors, their joys and their sorrows, belong
to the most precious of my life. In the Queens-
land shearing shed, on the goldfield, m the
factory in Victoria, in the New South Wales
colliery—everywhere I met splendid, sturdy men,
who were a realisation of Goethe s words :
Let Man be noble,
Helpful and kind.
Each time I set out for one of these laboring
centres my Australian friends warned me against
my mad idea, and talked of the “ rough crowd,”
and the bad time it would give me. Yet never
in all the time I spent as a worker did I meet
with a rough or insulting word : the good fellows
helped the “new chum” wherever they could,
had consideration for his lack of skill, and showed
themselves sympathetic brothers to the new mem-
ber of their guild. He who has learned to know
these men will have no doubts of Australia s proud
future.
Yet many have Sought to overcast this hope
with doubt, because these “masters of the tool”
stood apart from the culture of their country ; have
shed easy reproof on their lack of interest in the
tine arts, their waste of time and money on mate-
rialistic amusements—yet without asking what
was the cause, the reason for it. As a matter of
fact, and in no smaller measure than other
classes, the workers do strive after the ideal good
things of life, when those good things are brought
in their way, and when their powers, mental and
bodily, permit of it.
There in the Queensland shearing-shed was an
excellent library, maintained by voluntary sub-
scriptions of the workers and a Government sub-
sidy. After the day’s labor, the hands gathered
in their “School of" Arts”—their eating-room—
and, this time, sought food for their minds. The
book-case held English classics like Shakespeare,
Byron, Burns, etc., and if, it is true, I did not see
these often taken down, yet good English novels
(like those of Dickens, Ainsworth, etc.) and the
ethnographical works of Frazer had their friends
among the men. Especially glad was I to see
their affection for their own writers—otherwise so
sadly neglected : Lawson's splendid stories and
verses, Gordon 1 , Paterson, Ogilvie, Quinn, Brere-
ton —all spoke to the workers’ unsophisticated
feelings; and once I saw “ Middleton’s Rouse-
about ” going round the circle, carrying hope to
many a young man’s heart that some day, too,
honest toil would make him a master of Australian
soil.
On the tables lay Queensland and New South
Wales papers : it was a feast day whenever The
Bulletin arrived—that untiring champion of its
country and of the worker, ever ready to break a
lance with the defaulter of either ; and the Bris-
bane Worker, with its bitter words for present-
day social conditions, was gladly welcomed and
eagerly read.
Besides the readers, some tried their skill at
chess—and many a drawing-room player would
have found his master among them, If a whis-
pered conversation interrupted the reading or the
play, it was for a certainty about the bitter days
of Clermont, where the shearers fought and suf-
fered for the high ideal of workers’ brotherhood.
Quite different was the scene on the Victorian
goldfields. New deceptions every day, and yet
illusion refused to die ; the hour of rest was busied
with hopes for the hour that would realise the
faith in future that had been carried for years,
aye, for decades. Men sat there under the
gloomy eucalypts, around the fire that burned
before the tent, and spoke of the mysteries of
gold-digging, of friends that vanished or per-
ished, of times old and new : listened reverently
to the white-bearded miner with glittering eyes
who told of his early days in Ballarat,' where the
miners gave themselves the accolade as knights of
free democracy; then quickly turned their talk
back to the gold of their dreams. So, if I never
saw a book on the gold-fields, I can easily under-
stand-it; thejr own life is full of magic and
romance, and the poet’s fantasy would merely set
limits to their own. Scarcely even do they
allow the newspapers to disturb their golden
reverie. Narrow indeed is the world of print
when thought can rove unhindered from the very
origin of the earthly crust to the last fine uses of
the glittering gold.
J*
But if digging has its own charm and its own
accompaniment of dreams, if the spicy air of the''
bush gives the shearer new life and energy for
thought and reading, it is far different in the
factory. All that await the weary toiler are the
dusty air and mean streets of the workers’ quarter,
the bare crude home—nothing to wake him out of
his weariness, a weariness often as heavy as death,
or out of the dulness that is begotten .by the
montony of his task and the din of the factory.
If the handicrafts were once the centre of intellec-
tual life—Hans Sachs and the Meistersingers are
the well-known historical examples—the factory,
which has taken over their inheritance, is its
deadly foe. Scarcely fit to leave school, the boy
enters the horrid gloom of the machine-rooms, or
faces the glare of furnace and oven ; from the
very beginning he has no knowledge of the part
he plays in the general work; the more
perfect he grows in that part the narrower
becomes his field of activity, till finally
he wastes day after day, year after year,
in the repetition of a single mechanical trick.
What wonder if his brain dries up, if the dull and
blunted conditions of his bodily activity reproduce
themselves in his mental life, if he wastes time on
coarse, sensual pleasures, drink and gaming, or, if
he marries, takes a girl whose spirit has been
killed, like his own, in the factory, so that both
y see nothing in their married life but the chance of
easily-bought pleasure. “Spirit must brand the
flesh that, it may live,” says George Meredith—
but what is left alive in such a man as this ?
Naturally, then, he has little pleasure in serious
books; and the Australian press, whose diversity
could help it to be a great educational factor, has
lost all its influence over him. More than one work-
ing-man has told me that he was tired of wasting
a penny on papers that only seized the oppor-
tunity of every strike to scream at the arrogance
and insolence of the worker, to make heroes of
the blacklegs, who stab their fellow-workers in
the back, and to hurl the name of “criminals” at
the unionists who, through hunger and •self-
denial, lead on the van of social progress. The
factory-worker has no opportunity to hear his
own ideas defended in a daily paper, and weekly
publications like 'The Worker of Sydijey or Bris-
bane, however well conducted, bring him the
daily news too late, and rather too much abstract
socialistic matters in one heap. These seem to
be the reasons why these weeklies are so much
neglected.
J*
Colliery work, it is true, is less monotonous
and brain-destroying. But it is arduous physical
toil, and the conditions are unnatural—solitude
in a subterranean gloom. There is no need for
surprise, then, if the return to the upper light
and human companionship is a relief so intensely
to be enjoyed that quiet reading in a quiet room
is a thing unthought-of, and most prefer to chat
with their friends in a bar, or lounge idly along
the brilliant Streets. The only reading is the
scanty local paper—in the case of the immigrant,
very often that of his English rural home. If the
miner is a subscriber to a lending library, he will
be put off mostly with the lowest class of
English fiction, in which names like Corelli,
Boothby, Haggard shine out as lights, The lads,
of course, cannot afford such a subscription, and
destroy their taste with penny novels.
• A most necessary condition, then, which must
exist before the working classes can share in the
general culture of the nation, is the reduction of
the hours of labor in factory and mine, where
work-time should be estimated not only by the
amount of bodily exertion, but also by the soul-
murdering character of the task. Next, an
attempt must be made to meet the intellectual
demands of the people in every way ; for instance,
free popular libraries should be everywhere
established, as in America.
More important than this, perhaps, is the re-
form of, the evening amusements; and here
the State must help. It should introduce popular
concerts, subsidise a theatrical company and send
it on tour so that the people may, for a small
entrie, see pieces of educational value—for the
Australian stage ip flooded with dramatised
“blood and thunder” stories while the workers
of other countries nourish their minds with
Shaw, Wilde, Ibsen, Gorki, Maeterlinck, Anzen-
gruber, and Hauptmann, not to mention the
classics of each country.
A government that forbids gaming and drink-
ing must create amusements to take their place,
if it means to carry out a real social reform. The
State has full coffers : let it not then be miserly of
expenditure for the highest ideal good of the
people. At the present day the worker is often pre-
vented by the crushing or dulling nature of his
task from taking his seat at the table of culture ;
nothing is. done to arouse, to protect, or to nourish
his nobler instincts. Destroy the obstacles, and
the worker will take his part in your culture with
as much sympathy and delight as any.
DE ROBERT SOHACHNER.
(translated by 0. J. Brennan),
Mark Twain’s attack on “ Christian Science ”
is but a slight thing compared with the masterly-
history of the movement now being presented in
McClure!s by Georgina Mil mine. The history has
now reached the point where Mrs. Glover became
Mrs. Eddy. The founder of the new religion set
down her age in the marriage license as forty,
though this much-married lady was then 56. She
stated that a few years more or less were of no
consequence—-which merely shows that the new
prophet was a woman. Her new husband was a
sewing-machine agent, a quiet, dull little man,
who did all his own housework, even his washing,
and usually made his own trousers. “He could
do up a shirt as well as any woman.” Somehow,
that seems the sort of useful husband that a new
Mahometess would like to possess. Feminine
founders of religions are apt to forget to do up their
husband’s shirts. The history is singularly un-
biassed, and every stage in the eventful life of
this remarkable woman, and in the production
of her book, Science and Health, is attested by
contemporary evidence and documents, fehould
this new religion become a power in the land, as
Twain prophesies, the service done for future
ages by such a dispassionate and complete history
cannot be underestimated. It is curious to note
that the book, which has gone into such an
enormous number of editions, fell almost still-born
from the press, in some few instances being
handled severely by sarcastic reviewers.
In The Daughter of a Soldier and Other Stories
(J. A. Packer, Sydney), Agnes Littlejohn has re-
printed a number of short stories originally con-
tributed to The Presbyterian. They are the
sort of short stories that people who like to read
The Presbyterian would like to read.
“The way out of the present militaristic mad-
ness will be found by the first nation that takes
war seriously, or as the melodramatisers of war
will say, cynically. It has always been so. The
fiery Rupert, charging for God and the King, got
on excellently until Cromwell, having some experi-
ence as a brewer, made the trite experiment of
raising the wages of the Parliamentary soldier to
th'e market value of respectable men, and immedi-
ately went over Rupert like a steam-roller.
Napoleon served out enthusiasm, carefully mixed
with prospects of loot, as cold-bloodedly as a pirate
captain serves out rum, and never used it as an
efficient substitute for facts and cannon. Welling-
ton, with his characteristic Irish common sense,
held a steadfast opinion of the character of the
average British private, and the capacity of the
average British officer that would wreck the
Adelphi if uttered there; but he fed them care-
fully, and carried our point with them against the
enemy ” : — G. Bernard Pshaw.
(By The Red Page, Hunt clearn and J.M.
Leape.)

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Girl.
And, after coming all this way.
You haven't got a word to say I
’Twas thus a maiden spoke to me
When walking by the summer sea.
And, ’ere I could collect a thought,
She told me of a hat she’d bought.
And how her aunt had come to stay,
And she herself might go away.
And why she didn’t like the dance,
And wasn’t “playing speaks” with Lance,
With Charlie or with Reginald
Because “ the silly fools ” had called,
And met each other on the mat
And scowled and left, and after that
She simply wouldn’t cross the street
To speak to them. If she should meet
“ Those awful boys ” out anywhere,
But—anyway she didn’t care !
Then—never pausing once for breath—
She told me she was sick to death
Of everything, and now and then
She simply “ loathed the sight of men.”
And wondered why she came with me
A-walking by the summer sea.
And, as she made this sort of fuss,
I felt a bit superfluous,
And prayed that Reginald and Lance,
And ev’ry other circumstance
That made her feel as if she’d die,
Would Then I heard her asking why
On earth I looked so very blue ?
And—“ Please, do say a word or two ! ”
And, looking down into her eyes,
I started to apologise.
When instantly, she said, “ Oh, wait
A second, dear ! ” and did relate
A story, intricate and long,
Of someone who had sung a song
To someone else, and then a lot
Of other sweet and precious rot,
That didn’t matter, anyhow.
Then —once again, the wrinkled brow,
And, “Really dear, it’s too absurd !
You never seem to say a word /”
And while I thought “That takes the
scone,”
She chattered and she clattered on,
Of all the people whom she knew
(I daresay it’s been done to you !),
And so it came to pass that I
Said but four words : “Good-day,” “Good-
bye.”
VIC.
KODAK.
My True Love is in Town.
Grey the meadows are to me,
And blue skies dull and drear;
How can any color be
When she I love’s not here ?
How this changeless, cynic sun
Beats me beneath his frown !
Softer are those skies of dun—
My true love is in town.
Lone and long the country lanes;
Although the song-birds sing,
Still a solitude remains ;
The dark is menacing.
Dearer is the tumult where
She wanders up and down ;
Streets and pavements lead to her —
My true love is in town.
When the streets in grey mist whirled
To nothingness have come,
When the rain across the world
Smudges a slanting thumb ;
When the steely pavements wet
Black shadows deeply drown,
London is the sweetest yet—
My true love is in town.
N.S.W.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
The How and Why.
Far amid the Asian mountains,
In the aeons long gone by,
Sat an animal, half human,
Naked, hairy Man or Woman—
Pondering on How and Why.
Next, in Babylon and Memphis,
Gazed the sages on the sky,
And of Heaven-fire and thunder,
Of the stars and winds in wonder
Asked the ceaseless How and Why.
Greek and Roman, speculating,
Still conceived an answer nigh,
Still believed their human reason
Would, in fit and proper season,
Show them of the How and Why.
And to-day the priest and parson
Bids mankind no longer sigh.
They are wiser than the ages,
They are giants to the sages—
They have solved the How and Why.
But the heart spurns metaphysics.
Greeds are short-lived—soon they die,
And we theorise but vainly,
We but dogmatise inanely
On the How and Whence and Why.
N.S.W.
P.J.H.

How to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there ; the official
always is.

Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, ivhere practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence" column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 8000 ivords, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
M S. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
ore observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 22, 1907.
JEHOVAH AND THE GREAT
LANDLORD.
Some 3400 years ago, if the Scriptural authority is
to be relied upon, Jehovah handed down to His
people a land system, along with a moral code and
a mass of other regulations. It is the one recorded
instance in which the Deity whom Christendom
professes to worship did such a thing; as there
was no further manifestation of the kind, it is
presumed that the policy then enunciated was
good enough for all time—or at least for time as
far as it has got. It was handed down, certainly,
to only one people, but then everything that is
handed down at all must be handed to somebody.
And the revelation was made to the same people
who received the Ten Commandments, and was
made under similar conditions. If the land policy
that was drafted for Israel in the wilderness
was meant exclusively for Israel, then it is
a fair inference that the Ten Commandments
were also for Israel alone, and that the huge
majority which is not Israel is under no prohibi-
tion against murder, theft, and the annexation of
it’s neighbor’s wife. Certainly it is impossible to
hold to the whole letter of the law that was given
to Jacob’s posterity in the wilderness. The rules
dealing with Levites can hardly be followed by
races which, having no kin with Levi, couldn’t
raise a Levite to save their lives. The laws con-
cerning leprosy became extinct in many countries
because even the most strenuous fishing wouldn’t
bring as much as a single leper to the surface of
the sea of events. The immensity of rules and
regulations about the management of the famous
tabernacle can no longer be observed because the
tabernacle has been missing since beyond the
memory of man, and even the smallest remnant
of it can’t be identified. The New Testament
obliterated in one act all the miscellaneous laws
as to sacrifice. But the old Decalogue, and the
old land law, and the old financial regula-
tions were never annulled, nor did anything
ever happen to make them definitely obsolete.
While sin remains the Decalogue will fill the bill
as a moral code ; while the land remains the land
laws of Jehovah, given to the same people who
received the Decalogue, will remain a work-
able code. Time and Circumstances extinguished
the Ark, and the Temple, and the Urim and
Thummin ; and the Cities of Refuge ceased to be
distinguished because every city in every civilised
country became a City of Refuge in the old
Hebrew sense ; but something must survive unless
it is held that all that memorable and most wonder-
ful code of ancient law was mere waste time
and labor. And the things that naturally remain
are those which were never revoked, which were
never made impossible by change of circum-
stances, and which were equally applic tble to all
time and all countries.
* * * *
It is notable that in the land laws of Jehovah
there were no signs of the landlord or the big
estate, and for the rent problem it had no room at
all. It was essentially a system of small free-
holds each cultivated by the owner. That owner
was to sit under his own vine and fig-tree, not
under the landlord’s bit of hired shade. Jehovah
was the first advocate of closer settlement, the
first denouncer of land monopoly, the first and
bitterest opponent of great estates. So anxious
was He to prevent the aggregation of great hold-
ings that He even forbade the acquisition by one
man of another’s farm by purchase; as regards
country lands there was set aside a year of release
every half-century, and the most that could be
done in the way of the alienation of country lands
was to sell the use of them until the year of
release—that great cleaning up, when the
poor were to come into their own again, and
the congestion of wealth was to be checked, and
the nation was to make a fresh start. Town
properties might be sold—something of the sort
was inevitable for business purposes, though even
in such cases the seller had a year in which to
consider the situation, and any time within that
period he might buy back the property, appa-
rently at the purchase price; but the country
lands were portioned out in. small lots to the
Hebrew families at the beginning of things, and
were made inalienable by Divine command. And
so fixed was the Divine law against the aggrega-
tion of great estates that the lending of money at
interest by one Hebrew to another was forbidden.
If it had been permitted, the wonderful processes
of compound interest would tend to make the
rich man, even if he couldn’t legally acquire the
fee simple of the poor man’s land, the perpetual
mortgagee in possession, so even 2| per cent, was
made unlawful. There was an eloquent prohibition
against the removal of a neighbor’s landmark ; and
in later years, when Israel had fallen away from
the law, the most brilliant of its prophets called
down woe on the Clabkes and Manifolds
and the A.A. Co. and the Peel River Co. of that
day—those who “join house to house, that lay
field to field ” ; he classed the big landlord with
the drunkard, and declared that hell had been
enlarged to accommodate them both.
* * • *
It was a wonderful agrarian law—that which
the old Jewish God handed down along with the
Decalogue from Sinai; and it has justified itself
all through history. Every country which has
refused to accept it, or which has fallen away
from it, has been harassed by agrarian revolts or
dry-rotted with internal decay. The saying that
“ Great estates ruined Italy,” as describing
the fall of the Roman empire, has passed
into a byword ; there weren’t enough land-
lords to hold up the eagles, and the
mere Roman with the hoe had lost all interest in
the eagles. The same trouble wrecked Poland.
The Polish landowners couldn’t keep back the
enemy —moreover, being a race besotted with
riches and in the habit of devoting their lazy,
wasted, bloated hours to intrigue and internal
feuds, many of them had forgotten the very
meaning of patriotism \ and as for the serf the
Pole with the hoe—one master was the same to
him as another. Great estates and great fortunes
ruined Babylon; the landlord forgot how to sit
his war-horse because he was too fat, and the
Babylonian with the hoe reckoned that any
change of masters might be a change for the
better. Great estates have depopulated Ireland,
and they are helping England to its fall
by making its people less and less of
a sturdy out - door - living community, and
more and more a race of wharf rats
and slum-dwellers. Everywhere the Divine
agrarian law has wonderfully vindicated itself,
or is in the act of vindicating itself.
And every reformer, and alleged reformer, tacitly
admits the fact more or less. Even the bitter
Tory of the Argus stamp urges the people to get
on the land, though in his desire to preserve the
fortunes of the great stealers of the people’s
heritage he wants them to get there by paying
impossible prices for the land. The fact is every-
where recognised that the basis and foundation of
a strong community is a large, prosperous, and
contented agricultural population. Many other
things are needed, but that is required first of all.
And it is a dead certainty that such a population
is not to be found when the man on the land is a
mere rack-rented tenant. Also it is a certainty
that such a population is to be found in its best
form amid a community of intelligent small pro-
prietors. In that respect Jehovah is justified of
His agrarian policy, the policy which regenerated
Denmark, and enabled Switzerland to retain its
independence against all Europe, and which made
the Transvaal Republic so strong that its people
—who, all told, wouldn’t make the population of
a second-rate city—could hold the world’s biggest
empire at bay for three years, and even then had
to be paid to surrender.
$ * * *
There is no intention to make this a theological
article; the exact splitting of the theological hair
is best left to priests and parsons, and such-like
folk, who possess the necessary implements. It
may be that the old agrarian law of Jehovah
was only intended for local application, or that
it has somehow died out in the effluxion of
years. If the Church says so, this paper will let the
matter pass at that, merely pointing out that ip
saying so the Church accepts the biggest responsi-
bility ever shouldered by man, for if the Old
Testament decree against the accumulation of
great estates has died out, then it is just as
probable that the Ten Commandments have also
died out, and that Geokge Chirnside, and the
owner of the Bibbenluke Estate, and the Duke
of Sutherland, and all the race of great landed
proprietors can be murdered without any moral
responsibility attaching to the perpetrator of the
act. Nor, in quoting the burning words of
Isaiah, the prophet, that there is a curse upon
those who add house to house and field to field,
and that hell has been enlarged to contain them
and their kind, is there any absolute intention of
asserting that the men who dummied huge estates
all over Monaro, and the landlords who piled up
vast properties in the Western District of Vic-
toria are irretrievably lost. But if they are not,
then it can only be because that section of the
Scripture has been written off as a bad debt, and
if one section can be thus obliterated, then the
same may happen to any other section, and the
whole fabric of Christianity totters on its basis, and
all priesthood and parsondom threaten to become
dead things and of no account. Certain sections
of the Church are already dead and of no account.
They are the allies of the great landlord, and eat
his rich food and drink his rich wine, and
denounce any attempt to get back to the vicinity
of the old Hebrew agrarian law as confiscation
and Socialism. And in so doing they forget that
Jehovah, Whose whole system was designed to
promote the equality of man in the Hebrew
Federation, was apparei tly the founder of bocial-
ism, and its first practical Teacher.
» * * *
In Australia there is a party which recognises
that there is prospective ruin in the accumulation
of great estates, in the creation of a small body ot
loafing landlords, and a vast horde of rack-rented
tenants, in the holding of vast areas against settle-
ment to gratify the greed and vanity of the great
monopolist, in the building up of a few vast
fortunes which can only come to pass through the
impoverishment of the multitude. It has tor
its supporters a multitude of historical examples,
and behind them the old Law and the old Pro-
phets. And it has for its opponents the great
landlords, the Tory press, Bruce Smith, the
Women’s League, and certain sections ot the
Church. The matter seems to be, to a great
extent, Bruce Smith and the Women’s League
and Janet Mrs. Clarke versus the Old Testa-
ment. The Democratic party, which the aristo-
cratic section of the Church denounces as a party of
Socialism and Confiscation, aims in a mild way at
restoring the old agrarian and financial laws of
Jehovah —the laws which created a race of small
proprietors, which prohibited the exaction of in-
terest, which prohibited the accumulation of
great estates, which prevented the little farm
being seized by the creditor in perpetuity,
which declared that if a creditor seized
the debtor’s raiment it must be restored
before nightfall, which made the widow’s goods
sacred even against her creditor, which abolished
the bailiff by enacting that no man could enter
a house and take possession of the contents, and
which prohibited the taking for debt of any article
necessary for the debtor’s support. Yet the
crowd which upholds the religious side of the
argument in the great case of the Women s
League and Bruce Smith and J anet Mrs.
Clarke versus the Old Testament, is quite mode-
rate in its proposals as compared with Isaiah, the
prophet. His remedy for the great landlord was
damnation. It proposes, at the worst, a
graduated land-tax, with a maximum of 4d. m
the £, and a £5OOO exemption. There is a
quaint and philosophical toleration about the
party that aims at the breaking up of the
great estates in Australia, and its tolera-
tion is little appreciated by its opponents.
These same opponents, who have a curious ha.bit
of borrowing GrOD to bolster up their iniquities,
don’t seem to have any idea that the old sacred
law is meant for everyday use, and when it is
propounded to them, they take refuge in wild
assertions that the other party desires to “break
up the sanctity of the home.” Also when the
tenets of the Scripture are brought into common
use—when they are quoted against the land
monopolist, the usurer, the adulterator, and the
short-weight fiend of modern life—it has a con-
venient habit of saying that such practical appli-
cation of the Scripture is “ blasphemy.” Probably
even this dissertation on the old Hebrew agrarian
and financial law will be described in Tory circles
as “blasphemy.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Another Loan Curse Looming.
In explanation of the large increase under “ New Works
and Build.ngs,” I would remind the committee that since
the establishment of Federation Parliament has reso-
lutely set its face against loan expenditure, thus
necessitating the outlay of considerable sums out of the
revenue, which would otherwise be charged to loans.
Of course this is a state of things which will not continue
indefinitely, as in the event of the Commonwealth under-
taking large works, such as the transcontinental railway,
it will be found absolutely necessary to have recourse to
loan expenditure.—Australian Treasurer Lyne in his
Financial Statement.
In most political matters The Bulletin has the
pleasure of finding itself at one with William
Lyne —Big William, the sturdy battler from
N.S.W., who has kept up the honor and credit of
his State while small, weary Cook and Kelly
people would fain have dragged it in the mire.
But here, for a time, this paper and William
Lyne part company. It may be that the Com-
monwealth will be driven to borrow part of the
money (a small part, it is to be hoped, with a big
sinking fund attached) for the construction of the
two absolutely imperative transcontinental rail-
ways, and for the opening up of the Northern
Territory, but it will require a tremendous lot of
evidence to prove the fact. And, of course, the
Commonwealth can’t keep quite out of debt, for
it will take over a ready-made debt along with
the Territory, and it will also have to
assume that' bit of the South Australian
debt which represents the value of the
Port Augusta-Oodnadatta railway; but it will re-
quire a whole ship-load of evidence tb prove that
it need remain in that debt any longer than the
day when the debentures fall due, or even so long
as that if arrangements can be made with the de-
benture-holders. Why should Lyne— always
known as a big, hopeful, sanguine William—
propose to give up the honest and solvent ghost
without a struggle? There are necessarily vast
expenditures ahead (there are also vast revenues
by reason of these expenditures), for the recon-
struction of Australia has just commenced; but
the Commonwealth has already done wonders in
the way of meeting out of revenue great liabilities
for public works, which the old corrupt State
Governments used to charge to loans, and it
hasn’t yet got nearly to the end of its possibili-
ties. The fact that there are great things to be
done is merely a reason why the Government
should take stock of its great resources in good
time. At present there are, or should be, these
matters to be provided for
(1) Cost of the transcontinental (or alleged transcon-
tinental) railway to Kalgoorlie.
(2) Cost of the transcontinental railway from Oodna-
datta to Pine Creek on a sft. Sin. gauge, suitable for
swift traffic.
(3) Purchase of the railways from Port Augusta to
Oodnadatta and from Port Darwin to Pine Creek, and
their conversion into a broad gauge line suitable for the
swiftest kind of traffic. (It may also be necessary to take
over the narrow-gauge line from Pt. Augusta to Terowie,
where the S. Australian broad-gauge ends, and turn that
into a sft. 3in. line, too.)
(4) Interest and sinking fund on the Territory’s debt.
(5) A considerable annual vote for making roads and
surveys in the Territory, cutting up land for farms, and
bringing in a swarm of desirable settlers.
(6) A large annual vote for the construction of an Aus-
tralian navy, for small arms and ammunition works, and
cannon foundries, and for the establishment of a citizen
soldiery.
(7) As an item which must come along some day, the
taking over of the State debts, and the establishment of
a general sinking fund.
These are the burdens the Commonwealth has to
face, and even with all these troubles ahead there
is as yet no reason to recklessly sky the financial
towel in despair. Let the Commonwealth con-
sider its present and possible resources :
(1) The surplus now returned to the States over and
above the three-fourths of the Customs and Excise
revenue to which they are entitled. This amount
will be increased under the new tariff—for a while at
least.
(2) Any further gain which may accrue through a
re-arrangement of affairs with the States when the Brad-
don clause expires in 1910.
(3) The revenue (when it begins to come in) from the
two transcontinental railways. .
(4) The revenue (when it begins to arrive) froni Cus-
toms, Excise, lands, mining royalties, etc., in the Terri-
(s) Proceeds of a heavy Federal graduated land-tax for
the breaking up of big estates.
(6— possibly) Proceeds of a Federal income-tax.
(7) The monej' that might be raised by the adoption of
the Canadian banking system, whereby the banks are re-
quired to hold 40 per cent, of theircash reserves in Govern-
ment notes. This would yield something approaching
£10,000,000, and after establishing a gold reserve of, say,
£2,000,000, there would still be enough money available to
pay off the debt of the Northern Territory in one act, and
probably to build one railway as well.
There are other possibilities in the way of raising
revenue, but so far as these suggestions go, they
represent the debtor and creditor side of the case.
And it isn’t a hopeless ease by any means. If the
Australian Government looks ahead, and starts
to collect money together in good time, it should
have a million or two in hand when the first large
item of expense comes along, and should also
know where to lay its hand promptly on many
more millions, without any necessity for thinking
of a loan. The Bulletin urges W. Lyne not
to give way to despair in a financial sense. There
is still hope.
«
The Howl of the Importer.
When the new tariff was announced there was at
first a stunned silence on the part of the im-
porter. He found that at last Australia had got
something that looked like a really Protective
poliey, and it took him 24 hours to get his wind.
At the end of the 24 hours, he drew a deep breath
and delivered himself of a record squeal. But, to
his honor be it said, his squeal was not on his
own account. All his agony was due to the
thought of the sufferings of the poor dear people
under the terrible blow. The increased duties,
said the collective importer, would, of course, be
paid by the consumer. They always were. Some
of the tarradiddles told by the party in the pot
hat as to the impossibility of Australia making
anything for itself, and as to the absolute neces-
sity to keep on importing and paying the ruinous
duty, were humorous. Buzacott & Co. on the
impossibility of making such a simple thing as a
fishing net in Australia: “Made here? No!
There are no fishing nets made here. If there
were it would only mean the employment
of a few girls; but the business would not
pay for putting in the plant.” And yet,
during 1906, the value of the fishing nets im-
ported into Australia —the value at the port of
shipment—was £4751, which probably means
three times that sum to the public. Surely
£14,253 worth of fishing nets is worth employing
a few girls to make. Cusack, of the firm of
Bennett and Wood, lifted up the same derisive
snort re bicycle chains and tubing. Said the
Cusack: “A chain factory, if established in
Australia, would make sufficient chains in two
hours to supply the whole Commonwealth, and it
would cost £BO,OOO or £lOO,OOO to put up tube
mills in Australia, and when they had run one
hour they would have made more tubing than the
whole Commonwealth required.” Let’s see :In
1906 there were imported into Australia chains
worth, at the port of shipment, £49,329, and a
plant that makes bicycle chains could make others
as well. Just imagine a plant turning out chains
of the wholesale value of £49,329 in two hours.
If Cusack were not too slow to overtake a lame
caterpillar he would have started a plant long
ago—and set to work to make money with it at
the rate of £4ll per minute !! ! The figures for
tubing are not available, but the value, at the
port of shipment, of bicycle parts for making up
into bikes, tricycles, and similar vehicles amounted
to £183,749. This little lot would probably be
turned out in about six hours by the Cusack
patent brand of plant. Another importer, who
had the good sense not to give his name for pub-
lication, wailed re pianos : “It simply means this,
that all but the wealthy classes will have to put
up with inferior instruments... or go without.”
So notwithstanding that Beale, of Sydney, is
already making pianos of sufficient excellence
to be installed in the home of the Governor-
General, there is no hope of Australia making
its own music-box. Last year 12,263 pianos,
worth, at the port of shipment, £241,357,
were despatched to Australia. So the average
wholesale value of the piano that is imported into
Australia is £l9 13s. lid. Judging by these
figures, the importer gives but a very poor class
of piano after all, so it is doubtful, in the face of
the figures (which are from the Commonwealth
Trade and Customs Returns for 1906), if the people
will have to put up with much inferior pianos
because of the duty. Apparently, the present
piano is so darned bad that it couldn’t
well be worse. Another wild importer’s agent
declared that if a decent woollen mill was started
in Australia it would make all the woollens re-
quired in the Commonwealth in six months, but
under no conditions could it possibly make them
as good as they are made in England and on the
continent of Europe. The woollen importations
for 1906 were worth, at the port of shipment,
£2,083,754, which means in round figures, that the
consumers paid £6,000,000 for them. A factory
that could turn out that value in six months
could afford to shut up for the rest of the year.
The general result of the wail of the importer is to
leave the public wondering if the importer is as
big a fool as he seems to be, or if he takes the
community to be the out-size in imbeciles that he
appears to the public to be.
Some Fish Stories That Didn’t Hang Together.
Before it starts to tell its story, a fishing party
really ought to see that there isn’t aboard any
stupid person who has an outrageous habit of tell-
ing the truth, even if the truth is only a catfish.
The other day the over-sea steamship cos. put up
their saloon fares 10 per cent. Of course, the
Foreign-trade dailies stuck , the news under the
wails about the tariff. Also Anderson, of the
Orient Co., said the tariff “may have been a con-
tributing factor” ; and Wesche, of the P. and 0.,
“wasn’t prepared to say” that it wasn’t; and
Loth, of the Messageries Maritimes, said that
various things, “with the tariff added in Aus-
tralia also,” were responsible. Then Boner, of
the Nord Deutscher Lloyd, who hadn’t been told
what the fishing party was supposed to say, told
his yarn. Authorities in Great Britain as well as
in Germany wereinsistingon better accommodation
for crews, he said, and the Newcastle coal cos. had
formed a combine and put up coal 2s. per ton,,
and labor conditions (he was talking of Europe)
were much more stringent, and, from about the
time of the Russo-Jap war, prices had been moving
up; so the companies’ managers in Europe had
long been in communication about the matter,
“and finally 22 cos., including many of those
trading to Australia, agreed to raise the passenger
rates. The only port the increased charges, it
seems, do not apply to is Capetown.” So, besides
blowing out the miserable little insinuation that
the tariff was responsible for this too (though even
if it was it really wouldn’t be much to weep over),
the Nord Deutscher Lloyd man at the same time


knocked tlie bottom out of those comparisons of
prices with which the Foreign-traders have
been deluding the crowd; for this steamship
manager explains that, quite apart from the
tariff, prices have been going steadily upwards
even in Freetrade countries. The only place where
managers see they can’t hope to get any bigger
fares is Cape Town—the port of the country that
was going to be made fat and prosperous by a flood
of the same sort of cheap colored labor that some
Chamber of Commerce people want for the Nor-
thern Territory, and which, instead of being made
fat and prosperous, has been made so miserable
and depressed that the steamship cos. can’t apply
an otherwise-universal rule to it. Moreover, it
apparently never occurred to the stupid Foreign-
trade claquers that the rise in fares was a good
reason for staying at home, and keeping our
goods at home. “Goods, of course, do not need
food,” said the wise S.M. Herald, “but the extra
cost of coal, and the increased wages of crews,
must be debited to cargo as well as to passen-
gers.” Wherefore, if Australia has to pay more
for having its wool humped to Europe in bales,
and then humped back again in cloth, the sensible
thing to do is to put on a duty that will establish
factories, and make the humping unnecessary.

The Poor Man’s Troubles.
A feature of the Victorian end of the Foreign-
trade opposition to the new tariff was the tre-
mendous anxiety expressed by almost every
opponent as to the effect the tariff would have
upon “the poor man.” The Argus had big, black
headings, “Wage-Earners Hard Hit,” “Cost of
Living Increased,” and it denounced, with much
indignation, the attempt to raise the price of the
poor man’s necessaries :
The working man will inevitably notice a perceptible
growth in his food bill and his clothing accounts. . .
There must be hard lighting in and out of Parliament to
prevent the burdens on the . . . great army of small
wage-earners being increased without any compensating
advantage accruing to the Federation.
The Argus, of course, does not consider the
establishment of scores or hundreds of new fac-
tories a “ compensating advantage.” T-he journal
then, unfortunately, went into details, and got
some of them from the managing director of
Paterson, Laing and Bruce (the big fluff ware-
housemen). The director was even more indig-
nant than the Argus :
Denims, which used to be five per cent., had been
raised to 35 per cent. These were almost entirely used
by mechanics, ENGINE-DRIVERS and other workers ;
but it seemed to be determined that they should not be
allowed to use this cheap material.
And yet the Argus that same morning had
printed, in its tariff list, the following
Cotton, linen, dungaree, denims (other than striped
and fancy), moleskins and corduroys—British, 5 per cent. :
other, 10 per cent.
So that there is actually no increase at all on
denims if imported (as most of them are now)
from Britain, unless the mechanic w r ears striped
and fancy denims; and he doesn’t do that
as a rule. On the morning following the
announcement of the tariff, there was a whole
row of weeping importers on the Argus doorstep,
and without exception they ascribed their tears
to the awful prospect of the poor man. Some
sample weeps:—
Beath, Schiess & Co.—“ The new tariff would mean a
heavy increase in the cost of living, particularly to the
workers.’’
D. &W. Murray (weep No. 2).—“ There could be no
question that the bulk of the increased cost of living
would fall on the poorer people.”
Paterson, Laing & Bruce (weep No. 3). —“ At the
present time all the better class of blankets were made
locally; only the lower-priced blankets, such as the local
factories did not make, were imported. The poorer
classes, who were the sole customers for these goods
would have to pay a higher price.”
Thus do Fluff & Co. weep because for the future
it will be difficult to import shoddy blankets—
blankets made mostly of cotton—and sell them to
the worker. Until the Federal Commerce Act
came into force it was quite a habit to swindle
the poor working man by selling him these
blankets as all wool, and no importer cared
a tinker s malediction for the poor worker
then. He was taken down remorselessly. Quite
a number of importers, dealers and traders
have had to be pulled up at various times
for defrauding the “poorer classes” by selling
them goods that weren’t up to description. Then
one of the burdens upon the “ poorer people ” in
the city is the extra taxes they have to pay
because the importer’s friend, the rich country-
landlord, declines to pay his water rates, and per-
suades the Government to charge the poor toiler
for quite a lot of the cost of the roads that he
should pay for himself. These rich people in Vic-
toria who are so broken up about the “ poorer
classes” could relieve them quite a lot by becom-
ing honest in their dealings with the Government,
and by lowering rents, and by ceasing to oppose a
reasonable systeni of old-age pensions. And
then, as a side issue, consider the Tory Argus
weeping to-day over the increase—quite an
imaginary increase—in the duty on some kinds of
clothes largely used by engine-drivers, and con-
sider also the amount of sympathy that same
paper gave to the engine-drivers in the stormy
strike days of 1903 ! The Argus weeping over an
engine-driver is a record hypocrisy.

Glass and Bottles.
The Bulletin hears from a manufacturer— R. J
Connelly, of the Federal Flint Glass and Bottle
Works Co., Sydney :
The Sydney daily press has been making a final flutter
to stir up some interest in a lost cause. Since the an-
nouncement of the tariff a number of interviews with im-
porters have been published. All of them say just what
everybody knew they would say. Id wasn’t likely that
the men who have made their tens of thousands of
pounds out of the huge profits on goods bought at the
other end of the earth would say anything else ! But let
any firm start to make the goods on which these lar°-e
profits were secured, and down would come their prices
at once—until the manufacturers were cleared out of the
way ; then the original prices would be brought to light
again. °
Having represented the flint-glass trade of this State
before th<? Tariff Commission, I counselled the trade to
make only fair and reasonable requests. This was done
with the result that the proposals have been adopted in
their entirety. We ask for the duty to be applied at per
cubic foot outside measurement of case, and hope that
this will not be departed from for the following reasons :
—A German manufacturer starts by giving his factory
cost. His works may be 200 miles inland from the coist
and the cost of transit to the ocean steamer is a charge
on which duty can be demanded. Nevertheless that
charge doesn’t appear on the invoice. But to reduce
even the factory cost a very large buying commission
appears on the invoice. After that come a series of dis-
counts—lo per cent., 5 per cent., per cent. A deduc-
tion is then made for cases altogether out of proportion
to their cost in Germany. A further rebate is made of
from 2£ per cent, to 5 per cent. Some of these charges
and deductions are bogus, and when they are not bog’us
they are enormously inflated. Hence, we ask that the
duty be based on measurement only, which will allow of
no manipulation.
We started manufacturing in this State about 10 vears
ago, but soon found out that, under the old conditions
a manufacturer was only another name for a fool. We
then started importing in conjunction, with the result
that, had the tariff been delayed much longer, I should
have developed into a Freetrader, so very little exertion
being 1 required to make a living that way. But if the
tariff in its application to flint glassware passes in its
present form we will stop importing right now, we will
increase our hands right away, and we will make the
goods on the spot, with no increase in prices, relying on
an increased output to recompense us for the duty we
will have to pay on any materials which cannot be produced
locally.
The Starved Australian Factories.
“ Deposits,” said aForeign-trade, paper the other
day, talking about the latest banldng statistics—
“ deposits are still £19,368,938 in excess of the
advances. A good portion of this excess lies at
the credit of various banks in London.” Also it
mentioned that there is £23,711,427 of gold and
bullion lying in the banks’ vaults. A few months
ago the advances —-that is, the money out and in
use—were much less even than they are now, and
there was a larger difference between deposits
and advances. But, finding no other investment,
the men who own the money have either bought
land with it, or lent it to somebody else to buy
land, at increased prices. And increased prices for
land don’t add one farthing to the wealth
or the wealth-producing power of the coun-
try ; a farm won’t produce a blade more
grass, or put as much as a pound on
to its pumpkin crop, because in a jobbing trans-
action in Pitt-street or Castlereagh-street some-
body has given 10s. an acre more for it than it
fetched last year. The bulk of the increased ad-
vances have gone that way ; very little, indeed,
has gone in providing machinery and works to
manufacture some of the £42,398,589 worth of
goods (exclusive of specie and bullion) which
Australia imported last year. The Bulletin
tries to keep on the track of all joint-stock com-
panies formed for all sorts of purposes in Austra-
lasia, and in the past 12 months it has overhauled
more than 1000 balance-sheets; but there hasn’t
been one manufacturing co. added to the list.
And from the records of those that are on the
list, nobody can wonder at it. Manufacturing
concerns fall under three headings; they are dei
voted to the production of food, clothes, or tools.
Take, then, a typical case from each divi-
sion to see what inducement there was
under the old tariff to put more capital
into that sort of investment. First as to
food. The best-known co. is Swallow and
Ariell, founded in Victoria under the Protection
of the old State tariff. For the past five years it
has paid altogether 3f per cent, on its capital, or
an average of three-quarters of 1 per cent, per
annum (15s. dividend on each £100). That is
very alluring! On its ordinary shares the
S. Hoffnung Co., which imports things
from Germany, Britain, and elsewhere, paid
14 per cent, for the past year—say 18i times
the manufacturing concern’s average. Turning
to the next division—clothing—there is the
Queensland Woollen Mills, Ltd. That co. paid
H Per cent, in 1901; it has not paid a farthing
since. The Parramatta Woollen Co. has had much
the same experience; it has paid nothing since 1903.
The Castlemaine Co. has been paying 3 per cent.,
and even then appears to be wearing out its
machinery and not providing for its replacement
out of capital. The Ballarat Co., the most success-
ful of all the Australian woollen cos., is paying 5
per cent, on the capital actually put into it; the
dividend is represented as 10 per cent, because in
the early struggle to establish the business half
the capital was written off. In the third division,
Mort’s Dock and Engineering Co., of Sydney’
stands in a singularly favorable position; yet 6
per cent, is this company’s maximum. Other
cos., less favorably situated, have either gone into
liquidation or merely hang on in the expectation
that Australia will see the folly of allowing its
own industries to be crushed out of existence.
Before the new tariff was announced, James
Martin & Co., Ltd., of Gawler (S.A.), whose
machinery works were, a few years ago, under the
S. Aus. State tariff, the biggest in the Com-
monwealth, had called a meeting to con-
sider voluntary liquidation. Walker’s, Ltd.,
of Queensland, has paid no dividend since
the old Queensland State tariff went in 1901.
Every branch of Australian manufacturing in-
dustry tells the same tale. Australia supports
many factories, but they are foreign factories; it
employs hundreds of thousands of workmen, but
they are foreign workmen; it has millions for
investment, but, on the admission of the Foreign-
trade paper, £23,711,427 lies in the vaults of the
banks in Australia, while vast sums are also “at
the credit of the various banks in London”—
financing industries there, in other words, instead
of here; employing labor there instead of here.
And then Australia wonders why it doesn’t get
population!

The Fresh Outbreak of Joseph.
All last week there rose at intervals the uncouth
dog-howl of Joseph Carruthers complaining
about “Victoria,” and weeping bitterly. If it
were possible to suppose that the undignified
N.S.W. Premier believes his own tales, then it
would really seem that “Victoria” has become
with him a mental disease, or hallucination, or
form of dementia—a possession by an evil spirit
after the manner of the Gadarene swine, the
“ fixed idea ” which leads ultimately to lunacy—
something akin to the devils which holy but un-
washed men of old saw in the deserts and waste
places. For instance, if the theory of wilful lying,
petty slander, and a savage desire to stir up strife
and make Australians hate one another is left out
of the question, and if the assumption that
Joseph is suffering from an almost impossible
amount of ignorance is held to be untenable, on
what sane or sober basis could this tale be
accounted for ?
In regard to the increased duty on wire netting, Mr.
Carruthers remarked yesterday that there was an impres-
sion that a factory for the manufacture of this article
existed in Victoria ; but this Was a mistake, and the mat-
ter was thus made much worse. “ The Government
determined two years ago,” said he, “to manufacture
wire netting in the prisons, and machines for the work
were ordered from manufacturers at Auburn, and are
now being made in New South Wales. So that the
netting is to be made by Victorian prisoners, and thus
the farmers will have the consolation of knowing that in
paying this 35 per cent, extra duty they are giving more
employment to prisoners in the Victorian gaols. They
are to submit to the rabbits and dingoes and other ver-
min, in order that the people in the gaols in Victoria may
have more work.”
Now even the most malevolent insect should
know that a gaol industry doesn’t require Protec-
tion, for the simple reason that it has gratis
labor, and that it isn’t charged rent for the gaol,
nor gas bills, nor taxes ; it has only to pay for raw
material, so it can compete easily in an open
market. That is one point on which the snarling,
fish-fag Premier set himself to mislead the public.
The other point is his deliberate suppression of
the fact that the wire-netting manufacture in
Australia is, except for any trifle of netting that
may be made in the future in Victorian gaols, ex-
clusively a N.S.W. industry. It was established
in the old sham “Freetrade ” days by the simple
process of putting a duty of £3 per ton
on the imported article, and it still sur-
vives, and employs something like 400 hands.
The great Lysaght firm, of Sydney, turns out a
splendid article that sells all over Australia, and
with the Australian market secured to it the
Lysaght firm might be ten or twenty times the
size jit is, and employ 4000 or 8000 hands, and be
able not only to supply Australia, but to compete
in foreign markets. But the miserable Car-
ruthers—traitor even to his own State and his
own city—didn’t so much as mention Lysaght’s
concern; apparently his Freetrade hatred of
Australian manufactures extends even to the
point of ignoring Sydney manufactures. The
Victorian and Queensland and S.A. Protectionists
are ready and willing to advocate Protection for
the exclusively N.S.W. industry of piano-making,
and the almost exclusively N.S.W. wire-netting
industry, and to pay bounties to the N.S.W. iron
industry, and to help along with tariff aid the
N.S.W. sugar industry ; they do this because they
are Australians, and these are, in their view,
simply Australian industries. And, in return for
their willingness to help the N.S.W. wire-netting
industry all they know, the Victorians receive the
coarse insults of Carruthers, who seems to have
left all political decency behind him, and become
in his public capacity a naked maniac running
amok. How N.S.W. comes to tolerate this squall-
ing, ill-mannered kid at the head of its State
affairs passes comprehension.

Oil, etc.
The Argus, lamenting over the working-man’s
wife:—
All her purveyors had followed the example of the
Colonial Sugar Company, which on Saturday morning-
advertised that treacle and golden syrup had been ad-
vanced in prices 30s. per ton , as a consequence of the
duty of 30s. per ton, and she knew that the rise would
mean at least a halfpenny a pound, to her.
It is only a few weeks since it was announced
that the C.S.R. Co. had to run thousands of tons
of Australian-made treacle to waste every year
owing to there being no market for it. In view
of this fact, the above announcement is nothing
short of scandalous. The co., without a word of
reproach from the Argus, brazenly increases its
prices no less than 50 per cent, ahead of the duty,
and that, too, a duty that it won’t pay at all,
because it doesn’t import treacle, but makes it
locally. If this gigantic imposition is really to be
committed, it isn’t a reason for the abolition of
Protection, but it is good reason for the estab-
lishment of huge Government refineries to ob-
literate the Colonial Sugar Co.’s monopoly. Then
again:—
The worst loss of all was on kerosene, which is in thou-
sands of homes used as an auxiliary fuel, as well as for
light... The small, portable stove is a feature of every
humble kitchen, which is often tlie’only means of cooking
the poorer workwoman allows herself. The kerosene
had been advanced in price from 3s. 6d. per 4-gal. tin
to 4s. 6d., and that shilling meant a blow to even a well-
to-do worker. To the poorer people who buy their kero-
sene by the quart the advance was a penny the quart.
From this it would be imagined that all kerosene
is now under a duty. But kerosene arriving in
bulk or in packages of more than 10 gallons is free.
The Shell Oil Co.—a British concern—imports
the whole of its oil in bulk, and refines it here.
It is good oil, and cheaper than the stuff from
John JD. Rockefeller’s refineries, and is very
largely used already by the Argus’s poor unfor-
tunate working woman. The Shell Co. hasn’t
raised its price, but the Argus wholly ignores
both it and its oil. Presumably the fact that it
employs Australian refinery men, v box-makers,
tin-makers, etc., has made the Argus so mad that
it has cut the Shell Co.’s acquaintance.
Imported Jewellery—Genuine and Otherwise.
Another shocked importer has been up at the
Melbourne Customs Court making remarks. He
was charged with invoicing as 9-carat certain
rings which were found to be wax-filled and
“ shelled ” with gold of 2 J-carat —if such stuff can
be called gold. The presumption was that these
“ brummy” rings were intended to be palmed off on
a confiding public as 9-carat gold articles ; but the
importer swore that he had inspected the things
in the Cold Country, had seen the 9-carat stamp
on them, and had paid for them as such ; in
fact, he had, as shown by his invoices, paid
more for them than for other genuine 9-carat
articles in the same shipment. According to
the Customs Collector, these “brummy” rings
cost the importer from 16s. 6d. to 41s. per dozen,
and he showed others, genuine 9-carat, a trifle
cheaper. The Customs Collector admitted they
had been invoiced too high, considering what
they were ; also there was no complaint that the
genuine ones had been invoiced too low. Now,
the interesting point to the public which buys
retail is this : Genuine 9-carat rings cost this
importer from Is 4|d. to 3s. sd. each. Allowing
for freight, and say, 50 per cent, profit, they were
worth from 2s. 3d. to 6s. each—but where will
you buy a 9-carat ring for 2s. 3d., or even 6s. ?
Unfortunately, the importer could not say who
made these frauds; he bought them from a mer-
chant, and didn’t know whether they were built in
Birmingham or Germany or Kalamazoo (which is
near Chicago), so that about all he can do is to let
the swindles go, and decline to have any further
dealings with the man he bought them from. The
Commerce Act is daily doing Australia a tremen-
dous lot of good, but it can’t do all that is re-
quired. This case shows that, besides the Com-
merce Act, an almost prohibitive duty is
required on jewellery—as well as on many other
articles. Australia produces all the gold and
most of the gems it can use, and if 9-carat
jewellery can be made as cheaply as these invoices
seem to show —they passed the Customs, anyhow
—there is no reason why the importer should reap
about 200 per cent, profit. He might as well -be
forced to turn manufacturer, and pass on at least
100 per cent, of his profits to his workmen. Then
if the goods are made here they .will be made
under our own eye, and the chances of 2J-carat
rubbish being passed off as 9-carat goods will be
lessened. We can reach the Australian who
works that swindle; but an Australian Commerce
Act isn’t long enough in the arm to reach the
foreign fraud who does it.
