The Freetraders’ Financial Test.
While newspapers like the Melbourne Argus are
avowing their “acquiescence” in the Protective
policy of the Commonwealth (though each single
and separate duty is made the occasion for a loud
and mournful shriek) they cannot, all the same,
prevent themselves from opening dusty cupboards
and drawing therefrom old and mouldy skeletons,
which rattled their bones in the fiscal arguments
of half a century ago, and are still to be found
exposing their naked pallor in the ’Ome files.
One of these rattly specimens of mildewed oste-
ology came forth to shake its fungus-covered limbs
in the Argus last week. It was this :
A country’s imports are the things it buys.
Its exports are the price it pays for them.
Therefore, when its exports are more than its imports,
it is paying too much for what it gets.
Australian exports are 69 millions sterling, and imports
are 44 millions sterling; therefore (after allowing for
payment of interest and debt redemption) Australia is
being Drained of its M r ealth.
How mad and gibbering a skeleton this is may
be guessed by supposing that things were the
same, only a little more so. Imagine a country
so productive of all sorts of wealth, and so well
protected by tariffs, that its exports were worth
£1,000,000 and its imports were worth Is. 6d.
Then the Freetrade argument would be :
This country buys goods worth Is. 6d.
It pays a million quid for them.
Therefore, it gives away for nothing the sum of
£999,999 18s. 6d. per annum.
Or imagine, again, that things were the other
way round, and that the country EXPORTED
goods worth Is. 6d., and IMPORTED goods
worth £1,000,000. Then behold !
This country buys goods worth £1,000,000.
It pays Is. 6d. for them !!
So other countries are making it a present of
£999,999 18s. 6d. !
In fact, the whole of the Argus theory is so
childish that a boys’ school should see the futility
of it. There is no such rule about exports and
imports as the Foreign-trade paper alleges. A
surplus of exports may mean poverty or wealth.
A surplus of imports may mean poverty or wealth.
All depends on the reason of the surplus. The
excess of imports may signify that a country is
borrowing abroad and that the loans are
arriving in the shape of goods (which means
debt and poverty), or that it has many
millions invested abroad, and that the interest
arrives in the shape of goods (which means
that it is rich). An excess of exports may
mean that the country is frightfully in debt,
and that the excess goes to pay interest (which
is poverty), or that the excess goes to pay
interest and reduce the principal (which is miti-
gated poverty with a prospect of better things
ahead); or the excess of exports Imay mean that
the country is out of debt, that it is rich and self
dependent, and that it is sending surplus wealth
abroad for investment, and is gradually buying up
the earth. The United States exports twice as
much value as it imports because it is rich and out
of debt, and is investing money abroad; Spain does
the same because it is a beggar and in debt, and
in pawn abroad. The world is full of similar
examples, and if the Argus can’t see them, 100
pairs of spectacles would best meet its case. Its
spurious tale, regarded as a reason why we should
open our ports, and let in plenty of imports, and
kill our own industries in order to avoid being
“Drained of our Wealth,” is thinner even than
the story usually told by the person in the dock,
to explain how the pewter half-crown came to be
about his clothes.
The Boarded-Out Infant and His Friends.
The Infant Life Protection Bill, which Chief
Secretary Peacock is about to escort through
Vic. Assembly, may do something to prevent
the awful waste of child life, or it may urge the
parents and foster-parents of the undesirable
infant into new and subtler ways of disposing of
the incubus. A lot depends on the administra-
tion. The Bill provides that boarded-out children
are to be inspected until they are five years old,
instead of till they are two, as at present; that
the inspection is to be done by the Neglected
Children’s Department instead of by the harm-
less, uncouth constable; that, prior to being
boarded out, infants must be medically ex-
amined, and even then the consent of the
department must be obtained to the pro-
ceeding ; that all payments to nurses must be
made through the department, the minimum
being fixed at 10s. and the maximum 40s.
Why Jones Didn’t Leave “Home.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
John Jones, who was an Englishman, informed
his wife that he
Intended emigrating to a land across the sea,
Where the bailiff couldn’t haunt him, and the
workhouse wouldn’t be
The final habitation of his youthful progeny.
So he obtained an atlas, and perused it by the
light
Of a small imported candle as he lay abed at
night;
And his wife, in desperation, held him very, very
tight,
Lest the countries, that he mentioned should
entice him from her sight.
With eager hand he pointed to the Great Aus-
tralian Bight !
“ ’Tis there,” he cried, “that I will go—the land
of pure delight,
Where everyone is happy and the sun is always
bright ! ”
But here he felt that Mrs. Jones was trembling
with affright.
She shuddered at the mention of Victoria and its
farms;
Of New South Wales and Tassy she had vigorous
alarms.
But when he spoke of Queensland, and its verdure
and its charms,
She broke up absolutely, and she fainted in his
arms.
“ O ! Don’t go to Australia, John ! where immi-
grants are drowned
By order of the Government, wherever they are
found,
Where the Labor legislators by a solemn pledge
are bound
To mutilate the stranger who may chance to be
around.”
And then she told him all about the Petriana
Myth,
And showed her husband extracts from a speech
by Mr. Smith.
She told him of the ‘ 1 Hatters ’ ’ and the ‘ ‘ Potters ”
who were flayed,
And the terrible experience of Mrs. Tozer’s
maid.
Then he thought to pacify her by referring to the
fact
That the worker is protected by the Arbitration
Act,
But she simply paralysed him when she answered
with a sob,
“ What’s the use of Arbitration if you haven’t
got a job?”
So Mr. Jones decided, as he scratched his human
dome,
That he wouldn’t leave his missus and go speed-
ing o’er the foam ;
And she was quite contented when he promised
not to roam—
But Australia lost a citizen when Jones
REMAINED AT “HOME.”
WESTBALIA.
TOM BEASLEY.


Death to Prickly Pear, Lantana, Briars,
Poison Plant, Blackberries, etc.— For successful results
in spraying it is necessary to have a very powerful sprayer
working at about 1001b. pressure; the ordinary hand
pump is useless. An energetic man might be able to
work at 801b. pressure, for a minute or two, but he can-
not keep it up all day long. The Tyree Spray works at
any pressure up to 1501b., and it does the work without
labor. Therefore, in starting out to kill prickly pear or
any other noxious vegetation it is necessary to have an
up-to-date plant to do the work. The rest is easy. It is
not necessary to cut the 1 bushes, as the leaves absorb the
poison. All plants have the power of absorbing chemical
compounds when sprayed on their leaves. This power
varies with the plant and with the chemical. The novice
is very apt to put on too much by using a coarse spray ;
this is fatal to successful results. The Tyree Spray
throws a very fine spray, that enables the mixture to be
built up on the leaves in a similar way to dew at night
time. By having a very fine, fog-like spray the mixture is
blown all over the vegetation, with the result that very
little is lost. With a pump throwing a coarse, rain-like
spray, three-fourths of the mixture falls to the ground,
and not only fails to effect the result but ruins the soil
at the same time. With a Tyree Spray and Tyree
Mixture you can kill any vegetation. For full particulars,
write to Trrkk Limited, 314 George Street, Sydney.*

whether he will happen casually on the vacant
allotment or transpire unobserved on the banks of
the Yarra or elsewhere—Heaven only knows.
But if this Bill, when passed, doesn’t cause a
sudden lowering of the death rate of Victoria’s
irregular infant, then a State Foundling Hospital
—where all small derelicts will be received and no
questions asked—will have to be established as
the only possible remedy for what has too long
been a scandal to the country.

Wire Netting Troubles.
Joseph Carruthers plays a low game on the
N.S.W. “ primary producer ” these days over the
wire-netting duty. Carruthers prances about
the country like a bad egg, denouncing the 30 per
cent, duty, and rousing the cocky to gusts of hairy
indignation, by explaining how he imported wire-
netting to save the cocky from being devoured by
wild rabbits, and served this Socialistic netting
around on tick ( i.e ., mostly at the country’s
expense), but the Australian Government is now
going to ruin the cocky, etc. And to lend veri-
similitude to Carruthers’ narrative, the N.S.W.
Lands Department is notifying applicants for
Socialistic cheap State netting that owing to the
duty the price has been largely increased. Mean-
time the gulled cocky is cheering the benevolent
Carruthers, and howling at the wicked Com-
monwealth. And it hasn’t struck much of the
cocky yet that, as far as Carruthers is concerned,
there isn’t any 30 per cent. duty. As State
Treasurer, Carruthers is entitled to receive
back three-fourths of the Customs revenue (in
practice he receives back much more than three-
fourths), so that if Carruthers pays the 30 per
cent, duty on State-imported wire netting, at
least 22J per cent, will come back to him— i.e., he
won’t pay in the end more than 7£ per cent., and
will probably only pay 5 per cent. At the very
most the increase in the price of Carruthers’
imported netting should be only per cent.—
and it might easily be a lot less. What the cocky
should get into his head is that when Carruthers
charges him 30 per cent, duty, he is charging 22£
per cent, of that by a mere confidence trick,
devised for the present purpose of vilifying the
Federal Government, and the future purpose of
enabling Joseph to pose as a statesman who
achieved a “surplus” amid overwhelming diffi-
culties. Besides there is another aspect of the
case. Carruthers says solemnly, in various
Sydney papers, that any duty on wire netting
purchased by the State Government is illegal,
because State “property” is free of Federal
taxation, and he says that the action of the Aus-
tralian Parliament is “a horrible state of affairs.”
Now if he believes in his own alleged theory, he is,
of course, going to sprint like blazes to the High
Court, and stop the 30 per cent, duty so far as his
Government’s present and future wire-netting is
concerned. But apparently, despite his loud
talk about the absolute illegality of the Federal
action, he has a dreadful feeling, when it comes
to details, that he hasn’t a leg to stand upon. He
has, seemingly, such a gloomy conviction (in
practice) that his case (about which he is so confi-
dent in theory) hasn’t one durable leg that his
Government doesn’t think it worth while waiting
till the case is tried before it adds the duty to the
price of the netting. It might at least pause till
the matter ha§ been tried, just to keep up an
appearance of faith in its own belief, so to speak.
The Bearers of the Anti-Borrowing Flag.
Although neither of the weak political leaders of
N.S.W.—neither Joseph the Unsatisfactory nor
James the Loser of the ’Bus—has had sense
enough to raise the banner of honesty and
solvency, and to declare against the borrowing
curse, there are not wanting individual candidates
who have more courage. The soured Mother
Gummioge solicitor whose bright young soul was
blighted by Federation, and the stout, terribly-
ordinary boiler-maker who always jogs along in
the dust-cloud just at the tail of the missed
vehicle, may be willing to let things slide for
ever ; but such men as W. E. Vincent, who is
reaching out for the seat at Gough, and Hutton,
who proposes to shake things up for Gregory
Wade at Gordon, and Carmichael, Labor can-
didate at Leichhardt, are keeping the cause of
honesty alive. And there was never a time in all
N.S.W. history when there was more reason to
keep it alive. The great opportunity has come, and
is apparently about to go. Last year, N.S.W. had
a splendid alleged “surplus”—that is to say, it
was able to pay its way, to meet the cost of all its
repairs and patches and of its new public works
out of revenue, and also to reduce the public debt
by an infinitesimal speck. Of course, it only did
this by using about £1,000,000 of land-sale money
as revenue, though land-sale money isn’t really
revenue, but money raised by the sale of assets,
and the distinction is as great as that between
money received as rent for the house and money
obtained by the sale of the house. Still, the per-
formance was a remarkable one for N.S.W., and
the fact that it was the wise policy and the splen-
did economy of the Australian Government which
supplied the means, and that the Australian
Government got the curses while Joseph took the
credit, is only a side issue. Now N.S.W. is at
the parting of the ways. It can go either of two
roads. One alternative is to keep taxation
on the present level, to keep a firm hand
on expenditure, to abolish borrowing (except
for the renewal of old loans) by Act of
Parliament ; to keep the payments into the sink-
ing fund at the present rate, but to make it a
real sinking fund accumulating at compound in-
terest for the extinction of the old debt; and to
make the payment of, say, £1,500,000 a year into
a Public Works Fund a permanent charge on the
revenue. N.S.W. has got into a position, thanks
to Federation, in which it can do these things—
in which it can begin to be honest and to extract
itself out of the bog of debt. AND THAT
POLICY WOULD PAY. It would not only
pay the country as no other policy would, but it
would pay the individual politician. It would
buy votes wholesale. It would appeal to the
people as a great, honest, upstanding policy, and
something quite out of the usual rut. A party
which put honesty and solvency and absolute self-
dependence at the front of its programme, and
preached these doctrines in the voice of Stkntor
or Boanerges would scoop the pool. It would
also fill the bill and gather in the stakes. Then
there is the other alternative—the small, crawly,
retail alternative. This is to go on borrow-
ing, and to dissipate the surplus in a petty
sidelong buying of votes —one here with a
subsidy, one there with a remission of taxes, one
the other place with a reduced railway rate. It
is the sort of method which apparently appeals
greatly to the retail soul of Joseph, the State
Rights Gummidge, and (in a less degree perhaps)
to that of James, the Chronic Loser of the ’Bus;
therefore the first adopts it, and the second doesn’t
vigorously oppose it. Either way there are votes
to be bought; but there are far more votes to
be bought by the first device, and the man who
adopted that device would also get an advertise-
ment that would be heard all over Australia, and.
all over the Empire. His trumpet would be blown
in the ear of all the English-speaking world as the
man who saved his State, and as the first states-
man in the Empire who had the courage to make
public thriftlessness an offence by Act of Parlia-
ment. But it requires courage to strike such an
attitude, and if Joseph and James prefer the
small, humble side-track where there is no fame,
no advt., no trumpet, no saving of the country—
well, perhaps they are the best judges of their
own political dimensions. Anyhow, The Bul-
letin extends a cordial handshake to those war-
riors of the good cause, Hutton of Gordon, and
Vincent of Gough, and Carmichael of Leich-
hardt, and wishes them all success.
How to Abolish, the Betting Market.
“O.K.” writes : The humbug incidental to anti-
gambling legislation was strikingly illustrated in
Melbourne Age of August 6. In one column
appeared a report of the annual meeting of mem-
bers of the Victorian Racing Club, at which the
chairman said :
With regard to the fees charged to bookmakers, it must
be admitted that the sum is a reasonable one, particularly
when it is remembered that betting is now confined en-
tirely to racecourses.
And in thq next column, under the heading of
“ Sporting Notes,” the public was informed :
Attention was given yesterday (at the bookmakers’
clubs, during settling time) to the Caulfield G. N. Steeple-
chase. There was a run on Boomerang, who advanced to
the position of a strong favorite two points in front of
Cardinal, Blueskin, etc., while two points better odds
were offered against The Sun, others in the market at ex-
tended prices being Swimmer, etc., etc.
The statement made by the chairman of the
V.R.C. gets a flat contradiction on the same page
YES-NO.
I hope the supine Freetraders who allowed men like Messrs. Sydney Smith, Lonsdale, Conroy, Lee, and
Hindle to be beaten, will feel now the consequence of their stupid indifference. —G. H. Reid.
THE LAST FREETRADER: “ Strike me pink, George, you assured us fiscalism was dead, and the
only question was Sosh and Anti-Sosh! Is this what you call being dead? ”
of the paper, where it is reported ; nevertheless it
is a truthful statement “within the meaning of
the Act.” Cash betting, except on racecourses,
is forbidden by the Legislature, which also
vetoes publication of the odds on future racing
events. Nor is it clear that any of the wagering
reported was actual cash wagering. The bets are
understood to be booked, like spec, transactions
on the Stock Exchange. The prohibitory Act is
made to look ridiculous in so far as it is frankly un-
reasonable. By authorising the licensing of book-
makers for the convenience of horse-backers on
racecourses only, and by suppressing the publica-
tion of odds calculated to invite the outside public
to bet on future races, the Act fulfils the reason-
able desire of people who discourage the excessive
gambling habit as they discourage-the excessive
drinking habit, but who have no fanatical yearning
to ‘ ‘ put down” the moderate indulgence of either of
them. Where the Act trifles with common sense
is in pretending to ignore the fact that licensed
bookmakers must inevitably transact betting busi-
ness in town, as well as on the racecourses. One
betting club, and only one, in each city should be
lawfully open to bookmakers, registered owners,
and trainers of horses, and any other recognised
persons in the Turf business world, the club
being directed by the same committee which
is empowered to license the “ books ” on
racecourses. It is an absurdity to legalise
betting with the bogus intention of con-
fining it strictly to the racecourse. One lawful
betting club as an adjunct to the lawful open-air
gambling places might be so worked that it would
achieve an almost complete extermination of the
illicit haunts of small gamblers—the silver hells—
where the great bulk of the real gambling evil is
done. Bookmakers, under present conditions,
are supported by horse-owners and heavy backers
even in the States where the totalisator is licensed.
They meet the demand for early investments, by
the “stables,” on future events. They lay the
big bets at long odds which turn out to be good
or bad—mostly bad—according to how the gee-
gee progresses before the day of the race. And
because of these ‘ ‘ stable commission ” considera-
tions many owners are antagonistic to the totali-
sator, seeing that it diverts a lot of punting money
from the coffers of the Moses fraternity. Hence
the need for such an extension of the
totalisator license as would satisfy every-
body now interested in turf gambling—except the
bookmakers. The machine should have a race-
course monopoly of the betting, as far as starters
are concerned. And there should be a machine
for the legalised betting club—a big “all-in”
Tote where any of the hundred or two of entrants
for an important race could be backed as soon as
the weights came out. Arranged on a system of
£5 tickets, with a limit of 400 tickets for each
entrant, the Club tote receipts on a Melbourne
Cup might amount to, say, £30,000 at least, and
the stable that got in early and took a big risk
about its “good thing” might stand to win
£20,000, at fairly long odds, before the racecourse
totes for starters only were opened to speculation.
By legalising this exclusive receptacle for early
investments, the Government could strike a death
blow' at the bookmaking fraternity, and put an
end to the betting market. The odds that the
tote lays are an unknown quantity until the
accounts are closed. The machine is a silent
bookmaker that doesn’t regulate its prices, or
create false favorites, or bribe jockeys, or practise
chicanery in any form. Yet we have a mob of
noisy rogues licensed to bet in the two largest
States of the Commonwealth, whilst the totalisator,
controlled with due regard for the limitation of
the Gambling Evil, is sternly forbidden by hypo-
critical Governments, in the alleged interests of
public morality.

Faked invoices seem to be part of the importer’s
stock-in-trade. It is the S. M. Herald’s turn
now. Last week, under four flaring headings, it
threw a curse at the new tariff, and announced
that housekeepers hadn’t realised the horror of it,
“ but the following series of invoices should open
their eyes.” And then it proceeded to fake five
invoices without a blush —and faked them so
badly that it made “syds. of flannel at 1/6” work
out at 8/9, “ two pairs of socks at 1/- ” come to 2/4
and “ 281 b. mixed paints at 4d. per lb.” arrive at
12/-. The Herald isn’t honest enough to say that
it is printing purely fictitious invoices; in fact,
over one of its fakes it is dishonest enough to
print—“ Those about to furnish will do well to
look at the following invoice from another firm.”
It would be interesting to know what dead-and-
gone John Fairfax, who did leave some
traditions of honesty behind him, would think of
that sore of political campaigning. He, honest
old soul, hadn’t been trained up to the modern
Tory idea that a man can play the three-card and
confidence tricks in matters of politics and still be
a gentleman.
S.M. Herald is very sanguine about the result
of a very small experiment. It says :
The decision of the Commonwealth Government to
arrange for the military training of rifle-club members
will probably affect about 30,000 men.
Which is the idea of the local Rifle Ass., as put
through its secretary, whom the Herald had
interviewed. The old lady then proceeds to
expound on her own account: —
The idea is to build up mobile forces. It is not in-
tended to put riflemen through any ceremonial drill, but
to inculcate military efficiency. Thus, as population
increased, if rifle clubs multiplied in proportion, the
Commonwealth would have, in addition to its regular
troops and militia, a reserve army in the making.
Just what will make these troops “mobile” is
not stated, nor is the thing specified that is going
to deal them out “military efficiency.” So far
as numbers go, there are about 18,000 rifle-club
members in Victoria, 11,000 in N. S, Wales, and
12,000 in all the other States. That tots up to
41,000 in all. Granny is sanguine enough to
believe (in print) that 75 per cent, of that total
can be relied on to go to drill. The Common-
wealth only expects 1500, and will probably not
get that number. And anyhow the idea of the
“ mobile forces” is simply a joke. If anyone of
the clubs, with an average of 55 members in
N. S. Wales, or 60 in Victoria, were required
after 10 years’ inculcation of military efficiency
(whatever that may be) to make itself suddenly
“movable,” and take the field, it couldn’t do it.
It couldn’t feed itself, supply itself with cart-
ridges, or arrange for the care of those of its
members who got hurt, or fell ill on the march.
A scheme for inculcating military efficiency
which doesn’t even attempt ‘ ‘ ceremonial drill, ” and
which will rope in but a score or so of belated
individuals here and there, is so negligible as
hardly" to be worth mention.

In these delightful days of early spring
even G. L. Goodman, who sits alongside the
Sydney end of J. C. Williamson’s cash-box, and
looks as though he has weathered ten thousand
storms of applause—even he feels the sap stirring
at his heart, and breaks forth into poetry. It
isn’t much poetry, it is true, but it starts suddenly
and gets there quickly—
Andrew Mack
Is coming back,
and if you put plenty of white before and behind,
it fills up a two-inch advt. The Blarney is,
however, not back yet; it has still the
best part of a fortnight to go in Melbourne.
There was time, therefore, for another help-
ing of hash, so “The Belle of New York ” was
put on the plate at Her Majesty’s. “ The Belle ”
has been a hard-worked item in J.C.W.’s property
list; Rose Musgrove played in the name-part only
the other day, it seems. Yet the daring eccen-
tricities of the quaint foolishness are a perpetual
delight, and Fred Leslie’s nimble footwork as
Blinky Bill, the lightweight, is a joy for ever. It
gives Lauri a rest, too. He has, certainly, been
the reformer from Cohoes before to-day, but the
singing part is too heavy for him; and Alf
Stephens has been brought over to the co. to
intone the moral precepts of Ichabod Bronson.
Stephens gives a good, sound performance on the
old lines. Just as Blinky Bill might have been
built for Leslie, the Polite Lunatic might have
been designed for W. S. Percy ; to an amusing
picture he adds a touch of feeling when,
apropos of nothing, he calls for a ton of
coals and makes a final exit. It is a fine
little piece of business that. Miss Florence
Young, upon whom the honors of this season have
fallen every time, has here a part that doesn’t suit
her. Miss Young can be many sorts of girl;
but not the demure sort that the Salvation Army
lassie is supposed to be, and she drops all effort to
suggest demureness in the second act, and lives
right up to her change of clothes. Miss Alma
Barber is a dainty Fifi, who puts brains into the
part, but not much warmth into her love-making;
Reginald Roberts is but moderately effective as
Bronson’s boy ; and the rest are not remembered,
while the chorus isn’t as good as usual.
* sfc * *
At the Palace, Miss Baines is still forming
herself into a limited liability co., and giving away
shares in property that doesn’t belong to Merely
Mary Ellen at all, but to the other girl mas-
querading in the kitchen. The public likes
“ Miss Lancashire, Limited,” and the accent of
the Lancashire farm hand is spreading, and her
laughing songs and ballads are finding a paying
echo.
** * *
“ Hoppy ” bounds and blusters nightly to
good houses at Sydney Criterion, and no change
of bill is yet suggested. Two members of the
Hopkinson Co. committed matrimony last week ;
yet the play doesn’t argue much in favor of matri-
mony. The people who are in the blessed state
don’t seem happy, and the people who are not in
it don’t seem to expect to be happy when they get
in. Yet the players go and get married. Thus
does a great immoral lesson fail in its purpose.

“ A Great Awakening,” in which mostly a
man awoke to find a beautiful fiend in a blazing
red dress hammering him with the fire-irons, went
finally to sleep on Tuesday night. On Wednesday
“A Warning to Women” was to have been
staged at Sydney Royal, but at that time The
Bulletin was struggling through the press, and
it knows not this week what is the character of
the warning. Most likely it is a lurid one and
wears a large hat with a dissipated feather in it.

The Tivoli bill is the best mixture that
house has had for months. The newcomers in-
clude the Geraldos, four people who do a sur-
prising juggling act with hoops and clubs, and
make a picture out of it. Fred. Graham and
Miss Dent continue their little comedy; the
Svengalis get near the end of their season, which
hasn’t come up to the glory of their posters; and
Lex McLean and Gus Rennert, the wrestler, are
arguing as to which can lift most dead-weight.
They have agreed to settle the matter on Friday
night. There doesn’t seem to be promising ele-
ments about a performance which consists of two
men lumping pieces of iron ; but the top part of
the Tivoli has got quite excited about it.

“ Human Hearts,” a drama that has lately
been ravaging Maoriland under the direction of
remorseless Geach, arrived at Melb. Princess’s
on Saturday. In its manner of endeavoring to
make an audience laugh, weep and shudder, this
quaint production is wholly American. The
most prevalent character is a Tattered Tramp,
who drops into the proceedings as though he had
escaped from a Rickards co. to relieve the gloom.
And there is a prison scene which seems to have
been adapted from “The Man from Mexico,” and
where the Tramp asserts his usefulness by bring-
ing a free pardon for the village blacksmith who
had been duly convicted of parricide. By way of
apology for its impossible happenings, the drama
offers the public sundry conventional inducements
to weep. It has a blind old mother, a faithful old
negro (cast in the mould of Uncle Tom), a,
crippled bov, a semi-idiot brother, a nice girl
who quotes Scripture, and the inevitable cheeild.
All of these characters contribute words and
deeds which are accepted as “ affecting ” by many
people, especially the amphitheatre matron who
has brought up a family of eleven, without count-
ing the three she lost. Two other characters,
exclusive of the heroic blacksmith, are his
wife and her awful paramour, but the two
guilty people are destitute of human heartage,
and they come to grief in the last act. When
the male villain has foully murdered the Huy m
the presence of witnesses he is led awjy by the
police, leaving the witnesses to perform a touching

The amazing Bent tells his Brighton con-
stituents of his travels (Age, 16/8/’07)
I went across the Indian Ocean—an awful trip—
(laughter)—and got to Ceylon. Some of you know
Ceylon ; some of you have seen the almost nude niggers—
(laughter)—when they go down for sixpences. A great
sight it is, especially for the ladies. (More laughter and
smiles from Mr. Bent.) Then I landed in Ceylon, “ where
every prospect pleases, and only man is vile.” (Laugh-
ter.) There are lots of fat niggers, fatter than I ani, and
you should see them tempting the ladies—(great laugh-
ter)—to buy all sorts of things. They began by asking
for 200 rupees, and ended by taking 10. They offered
me cats’ - eyes for 30 rupees, but at the
finish I got them for four. (Applause and laughter.)
There was a terrible dust storm, and it was hot. I had
a bad time ; we all did, and the unfortunate ladies
thought they were going to die. Then we got to Port
Said, that dirty place. (Laughter.) It is a dirty place.
(More laughter.) As one passes through there he is liable
to take up a disease, and I told Lord Cromer what sort of
a place I thought it was. Well, I was very anxious while
I was there to find out where Moses crossed over the Ked
Sea. (Laughter.) I was shown one place by some
people, and other places by others. I asked our
Governor (Sir Reginald Talbot), who was travelling
with us, if he could tell me. The reason I asked hjm was
that he had lived in Cairo for a number of years.
(Laughter.) But none of them could agree—even the
ministers of religion did not seem to know for certain—
and I can’t tell you yet within a few feet the spot where
Moses did cross. If England wants its Empire it should
look after Port Said, and see that health is one of the
things it has in its Empire. (Cheers.) Then we saw what
some people call Mount Vesuvius, but I have another name
for it. (Laughter.) It was in active eruption when I passed
by—very active eruption. (Laughter.) Some of the ladies
begged the captain to steer a different course, but he said
he thought we would get through all right—(laughter)—
and we did. I went ashore at Marseilles and was taken
to Notre Dame, a big place stuck up on a hill. It is the
place the sailors used to go to and pray for a safe voy-
age before they started out; very often the voyage
proved safe, too. There is a lot of lime and cement dug
at Marseilles. Etc., etc.

pious girl is charmingly presented by Miss Guild-
ford Quin, who wears the right facial expression
for the part, and speaks it to perfection. “ ’Unian
’Earts ” is asked to run for 12 nights at the Prin-
cess’s.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s (Melbourne) the weakest
■of Andrew Mack’s pieces, which is “ Jack
Shannon,” was put on last Saturday, and will be
put off on Friday. In “Mack Shannon,” the
decorative leading man is rather less decorative
than usual; also he is handicapped with a heavy
father, who suspects him of robbing a safe, and
the principal business of his heroism is to persuade
his uninteresting parent that he was wrongfully
suspected. As usual, the unalterable Mack
nurses a child, and bursts into.song, and frequently
rouses the house to enthusiasm with a remark
which ends in “ begorra.” This Saturday brings
a revival of “ Tom Moore,” the variegated drama
that sets forth Mack’s valuable stock-in-trade to
the best advantage. One night of “Arrab-na-
Pogue ” ends the season on the 29th.
* * iff *
“The Bondman,” in its third week at
Melbourne Royal, continues to be marred by a
false ending. Jason, who is rather anxious to be
shot and buried, and has made himself comfort-
ably drunk in anticipation of his doom, is saved
from execution by an obnoxious old grandfather,
whom he reluctantly recognises as such. This
appears to be Bland Holt’s own original notion
of a happy finish—the reconciliation of an
unwilling grandson with a hoary tyrant. Instead
of a martyr’s crown the interrupted Jason will
presently gain his relative’s earthly possessions,
and fall a bloated victim to his taste for liquor.
The future of Jason is a sad subject for conjec-
ture. He had stated, ere grandpa spoilt the
climax, that his execution was desirable because
it would prevent him from dying in a ditch. He
didn’t wish to goon fighting the hard black bottle
of life. In his reprieved state he seems pretty
certain to pass disgracefully away on a spring
mattress, with ten doctors in attendance, which is
the least artistic decease a confirmed inebriate
can arrive at.

The last nights of the tall, affable Wizard,
at the Bijou, are announced in connection with
the news that Charles Holloway will come along
next week and revive “ Two Little Vagabonds.”
Also, twelve last nights of West and the
Brescians are now being reeled off at the
Athenseum Hall.
* * *
The first, of Coward’s Picture Concerts
eventuated at Melbourne Town Hall on Saturday
evening. That temple of the winds was filled by
folk who showed their approval of the rational
tariff—it varies from a meagre saxpence to a
cheap little florin—by turning up early, and
applauding hard. The West Biograph spoils one’s
appreciation for the samples that are hired out to
occasional entertainments. Therefore the sight-
destroying sections of the Coward “Pop’’only
elicit an adjective of condemnation. The prin-
cipal feature was the first appearance of the Har-
monists—four males who mixed their voices
melodiously enough in some charming old compo-
sitions and a couple of humorously-intentioned
new ones. Mr. Coward, who is an alto,
sang some familiar ballads, into which he intro-
duced certain trumpet-like falsetto notes. He is
perfectly trained, but, somehow, when a stalwart
six-footer, with long grey hair, chortles in the
tones of a choir-boy, the effect is—oh, well, never
mind ! Mr. Avalon Collard made polite jokes,
and sang one or two ditties. Miss Warman,
a pig-taded damsel, billed as “The Brilliant
Soprano,” carolled a waltz-song or so in a capti-
t a ting healthy mezzo voice. Altogether the show,
barring the biograph nuisance, was well worth
whatever coin one paid-up to a florin.
* * *- *
The bill at the Opera House has not been
refreshed by a novelty since Edward Cranston
and co. became responsible for an Irish Racing
Sketch of surpassing foolishness. Last week’s
reference to this pretentious trifle omitted to
mention that Carden Wilson is the redeeming
feature of the co. Earlier in the evening Wilson
gives his clever impersonations of people he has
met, and if he were to introduce a second series
into the racing sketch, whilst Cranston made
himself scarce, the Rickards’ entertainment
would be strengthened in its weakest link.
Happily some new “turns” will occur on
Saturday.
** * *
Adelaide has roused up suddenly in the
way of amusements. The Holloway Co. has
moved out of the Royal with the corpse-in-the-
eellar dramas on which it squanders so much
good acting, and has made way for Leslie Harris
and “ Living Paris,” which jingling combination
of music and pictures makes a respectable pro-
gramme. The Tivoli has been starring the quaintly
talkative Carlton, and at the Town Hall the
rather time-worn Albani is succeeded by the
vigorous and up-to-date “Besses” Band. The Lan-
cashire men were much impressed at getting a
formal “reception” on arrival from Governor,
Mayor, and Premier (Tom Price says that he is a
Welshman by birth, but a good Liverpool man
by early training). The centre of general attrac-
tion, however, is the Exhibition, where the
Women’s Work show has just started on a reckless
10 days’ splash, with rifle-shooting, needlework,
hockey, photography, and other feminine occu-
pations as side-shows.

AT POVERTY POINT.
The Bulletin is asked to state that the musical “ All
Blacks ” are touring S. Australia. It really doesn’t know
the All Blacks from crows ; but, as they have advertised
in this paper, they are evidently aborigines of discern-
ment, and Brother Bills who know where to get good
value for six shillings. Therefore, on general principles,
The Bulletin hopes that at Yudnamutana the orchestra
will have to sit on the roof so as to give more accommoda-
tion to the crowding youth and beauty of the place, and
that the “ Blacks” will be able to wind up their season at
Tantanoola without having to play “ Uncle Tom’s Cabin ”
to an audience of one tiger.
“0.K.”: With all my heart I say “ ditto ” to “ Billy T.’
when he denounces the stage Irishman as the most
palpably fraudulent of love-makers. To me it seems that
the “purty ” speeches, in which the nauseating treacle is
laid on with a trowel, should be not less unacceptable to
a woman than Paddy Blarney’s self-confident manner of
uttering them. Of the art which conceals art there is
not a suggestion in the posing, sighing, speech-making
Patrick. The brazen insincerity of the blarney-monger
reflects contempt for the intelligence of any girl who
could delight to listen to him. This, from a non-irish-
man’s point of view. But I have been told by an ex-
ceptionally clever and wideawake girl (of Irish parentage)
that “Andrew Mack makes love beautifully,” or “like
an angel,” and so I am confirmed in my old opinion that
women—for all their claim to refinement of taste—are
essentially “ bad form ” in their emotional temperament
from a man’s point of view. Their critical faculty is but
little developed where flattery of themselves is concerned,
and although they laugh at the gullible man whose vanity
they feed, the bigger laugh is on his side every time. A
little compliment goes a long way with the average man.
A big dose of flattery, such as women crave for and
swallow greedily, would sicken him. So the woman’s
instinct warns her to be cautious in helping him to
treacle. Moreover, her love-making impresses him be-
cause it comes natural to her. She is the born
actress. But the average man is not an easy
artist in pretence. His acting is not nearly so
, subtle as hers. Looking back at one’s own little
successes in love-making one remembers that they
were marked by the thinnest and most hackneyed o'f
histrionic effects. The girl was satisfied with nothing
less than wild exaggeration. No expression of
the yearning spirit—in the rapt gaze, the tender utter-
ance, the eager embrace—could be too florid for her
liking. She wanted melodramatic ardor. A man who
has never tried to make love theatrically (because he isn’t
built that way) may think himself quite satisfactory,
seeing that an occasional girl accepts him, under silent
protest, as the only sweetheart that offers. But a
histrionic wooer, who affects an air of sincerity, can
always cut out the matter-of-fact fellow. And may be
the most successful Lothario of all is the one who plays
down to the mere blarney level of the stage Irishman.
The widow of Dan Leno (“ the only genius the British
music-hall stage has yet produced ”) remarried last
month. In the early ’eighties, the lady, then Miss Lydia
Reynolds, was well known as a ballad singer. Her choice
is a very youthful alleged comedian named Best, which
(vide the Jersey Lily, Ellen Terry, etc.) affords yet
another instance of the ex-stage lady’s passion for picking
up things young and callow in the male line.
Walter Baker, for many years the hero in Bland Holt’s
companies, has reached London. Walter is a Sydney
native and son of an old political agent, Edward Sadler,
well known in electioneering matters a generation ago—
what time James Martin and Henry Parkes wrangled
in Parliament. Ted Sadler was a cordwainer, who
had served his apprenticeship at Miller’s Point, when the
“Black Dog ” (afterwards “ The Ocean Wave,” and now
known by another “ Shellback ” name) was the head-
quarters of the “Rocks” electioneering push. There
was another political shoemaker in the neighborhood—
Hussey, of Cribbs Lane.
Highly condensed dramatic criticism by “ Sydolt ”
A dramatic club recital occurred at Glen’s last Tuesday.
One Arthur Clarke was responsible. The Comet still
shines. Friends, relations, and other fiends stamped the
affair a success—financially. Genius skulked—fled on the
first bar of the overture. Then once again was W. Shak-
speare maltreated for the pleasure of the mob. Avenging
spirits stalked down the aisle, but the aspirants voiced on
to the end. The bright star of the evening was kiddy
Donald Cameron—he of the ginger mop in Mack’s “ Tom
Moore ” Co. This youngster respected the Bard of Avon,
but he tackled Browning! “An Incident of the French
Camp” was a big task, but the little fellow possessed
dignity. Can the Comet spare a tail ? One
damsel “ did ” Lady Macbeth. They should have
stopped her, for Tragedy has its limits. “Denver’s
Dream” was attacked by a good-voiced youth whose
effeminacy of action and coiffure made you hysterical.
It’s wicked to watch an elocutionist of some ability
tenderly sweeping the uncut locks from his eyebrows
while he cries, “No shelter, no escape—ah, no hope. Oh
God ! Oh Gawd 1 ” A lisping Lady Teazle was also there.
A rather versatile Jewish youth played Shylock in the
Trial Scene. Result: a pawnbroker’s grumble. One sweet-
faced performer, in trying to get the best results, had
cultivated a South of France accent with a dash of
Tittell Brune in it. Have you noticed how the female
amateurs imitate Matilda? Anyway, the South of France
lady wasn’t too awful. She meant all she said, and knew
what she was talking about. It was only the way she did
it. She got through with a shove. Others were encour-
aged with the same shove,-but fell outside the gate, and
were pushed through. Shoving was allowed, but push-
ing meant Death. Others recited with mediocre results.
Managing such a Club spells Cash. Girleen recites, and
her friends flock to hear her out of sheer spite. That
means buying a ticket, but a sly, isolated giggle at
another’s expense is cheap at the price of a ticket.
“Blue Peter”: “Theßondman”costumesarebeautifully
vague. The programme fixes the date as 1850. In the Isle
of Man, Maggie Knight wears an Elizabethan dress, and
Frances Ross an indefinite peasant costume. Charlie
Brown has a modern brown suit; Maxwell, the latest thing
in flannel suits, with one of the new kind of collars ; and
George Rignold, a brigand get-up, trimmed with a blood-
stained handkerchief and a long knife. George is fond
of large slouchy hats and brigand outfits, as witness his
huge base bass conspirator in “ Called Back.” In Sicily,
Maxwell dresses like David Copperfield, and Frances
Ross’s hat and other fixings are the newest things for the
coming summer. Bland seems to have let them pick any
old century they liked. I wondered if some buxom
walking lady would wear blue fleshings and rabbit-skins,
Ancient Briton style, but no one did, worse luck.
London Sporting Times in a recent issue chuckled un-
kindly over the coming discomfiture of a personal friend
of its staff, who wrote to it from Maoriland that he had
heard the Besses o’ th’ Barn were due there. He
meant, he said, to be early on the wharf to meet them,
“ to get the pick of the muslin before the other bounders
chip in.” There is something almost pathetic about Percy
Adolphus with his supper-proposals in his hand, bringing
up with a shock against a crowd of stalwart Lancashire
male ruffians.
“ S.” : Miss Ada Ward says Australia is cold and dead
to religion. “ God is not wanted here,” she said in Mel-
bourne, “ and I am leaving on Tuesday.” There is an irre-
verentinferenceinthis, that the melo-evangelist is taking
the Almighty with her. It is the egotism of all evangelists ;
they cannot help identifying their little side shows with
the Creator, and consequently the person who refuses to
flop at their bidding is a scorner of Providence. The
notion is quite common amongst clergymen. For in-
stance, I know of one plump parson (something of a
notoriety in his way) who, on being made the subject of
several caricatures in a weekly paper, tore the journal up
in a burst of righteous indignation, exclaiming : “ These
people have no respect for God ! ”
Florence “Ballara” returns to Europe by the P.
and O. steamer Moldavia. She will visit Germany
before again seeking a foothold in London. Miss
“Ballara” no longer hopes to take the world by
storm ; she will be thankful if she can take a bit of it.
The Ballarat lady is nicer than her voice, which did not
impress Australia very favorably. Also, she is nicer than
her stage name; people always think of her as “Miss
Bally. ”
“S.”; Ernest Leicester, of Edwin Geach’s “Human
Hearts ” company, doesn’t look an ancient, but he cannot
be so much of a chicken as to induce the curious to search
for fragments of the shell still clinging to his exterior.
Describing his long, honorable record to an Argus inter-
viewer, the other day, he went into figures. His longest
engagement, if I remember aright, was for 15 years ; his
shortest, if my memory isn’t obscured by drink, seems
to have been for five ; and apparently there were 20 of
them, unless tobacco and late hours, and a long
course of sordid dissipation, combined with un-
punctuality and cruelty to animals, have im-
paired my powers of calculation. Putting this
and that together, it would appear that as a boy
Ernest played with Methuselah, but has outlived his dis-
tinguished contemporary. In the same interview Mr. Lei-
cester felt called upon to enthuse over the melodrama, its
sweetness and light, and its freedom from those pernicious
problems which we findin the noxiousplays of a Pinero. The
average melo-mummer cannot praise the dreadful Sutton
Vane without declaring Pinero to be mere mud, just as
the candle-maker cannot advertise his tallow dip with-
out casting aspersions on the sun. But is the
melodrama the clean, wholesome thing it is alleged to
be ? On the contrary, is it not straining in a crude, bald,
vulgar way to raise beans from precisely the same dirt
that Pinero is cultivating artistically, craftily, and with
brains? The stupid people who rush to see “The Most
Horrible Female in Greece ” and revel in it, do not hesi-
tate to rebuke Ibsen, Sudermann and Pinero for their lack
of respect for the domestic virtues. And in doing so they
mistake inferior intellect for superior morality.
The age of the famous Hungarian violinist, Joseph
Joachim, just deceased, is generally given as 76, but if
some green-eyed fellow-professionals of his earlier days,
are to be believed he was at least three years older. He
started as an infant wonder. As a seven-year old, whose
embittered rivals said he was 10, he fiddled with great
success at Pesth ; and at 13, when infuriated competitors
said he was at least 16, “ the celebrated Hungarian boy,
Master Joachim,” was one of the instrumental attractions
of London’s Drury Lane Theatre. But however young or
elderlyjhe might have been when dragged from the nur-
sery, Jdachim put up in his longlife a solid musical record
that fmfpng whatever to do with the size he took in
years.^f'pi

wu y tf ! e record for emptying a house in fast time.

' Dux, from New Plymouth (M.L.): —Musgrove’s long
string of German sausages arrived here yesterday. Con-
trary to expectations, they played “Lohengrin” last
evening, as advertised. The audience was requested to
be there not later than 7.40 p.m., and the cow was con-
siderably hurried, so that its owner might be up to time.
After a wearisome wait, the rag went up at B.2o—only
40min. late. Three intervals of 20min. apiece damped the
enthusiasm of even the soulful ones, and when I left at a
quarter past midnight the show was still dragging along.
These Teutons have no consideration for the cow-man.
Several who saw the thing to the bitter end were sacked
for being late for “ bail-up ” this morning. In the early
hours of the morning, according to report, 15 of the co.
sat down to a supper of ham and eggs, bread and cheese,
corned beef, etc., and claret. A squabble arose over
something, and 13 of their number took the huff and
turned into bed supperless. The remaining two are said
to have scoffed everything in sight, barring- the plates
and the good hard table. It was a great feast.
Gertie’s first business experience was in wrestling with
onions at the pickle foundry. She was tender-hearted on
her father’s side, and wept over her work. She was one
of a vulgar crowd who went to and from their job with
their crowning glory tied up in rags. They said smart
things to respectable people who dared to look. Gertie’s
boots were of the heelless variety, and her costume was
not a la mode. In the evenings she frizzed her front hair,
put in the pads, and donned the war-paint. Gertie sang
coon melodies in a nasal tone at the Poseidon Select
Assembly (gents Is., ladies free). After that she
shook her limbs in an imitation of a dance.
The boys said she was a dead ring for Grade
Grahame. Pretty soon she took a prize in the East Col-
lingwood Competition. From that out her fate was
sealed. When the enterprising manager inserted a
shilling squib for “ Girls, smart, able dance,” Gertie ap-
plied at the stage door, and qualified with honors. She
ieft her responsible job without giving notice, and
thereby busted herself in commercial circles. The first
week she developed peroxide hair and a maiden’s blush
complexion. After a time she was promoted to the front
row, because she could kick higher than the manager’s
head and knock his hat into next week with her toe. She
afterwards became one of the special four, called “The
Wiry Girls,” in the big production. She displayed
more limb than all the rest of the show put to-
gether. The gilded youth reserved front stalls by the
row, Gertie high-kicked herself into favor, and went for
motor rides with the male division of the Upper. Ten.
She was considered just the thing, and carefully avoided
making grammatical errors. The time came when she
married a wealthy Anti-Socialist, and presided over a
regiment of menials. Sometimes she passes the pickle
emporium in her 16-h.p. motor, with the latest thing in
“ chuffers.” She always looks the other way when she
passes the pickle factory.
“Araemo” (from Melbourne): About the only shark
whom The Bulletin has so far failed to drop on with
both feet is the gentleman who runs the cheap suburban
variety-show. His company consists of a couple of “ has-
been ” comic singers, and seven or eight casuals. These
latter are generally attracted by an advertisement
announcing that a job as balladist, serio, or juvenile
song-and-dance artist, is going begging. In the case of
the juveniles, many A.N.A. “ champions” apply, and go
through a private performance in the presence of the
manager. He chooses a few of the best, and promises to
let them know when he requires them. When that
joyful time arrives, the budding “ star ” is usually pro-
vided with a new costume for the occasion by its proud
parents. These same roll up with all their other kids,
and also their friends, and pay for admittance to the
show. The engagement lasts for one night, and the
youngster gets 2s. 6d., or sometimes even as much as
ss. This is more than balanced by the admission-
money paid by parents and friends. The serio and
balladist are very often caught in the same way,
though not in such numbers. Some of them sing a
couple of songs in each part of the programme for. the
magnificent sum of ss. or 7s. 6d. An even worse shark is
the one who runs competitions—vocal and dancing—for
children. The prize in each division is generally a silver
medal, which costs about 4s. 6d.; the admission to the
show is 6d. The “management” is careful not to let
the competitors know for certain on what night they will
have to do their turns ; therefore they and their friends
have often to attend several times. There are several
preliminary contests, and then the finals. Thus, even
the winners only get an allegedly-silver medal for several
performances ; the others get nothing. The audience is
allowed to judge, so, of course, each kid persuades as
many friends as possible to attend. Why not pass a law
allowing the police to shoot all competition-promoters
on sight ? Or gazette them as thistles?
THE CURTAIN PALLS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Where is the tree
The lovers discovered
Where the bird sang
And the bee hovered ?
Fallen the leaves*
The tall trunk is sundered
Of old, through the limbs,
A lonely moon wondered.
More than the tree
Is dust and departed,
Gone on the wind
That sighs heavy-hearted.
Gone is the bird
Whose song was a measure
That made all the leaves
To tremble with pleasure.
Gone is the bee—
The rover brown-coated—
Who fed on the sweets
Of blossoms red-throated.
Hearken, OWind,.
Sighing scent-laden,
Gone is the youth,
And gone is the maiden.
N.S.W.

“K” : lam deadly tired of the superior
English person who conies out here and makes
remarks about the local twang. What about the
Cockney twang ? And the Cum-
berland dialect, which is scarcely
understandable ? And the York-
shire, which is not understand-
able at all ? And the Devonshire,
which is fearsome? And the
“ Zomerzet” ? And the Midland,
in which “ I will ” is “Oy
000000 l ” ? And the aspirate
trouble, which makes the Eng-
lishman say “ Hit hain’t the
’untin’ as ’urts a ’orse but the
’ammer, ’ammer, ’ammer of
the ’orse’s ’oofs on the ’ard
’igh road ” ? And the strange
inversion of verbs which makes
the belated Baptist choir-
singer demand, “ Did nobody '
never see nothin’ o’ never a
’ymn book nowhere ” ? I teach
the young idea, and I know
whereof I speak. Last batch
of immigrants gave me three
new pupils—one from Cumber-
land, a Yorkshire boy, and a
Cockney. Two I couldn’t under-
stand at all, the third made me
writhe every time he spoke. I, a
Maorilander, presumably more
or less afflicted with a twang,
have to teach these English
youngsters to speak English. The
Cockney I despair of ; the others
are beginning to speak almost in-
telligibly, after six months’ strug-
gle. But some of their barbari-
ties beat me still. Young Cum-
berland always pronounces eight
“ite,”. and when he says his
“ite times tairble” it is a treat.
One of our English educational
importations happened along, the
other day, in time to hear him
affirm that “ite elevens are ity-
ite.” Visitor murmured some-
thing about “colonial twang.” Then I
jumped at my ohance and explained at
length, with scorn for the barbarous
English dialects. And that Englishman
didn’t say a word.

Bulletin uiltil it received a letter of complaint
from an iriniateof the Parramatta (N.S.W.) insti-
tution. A brief extract
The other two meals are a slice of bread, no butter, not
even for the sightless men. . . . Two fowls that were
cooked weekly for the dying- are discontinued.
In earlier days, presumably, the blind man got
butter on his bread, and the dying man was
reminded once a week by a portion of fowl that
the grave was yawning for him—just as the man
condemned to be hanged can have anything he
likes for breakfast the morning of the execution,
so long as he doesn’t ask for a bit of some animal
that roams the African forest, so that the procur-
ing of it would delay the hanging. It is hard to
say which was more grimly cruel—the invidious
indulgence or its abolition. Cannot the State do
a little better than this for its old citizens ? If
the bit of fowl, being practically a death-warrant,
did more harm than good, why not establish a
sort of an alibi , and obscure the issue, and avoid
any harrowing differentiation, by giving occa-
sional fowl all round? Then the dying man
would get his chookey hen without seeing his
burial certificate tied to its leg.

Melbourne City Councillors, having been
decorated with small metal plates in order that
their unimportance may be discernible at a
glance, a movement is afoot in Victoria to provide
medals for Justices of the Peace. The proposed
bauble is to have ” J.P.” in bold letters on one
face, and the name and date of the owner on the
other. The Bulletin sees no objection. When
this country reaches the stage where knights,
J’s.P., dogs and aboriginal kings all wear their
metal plate, and the adulterator has a bill with
the record of his conviction pasted up outside his
shop, and the pious gentleman who draws rent
from the house of ill-fame is compelled to have
his own domicile painted bright red for a whole
year after the discovery of his iniquity, things
will begin to look up.

South Australia is only a very occasional
co-respondent—for years past it hasn’t averaged
more than about five and a half divorces a year,
while N. S. Wales can easily manage 300 ; and
when it does get a divorce case S. Australia acts
as though it were ashamed of being found out,
and hides the proceedings away in the middle of
the law reports, so that nobody but hardened
lawyers will be likely to find them. Consequently,
by reason of this lack of practice, the Adelaide
co-re. hasn’t the least idea of what is required of
him. The other day there were three of him in one
case, and all put in an appearance, because they
were told to by a piece of paper that never ex-
pected they would. When the first started
to go into details, the Judge stopped him and
told him that he really needn’t if he thought he
was giving himself away. But all he did was to
dive a bit deeper into the lady’s past and his own.
Then the second co-re. stepped up when his name
was called, and while the Court handed him “ th’
Book,” the Judgedecided to take time by the front
hair, and he confided to the gentleman tliatif hehad
anything to say that was the worse for himself be
needn’t proceed. By this time, however, he had
sworn to tell the whole truth, and he told it
elaborately. And so on to the third of the revela-
tions. There was no doubt about that decree

A story of domestic infelicity is going the
rounds of the Maoriland press. The husband, to
annoy his wife, insisted on turning the pictured
face of his ma-in-law to the wall, declaring it
made him bilious. The wife retaliated by taking
a tin whistle to bed with her, and blowing it
hard during the noisy watches of the night.
Then the husband applied to the Court for relief,
pleading that the tin whistle threatened to drive
him into a state of chronic insomnia. He wanted
the lady “bound over” or something, but she
was merely ordered to pay her own costs, and a
flabbergasted Court dismissed the case.

The N.S.W. Inebriates Act or Drunk
Law was passed in 1900, and it captured its first
inebriate on Monday of last week. So the delay
over the fixing of the Australian capital may
consider itself knocked out in one round.
* ■* « *
The repeated infliction of the fine of £lOO
whenever an undesirable Chow- escapes from his
ship into the Commonwealth is having its effect.
Last June, for instance, Wong Ah Fook removed
himself from the s.s. Harbart into Lilly Buck-
stleet, Melbourne, and dwelt there till the vessel
sailed without him. Before the Harbart left one
of the officers noticed Wong’s absence ; but,
instead of reporting the matter and getting his
skipper or his owners fined, he took his
Chow bo’sun aside and requested him to
discover Wong, or any old Chow who would
pass for Wong. The bo’sun acquired a yellow
alien somehow and somewhere, soothed him
while the boarding inspector tallied the undesir-
ables and certified them as correct, and carted
him away in the ship to foreign parts. A little
later Ton Neng vanished from the ship Wimble-
don, and his disappearance was duly reported.
Inspector Christie, skirmishing round Melbourne,
happened across a new-chum Chow, ran him in,
and tested him ; and, as he failed to pass the test,
Christie put him down as Ton Neng, and had him
remanded for deportation. But the Wimbledon’s
captain came along and said this new-chum Chow
wasn’t Ton Neng at all, whereupon the unknown
confessed that he was Wong Ah Fook, late of the
Harbart. Ton Neng is, therefore, still missing,
and an unknown Chinkie, who was alleged to be
Wong Ah Fook, but evidently wasn’t, is sailing the
briny and getting a steady reply of “No savee”
in answer to his question of “Whaffor ?” As
matters stand, the Wimbledon’s captain is £lOO
out; the Harbart is “in ” the amount of Wong’s
overdue wages, but, per contra , it probably has a
very angry Chow to keep in order ; and Ton Neng
—well, Heaven only knows what has happened to
Ton, or where he is, or, in fact, who he is. These
Chows are a most unsatisfactory herd.

In Perth (W.A.) the hard-drinking belt is
held by the drivers of butchers’ carts. In a recent
police-court case the Stipendiary asked a witness
if he had taken anything to drink before break*
fast on a certain day. “ Oh, not more than seven
or eight,” he promptly replied. “Eh, what?
What are you?” “I drive a butcher’s cart.”
“ How many do you get through in a day—drinks
I mean ? ” “ Some days about 30 or 40 —average
say about 35.” “ Call the next witness.” He was
also a driver of a butcher’s cart, but his average
was 60 drinks per day.
* * * *
“Dormant”: Ballarat, the erstwhile
golden city of Victoria, is in a bad way. The
shifting of the McKay Harvester Works gave it a
real Tommy Burns. Big landlords block settle-
ment round about, and the mines are dead, like
Blastus, the King’s chamberlain. Only one or
two factories are left —two, I think, as I heard a
man calling out on the corner of the street,
“Who’ll have the last two for a bob?” No
credit is given, unless a declaration is produced,
signed before a P.M. (they won’t stand J’s.P.),
stating there are to be paying guests in the house,
during the South-street competitions. These are
Ballarat’s only visible honest support now, and if
you haven’t them you’re a deadbeat, with your
hair sticking through the shattered crown of your
hat. The Besses o’ th’ Barn cancelled their
engagement at Ballarat on account of the poor
booking of seats. The English cricketers have
decided to avoid Ballarat like a bankrupt serpent.
Latelv an affable person came from Melbourne,
and rented one of the many empty houses for a few
weeks. He was not overburdened with furniture
and effects—just carried a portmanteau with a bit
of plaster-of-Paris in it, and a few other little
things necessary for the manufacture of the kind
of half-crown that doesn’t ring too well when
foolishly thrown on a counter. After six weeks’
work he started to get rid of his load of spuriqus
stuff in the city of statues; but after passing
three, and receiving 2s. 3d. change in each case, he
found that he had cornered allthesilver in Ballarat,
and he had to seek fresh fields. Being a firm
believer in local industry, he paid his rent with
locallv manufactured coin. The detectives
arrested the plaster-of-Paris after a violent
resistance.
THE MAN-MACHINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
As to the difficulty of handling heavy wheat bags,
farmers assert that not reduction in the size of the bags,
which would add to the cost of production, but the in-
creasing use of machinery ... must be relied upon to
save the wheat-lumpers from unnecessarily heavy and
exhausting work.— Argus, 29/7/’O7.
There’s sweat upon the brow of him who lumps the
bulging jute
With stony heart and loaded limb, truck-feeding sling
and chute ;
In rolling dust, red morn till eve, till noon again, there
comes
Dead, wearing weight for him to heave while yet the
donkey drums;
And, though jute-shysters track to town with good
black on their backs,
Though, clad in cords of easy brown —plain, town-cut,
lounging-slacks—
The acre-owner breakfasts with the Argus near his nose,
Up to his lumpin’ William Smith in bluey-wreckage goes.
The hairy-faced Conservative who grows the golden
grain
Smells robbery when he must give fair pleasure for their
pain
Who work for him ; with plot and plan he saves his silly
ways,
While brokers squeeze him—wild old man for whom the
country prays!
Down in the den of thieves who mesmerise him —mad
old man—
The jute-shark even savages his brother, when he can.
The lumper-lad, from him alone the road to wealth is
barred;
The upper and the nether stone, they grind him good
and hard.
And yet, he owns a fat, proud soul, who spoils these man-
machines.
He starves them with a niggling dole out of his miser-
means.
He sees no sin in freightage-fraud—when railroad-gods
rebate
It is his pleasant way to laud the too complaisant State ;
He whines for elevators, and wants State funds shovelled
out
That Smith may find a helping hand to hump big wheat
about;
He is a bold, mean, scalping card, intent to buy and sell
Tough Bill, whose toil is shameful hard—whose feet take
hold on Hell!

OUTFITTERS,
309-311 GEORGE STREET, SYDNEY.
At Canterbury (Sydney), a while ago, the
police interrupted a cock fight, and proceeded
against seven persons under an Act which says:—
Whoever encourages, aids, or assists in the fighting- or
baiting of any animal shall upon conviction before a jus-
tice be liable to a penalty not exceeding £5.
It looks plain enough, but the amazing magistrate
who heard the case dismissed the information on
the extraordinary ground that there was no evi-
dence that the place where the seven were arrested
had ever been used for cockfighting before. If this
decision was sound, it implied that, provided the
cockfighters moved the scene of their cruelties to a
fresh spot every time, no conviction could ever be
obtained, and that, on a quiet Sunday, rooster
struggles could be held with impunity on Circular
Quay. On appeal, a Judge upset the absurd
theory that almost any cruelty can be perpetrated
upon animals provided it is done on a fresh spot,
and sent the case back for trial. But where does
the haughty pigeon-assassin come in ? Surely
there is some clause to meet the case of that
shocking ruffian, and he is generally to be found
at the old blood-stained spot.
“ WITH A VIEW TO THE ABOVE"
A Melbourne street scene that suggests a new
form of matrimonial advt.
A recent letter from Mr. A. Jennings, of
Coffs Harbor (N.S.W.), to Sydney City Council
recalls an Australian pioneer of the “System”
days. The little ship Rover was built in N.S.W.,
about 70 years ago, and was used to convey con-
victs to the coast settlements. Sixty years ago
the Rover was wrecked in Broulee Bay, on the
N.S.W. North Coast, and passed out of history
till the other day, when an easterly gale gathered
up the battered ruin, and drove it close inshore,
People who boarded it found the timber? still
sound. A few crumbling muskets and a cannon
are all the ironmongery that survive. Mr. Jen-
nings is sending the cannon along to the City
Council as a birthstain, or something.

He was a stranger

and a Chow at that,

I smiled. The mis-

yet she wasn’t satisfied.
* * * *
“Happy is the nation whose people
marry young”—but, judging by the August
Divorce Sittings in Melbourne, when it does
marry young, the proportion of misfits is liable
to be pretty large. A couple, who started
married life at 21 and 18 respectively, found
six years sufficient to wreck their dream of
bliss, the lady in this case transferring her
affections to a Chow. Twenty-one and 17
were wedded five years ago, and last week
Justice A’Beckett gave them their liberty again.
Twenty-one and 20 also found life awry. Twenty-
two and 21 got their decree, 22 in this case having
left 21 at the church door “to go and make a
home for her,” and, after six years of vain effort,
concluded that “ the marriage was a mistake, and
she could do what she liked.” Twenty also asked
the Judge to release him from 21, whom he had
married only last September. He had left his
bride with her parents while he went up from
Queenscliff to Melbourne to furnish his house;
but 21 wearied of her grasswidowhood within a
week, and found a substitute for the hubby who
was buying the furniture. At the other
end of life’s calendar was 71, who sought freedom
from 69, his bride of 1862. It scarcely seems
worth the trouble at that age.

“ Flinderino ” : The Bulletin (8/9/’O7)
mentions that the coal-lighter, John W. Dare, 76
years of age, is the oldest hulk in Melbourne
waters. I know it’s rude to contradict, but
still- While fossicking the other day under
the Yarra wharves and jetties, and amongst the
barnacles in Rotten Row, I happened upon an
ancient craft that is old enough to be the Jack
Dare’s father. This interesting piece of marine
architecture is the Augustus, at one time a pilot
boat on the Hooghly. The Augustus is 93 years
old, having been built in Java in 1814. It is
lying partly submerged at the back of Coode
Island, in the now unused portion of the Yarra
known as Humbug Reach. Another old “has-
been ”in the Yarra is the Proteus. It was put
together 92 years ago at Java, and, in its present
dilapidated condition, would easily be mistaken
for a sister ship to Noah’s Ark.
* ■* * *
The melancholy tale of the bankrupt
pocket-handkerchief municipality of Camperdown
(Sydney) is still told at intervals. Camperdown
went broke as the result of a career of aldermanic
muddle and riot, and the bailiff was put in by the
creditors after the jamboree was over. He is still
in, collecting the miserable little bit of revenue on
the creditors’ account. The highways of Camper-
down are hardly cleaned at all now ; the removal
of house refuse takes its chance to a great extent;
and garbage collects horribly in the quieter streets.
Camnerdown, almost in the heart of the metro-
politan area, is going back to savagery, and is
likely soon to be the breeding ground of a
pestilence. A while ago the city of Sydney
offered to take it over, pay out the bailiff,
put the finances in order, and drastically clean
up. The Camperdown aldermen said the pro-
posed cleaning up was too drastic, and the
negotiation fell through on a question of excessive
or superfluous wash. Then Camperdown offered
itself as a willing but insanitary bride to the
adjacent municipality of Newtown; but Newtown,
so far, has not taken the unwholesome object to
its arms. It is becoming urgently necessary for
Parliament to take some measures with this
malodorous thing, which has died on Sydney’s
premises. The shortest way would be an Act of
Parliament annexing Camperdown to Sydney,
without any question of the feelings of the local
aldermen. The public health is of more account
than the feelings of aldermen —especially such
aldermen.

“Smiff” : Saw a fire-engine smashed up
in Collins-street, Melbourne. Horse killed;
hurt firemen sprawling on the ground ; crowd ;
crying; consternation. Damsel in excited pallor
grabbed me closely, squealing : “Oh, I saw it, I
saw it, right under my eyes! ” Liked her;
squeezed ; smiled with sympathy; 'sorry when she
let go. But she let. Passed on. Second damsel
(less beautiful) grabbed me (closer); said she also
saw it. Me still smiling. Damsel departed.
Wanted to know the time. Looked for watch.
Gone. Which girl ?
The case of a divided family is running
its course in Adelaide. Father said things about
son. Son sued him. Court called it slander, and
gave damages against the aged predecessor.
Now, the son, having waited a year and not got
his money, has his progenitor arrested. The
ancestor, being penniless, goes insolvent in forma
pauperis— and the son figures in Court as “oppos-
ing creditor.” That is as far as the animosity has
got at present.
* * * to
The present arguments oVer widening
George-street North and other Sydney streets
recall a superstition of the early days. “Make
streets narrow, run them north and south, or
where that is impossible, bend them here and
there,” were early surveying directions. The idea
was, that when houses were built on either side,
the streets would be protected from the sun.
Gipps, an early Governor, had the fad badly, and
was father of many narrow streets in Sydney and
Brisbane. He raised a historic quarrel at Ipswich
(Q.) over the matter, the issue being ultimately
carried against him. A wonderful faith in cold
and dirt and gloom had those early Governors—
bleak animals from England, who couldn’t realise
that in two erenerations the people of the new
land would become veritable Children of the Sun,
and be all the better for the change.
* * * *
Some time ago the directors of Sydney’s
Prince Alfred Hospital induced the Government
to build two new pavilions ; but when the build-
ings were finished the directors found out—if they
didn’t know it before—that they could neither
provide the 200 beds nor maintain the patients to
fill them. Consequently the Board now makes
the cheeky proposal that the Government shall
close the general wards of its own hospital at
Little Bay, and send the 200 patients treated there
to fill the empty new pavilions of the Alfred.
But the directors don’t propose to relieve the
State of the expense of those patients ; they want
£5O per bed per annum, which means an endow-
ment, or subsidy, or anything you like to
call it, of £lO,OOO a year to this hospital, nomi-
nally “supported by voluntary contributions,”
and managed by a Board elected by subscribers.
Meantime it is alleged that the Prince Alfred
hasn’t got proper accommodation for the nurses
and staff of the hospital even now, when the new
pavilions are not in use for patients ; in fact, it is
alleged that part of the new pavilions has been
seized upon as nurses’ temporary quarters, and
that, if 200 more patients come in, then the Board
will have to provide accommodation for nurses
outside the hospital. Presumably the end
would be that the State would be tapped for a
few thousands more to provide quarters for the
extra staff. “Prince Alfred ” rather prides itself
upon being an.Anti-Sosh institution. Its Board,
anyhow, is blotted all over with prominent Anti-
Soshers ; and they are keeping up the Anti-Sosh
reputation all right, by the vigorous manner in
which they hold out the hat of cadge to the State.

* * * *
The Melbourne management of the Com-
mercial Bank has struck terror to the souls of its
old employees. Recently some five responsible
position-holders—-managers of branches, accoun-
tants, etc. —received their conge for no visible
reason save that they were receiving salaries of
some importance. The unfortunate five had
grafted for 30 years, more or less, subsisting
during the broken boom period on humble pay,
and drawing comfort from the assurance that
when palmy days returned they would get due
consideration for having stuck to their guns.
Now the fellows who stuck are receiving the in-
glorious sack, their positions being filled by
inferior-salaried men, while boy labor at very
little per annum is being pushed in with rapidity.
A veiled reference to this “ retrenchment” policy
was made at the general meeting held a week or
so back, when
A shareholder drew attention to the fact that no men-
tion had been made of the status of the clerks. The
shareholders, he said, would not like to see any sweating.
The Chairman: That is entirely a departmental
question. Every half-year the salaries of the staff are
under review, and we believe that the staff is thoroughly
satisfied with what the directors decide.
Right through the staff of the Commercial there
is dissent, and the rumble thereof has been the
talk of banking circles. Meanwhile the dis-
charged employees are worried with family
responsibilities, and find it difficult to obtain
work owing to their ignorance of all business save
the octopus system, which they have spent their
lives in learning.

WILLIE-BOY TO GUSSIE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A local resident who obtained two suits of clothing
from England the other day of the total value of £lO ss.
had to pay £5 ss. Bd. in duty.— Goulburn Penny Post.
Oh. dash it all! This is a bit too rough,
Deah boy, we’ll have to rise and take a hand ;
We’ve let these bounders run things long enough,
The time has now arrived to make a stand.
*Twas right enough to let them have a show
On questions that have never troubled its,
Like this absurd Defence scare, doncherknow,
And all that silly White Australia fuss.
So when the} 7 hanged those English hatter coves,
And threw those shipwrecked niggers back to drown,
And packed those potters off to gaol in droves,
Because their features were a shade too brown,
We never tried to stop their foolish game
(These vulgar polling booths a chappie loathes) ;
But now, my patriotic soul aflame,
I’m going to fight to keep our English clothes.
This bounder Lyne, who runs the Federal show,
Talks large about our duty to the State ;
But where’s the fairness, I would like to know,
Of charging me for duty five-five-eight ?
Two lovely suits they are, direct from liantz,
Designed to strike these bounders dumb with awe,
Who have to wear those wild, colonial pants
These native tailors cut out with a saw.
So now, deah boy, I’m eager for the fray ;
I’ll die before I’ll wear colonial tweed.
I’m writing to the Telegraph to-da.v
To give us back that Freetrade johnny, Reid.
N.S.W.
