ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
Bulletin Office, Monday, 9.3op.m.—This
paper begs to report the receipt, this afternoon
and evening, of 16 letters to the effect that: (1)
The laughing jackass habitually lifts live snakes
in the air and drops them (once or oftener), and
eats them when they have been killed by much
dropping; (2) the laughing jackass can’t and
doesn’t and wouldn’t think of lifting snakes; (3)
the jackass only eats animals it has killed itself ;
(4) the jackass eats almost anything, alive or
dead, provided it isn’t too large. Before this final
rush of jackass correspondence came to
hand, isolated jackass letters had been
dropping in. The most interest-
ing of these were marked
“ Abo.” in the left hand
top-corner and sent across
to the printer. About
noon the boss printer—
who is a wise and judi-
cious man, and who
really edits the paper by
deciding what matter he
won’t set when he has
twice as much matter as
he can set in the time at
his disposal—represent-
ed that the amount of
kookaburra copy which
he had on hand was
really alarming. He was
quieted by an assurance
that an effort would be
made to keep down the
kookaburra copj' as m ueh
as possible. Then the
staff went out to lunch,
and came back to find
nine more letters re the
laughing jackass and the
snake, and the other
seven letters about the
laughing jackass and the
snake dribbled in during
the afternoon. They
are about equally divi-
ded ; half say the jackass
jifts the snake and half say
it doesn’t. Therefore, barring
the letters which have been
already sent to the printer, and which will
appear by instalments, unless the printer
thinks better of it, it seems advisable to
close the debate. The verdict is that the kooka-
burra mostly lives on carrion, and that the tomtit
is in the habit of lifting the python to the height
of half a mile, and dropping it on top of the
N.S.W. new syllabus (which is also a disputed
question), whereby the syllabus has been bashed
out of all shape and is now almost past recogni-
tion. The connection between these matters and
the number of gates that you have to open as you
drive from Broken Hill to the Queensland border
is a matter of uncertainty.

“Some little time ago,” says Mr. J. G.
Boyle, Kelmscott, W.A., “I was suffering from
8, severe cough, which threatened to take a serious
form. Hearing so much about Chamberlain’s
Cough Remedy I decided to give it a trial, and to
my great satisfaction was completely cured by the
time I had finished the first bottle.”*
“ Jimmie Pannikin ” : The Red Page man
makes rude remarks about the “ Abo” correspon-
dent. Let me say this —the genuine, old-estab-
lished Abo-contributor does not need a handy
reference library ; he has his book of notes made
on the spot, and the well-stored memory of a
trained observer. Writing of Le Souef’s observa-
tions, he says, triumphantly : “He knows, for
instance, exactly where the pelican builds its
nest.” Well, I’m not a trained naturalist, but I
also have that exact knowledge ; so have a small
crowd of Barrierites, who shoot east and west-
o’-Darling. In 1904 I visited a huge pelican
rookery near Windamingle, on the Anabranch.
The smell would just have fascinated the Stinking
Fish party. There is no great mystery to out-
backers about where the pelican builds its nest.
To my mind, a rarer nest is that of the bower
bird. During my three years of out-back exile
and experience I did not come across one bower.
I was once taken to see a left bower, not far
from Port Pooncarie (please do NOTomit “Port”),
but the nest had vanished. Will some Abo-T.T.
kindly send me a photo of a genuine bower bird’s
nest? I don’t want a studio-fake. P.S., also
N.B. : “ T.T.” stands for truth-teller.
* * * *
“ Smiff ” draws a picture of Mackay (Q.): —
Mackay is busy manufacturing evidence in favor of a
cheap-labor immigration policy; and we, who are at
present doing the toil, supply the raw material. In some
of the sugar centres —Bundaberg - , Proserpine, Lower
Burdekin, Mulgrave, and Mossman —harvesting is in full
swing, and the growers seem to be of one mind that labor
is both plentiful and reliable. The Mulgrave mill, after a
couple of weeks’ work, had the unique experience of
requesting white cutters to cease toil for a few days to
enable the mill to overtake the cane supply. Yet in this
nigger-loving district of Mackay labor is scarce, and the
scarcity of it will, I admit, seriously affect harvesting.
There will be a squeal in consequence ; but before any-
body takes the squeal seriously, he ought to find out why
labor is scarce here, when it isn’t scarce elsewhere.
The fact is that the position taken, up by the farmers
during the past few months has not only driven good
white workers out of the district, but has practically
prevented the influx of good sound labor, usual at this
period. For weeks past the local Farmers’ Association
has advertised broadcast its intention to only pay
£1 per week and found in the “ off ’’ season, and £1 ss.
during crushing ; and this when other districts have
been engaging labor for the crushing at from £1 10s. to
£1 17s. 6d., and even £2 per week, and found. Time
after time, for months past, the local Sugar Workers’
Union has endeavored to arrange a conference with
the Farmers’ Association to fix a scale of wages, but
the cookies delayed matters until the eve of operations,
and then their delegates attended with a scale drawn up
by themselves, and intimated that the workers’ delegates
must agree to if, as they (the cockies’delegates) had no
authority to vary it. And the cockies called it a con-
ference ! Of course, the “conference” was abortive.
How could it be otherwise? Have seen the scale sub-
mitted by the workers, and am of opinion that it erred
on the side of moderation, especially regarding contract
rates, which were in some cases 20 per cent, less than in
other districts. For a 15 to 18 ton crop the Mackay
workers asked for 4s. per ton ; Richmond River pays 3s.
to ss. ; Mossman, ss. ; and Mulgrave, ss. 6d.
I have spent over 20 years in the Queensland sugar
districts (am at present cane-cutting), and, speaking
generally, for narrow-minded stupidity the local cockies
take the whole aerated bread emporium. The prevailing
Mackay idea about sug-ar-cane growing is simply—Make a
hole in the ground, put in a cane plant (any sort; if
Jones has none, get some from Brown), stamp on it,
and call round in 15 months or so to cut it.
Lantana and other abominations have an undisturbed
time, and the farmer who doesn’t farm is surprised at his
crop failure, endeavors to throw the onus of hisignorance
or indolence on to the poor devils of white slaves, and
declares he cannot afford to pay decent wages. Multiply
this item by a few hundred, and a sort of idea can be
formed of cane cultivation in this district.
The existing conditions as to accommodation and food
are abominable. Most of the white workers have to camp
in filthy hovels. I worked for one cocky, grubbing, at a
pound a week and found, and had to provide my own tent
and utensils. At the same time four or five others were
camped in the stable. When I say in the stable, I mean
the stable. Horses had one half, men the other half, and
there was no dividing partition.
THE EDITOR'S TOPHET.
A probably apochryphal yarn from the
North :
A gem buyer with the usual foreign name, who did
business at Anakie (Q.), despatched per post some of the
stones as a present to a European President. The parcel
reached its destination in due course, but when the
secretary caught sight of the word “Anakie” on the
label he fled, and gave orders for the destruction of the
packet with great caution and much despatch. After-
wards he read the accompanying letter, and learned that
there was no material connection between the words
“ Anakie ” and “ Anarchy.”

“Snell” : I knew the Heegans and the
Hogans were deadly enemies, although I was only
a casual visitor at Speargrass. The Heegans and
the Hogans were neighboring selectors, and the
feud had sprung up like Jonah’s brolly in a night,
for one morning Hogan descended upon Heegan
with much bull-roaring, and incapacitated him for
two days with a wild whirling of arms and legs.
After that a Hogan never met a Heegan without
a similar manifestation of antipathy, and the
Hogan was mostly triumphant for he had the
weight and the venom, and thrice is he armed
who has his quarrel just. This much was told to
me, but I had the good fortune to witness the
denouement. Hogan was sinking a well. It was
down about 30ft. I happened to be overlooking
operations on the second day of my visit, when
Hogan’s boy, Daniel, went up to the house for a
drink. Heegan had, apparently, been waiting
this opportunity, for he made out of the saplings,
and ran to the mouth of the well. “ Hogan, yeh
dirthy crawler! ”he cried. Hogan looked up,
“ Heegan !” he yelled “ What ’re ye doin’ on me
land, ye thafe iv th’ wor-rld? Be me soul, I’ll
kick* the head off yeh—l will.” Hogan started
to climb up the rope, and Heegan grasped a half-
hundredweight of bluestone. “Iv yeh stir another
fut, Tim Hogan,” he said, “I’ll bat yer back in.”
He held the rock ready for a drop, and Hogan
promptly flopped into the mud and water at the
bottom of the well. He arose raving, and hurled
threats and profanity at his enemy. “I’m th’
paceful, neighborly man, Tim Hogan,” said
Heegan, “ ’n’ now I have yeh under me thumb,
it’s an explanation I mane t’ have. What d’yah
mane be ut, Hogan ? Ewhat have I done by yeh
that yeh turn on me ’n’ bate me wid every
chance ?” “ Yeh insulted me fam’ly ! ” screamed
Hogan. “I did not.” “Yeh did, yeh liar!”
“Nothin’ iv th’ kind.”. “’Twas yer mane,
crawlin’, cross-eyed viper iv a son did ut.” * l ’N*

* « * *
“ J.R.” : Giving evidence before N.S.W.
Timber Commission, the N.S.W. State Govern-
ment Architect made a statement which, though
true, will be news to a good many people :
The sandy beaches south of Sydney are similar to
country near the Bay of Biscay, where large tracts are
planted with pine and other woods which have grown to
perfection.
Wonderful how we gather up scraps of miscel-
laneous information. Another witness, a day or
two before, said the only real softwood in N.S.W.
is “hoop pine,” which grows in abundance all
along the coast in the poorest sandy soils. It
used to be called “Maryborough pine,” and was
sold under that name in quantities in Sydney.
The “similar country near the Bay of Biscay ” is
the stretch of coast-line between the mouths of
the Garonne and the Adour, which, less than 300
years ago, was very like the Bondi and Botany
sandhills, well known to Sydney people. It
was reclaimed by an “Intendant of Gascony,”
under the orders of the wise Colbert. Colbert
directed the planting of hardy pines to supply
timber (especially spars) and pitch for the French
navy, which in those days of wars with England
was always in danger of being cut off from the
Baltic, the usual source of supply. If there were
a man as wise as Colbert in power in N.S.W. it
wouldn’t be long before all the desolate sandhills
along the coast were planted with hoop-pine.
* * * *
“ Emubush ” writes :
I want to make a few remarks about an excrescence in
the new Cornstalk syllabus. My only child (a girl of
nine) is suffering from the effects of arresced sneeze
She attends a big suburban school, where the hideous
silence bell is an adoration —I mean an institution. A few
minutes before the last bell, which is the signal for falling
in line, the silence bell is rung. When it sounds, all
talking, all laughter, all movement, all signs of intelli-
gent and happy child life must cease immediately. Every-
body is expected to be dead standing up. The play-
ground looks as if the children had suddenly become
petrified. There are half-finished smiles, abruptly-ended
sentences, arrested sneezes, never-finished movements.
The unfortunate pupils stand still and mummified till the
last bell sounds. Three weeks ago my girl was just going
to sneeze when the silence bell rang. For ten seconds
she held out, turning purple and swelling to twice her
natural size. Then she sneezed internally, and she has
never been the same since. Moreover she has sustained
other injuries. The day after she sneezed inwardly she
was doing a high jump when the bell rang. She remained
up in the air, not daring to come down, and the strain
has told on her. I don't think much of this new syllabus,
whatever your correspondent, “Jimmie Pannikin” or
“ James Dish,” may say about it.
AN ALPHABETICAL AUSTRALIAN ANECDOTE—BY
THE BOUNDARY-RIDER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
A was an ant of the sojer sort
What sat on a sun-baked plain ;
B was the bite that he bit me with,
And O was a cry of pain.
D was the d—n that I d—d him with,
E was its echoing roll ;
F was the fear that I’d sat too ’ard
On the top of a red-’ot coal.
G was the grease-stained moleskin pants
Which I had to get up and rub,
And H my haste as I humped myself 1 «yk.
To the bar of the nearest pub.
I was the Inn where the snake-juice flows,
An’ yer drought-struck throat gets sluiced ;
J was a “ jag ” of the good old sort,
An’ the “jumps” that “ jag'” produced.
K was the kind of a time I ’ad ;
L was a large-sized ’ead ;
M was a mouth like a quick-lime pit,
Or a mug full of melted lead.
N was the nasty things I saw
As I lay on my back all day,
Which goggled their eyes as they grinned at me
In the reg’lar jim-jarn way.
O was an Owl of the 'ard-boiled kind,
Which I certainly' seemed to be ;
P was the pledge which I took before
- A remarkably fat J.P.
Q was the quilh which would not keep still
As I signed my name, dead slow ;
And R the respectable life I thought
I'd lead for a month or so.
S was the swag which I ’umped next day,
When the boss give me the sack ;
T was the terrible time I ’ad
With the station cooks out back.
U is the unkind way they ’as
With a cove when ’is luck is down ;
And V’s my vow to get square some day
When I meet them cooks in town.
W’s the well-cooked feeds you want
When the wallaby track you roam,
Which you never will get from a station cook
If y r ou tramp till the cows come home.
And now this alphabetical yarn
Must stop, for I may allege
That X Y Z are a bit too stiff
For a cove wot’s took the pledge.
N. S. W.

“ Benicia” : In Maoriland, where almost
every imported wild animal flourishes till it
develops into a curse, the acclimatisator has
come to be regarded as the local Stinking Fish.
On the ground that everything imported must be
better than anything native, he has brought in
the skylark, blackbird, thrush* finch, starling,
minah, linnet, sparrow, pheasant, quail, partridge,
rabbit, ferret, stoat, weasel, hawk, red deer
fallow deer, axis, Ceylon elk, hare, opossum,
bumble bee, trout, aide-de-camp, etc. Some oi
these have panned out well; some have been 10
different kinds of horror and disaster. They were
thrown into the country promiscuously, and the
results were various. The latest trouble is the
Tasmanian opossum. It has flourished like the
green bay tree, and is so earnestly trying its claws
on the young native trees that it has become the
most promising ringbarker in all Maoriland. A
perfect frenzy of ringbarking appears to have
seized upon it, and it is behaving as it never did
in its native land, and people begin to believe
that one ’possum can destroy a whole young
plantation. The Government, and especially
Lands Minister McNab, refuses to permit the
shooting of the ’possum in Maoriland, on the
ground that it does no serious damage in Aus-
tralia, which is just as satisfactory for the people
interested as the assurance that the rabbit isn’t
bad in Timbuctoo, and hasn’t undermined the
throne of Siam. Apparently it is the want of his
accustomed gum-leaves that has made the ’possum
turn into a bark fiend, and now Maoriland sees
nothing for it save to import 500,000 gum-trees to
ameliorate its ’possum—unless, of course, it takes
the other alternative and ringbarks Minister
McNab. Meanwhile, alarm has been excited by
a rumor that the Acclimatisation Society means
to import giraffes, which will look down cottage
chimneys while ladies are going to bed at night.
COULDN’T STAND IT.
REFORMED GENT : “ Friends, it’s a month to-day
since I gave up the drink, and I’ve saved
enough to buy a coffin; and if I keep off
drink another month I'll ’ave to use it.”
“ J.G. finds some more of the serfs of
the cow
Who says the Factories Act should not be applied to the
country districts of Victoria? I know a woman who car-
ries on a dairying business, or, rather, it is carried on for
her by three school-girls—two boarded-out ehddren, at no
remuneration a week, and a borrowed niece, at nothing
every seven days. One of these kids has to get up before
daylight, trudge a few miles, and bring home the cows. The
others have to get breakfast for themselves, the missus,
and the cow-girl. The missus’s breakfast is eaten in bed.
C -tn le M lip ’ ' he (f”w s
milked bv the girls (nine cows per girl), the separating
done and the separator cleaned ; and after that the three
slaves are supposed to get ready and go to school. The
missus stops in bed until she gets hungry, and has to get
up for a feed. Then she wishes that there were a few
more school holidays, so that she wouldn’t have to get
her own tucker so often. The miserable girls come home
from school to find cow again awaiting them. They have
their tea about 9 p.m., shamble through their home-
lessons, go to bed, sleep a few hours, and awake to more
cow. This world is full of cow.
A MATTER OF TIGHTNESS.
JIM : "Lor' blime, Bill, wot ever ’ave you been doin’ to yerself! ”
BILL : “ Well, yer see, cornin' back from the races, yer see, the girth wasn’t tight—an I was.”
Working on the Johnstone River, North
Queensland, right in the heart of the tropical
regions in which the black-labor party once
asserted white labor could not stand cane work, a
gang of 11 white men, in 21 working days, lately
loaded 278 trucks with 759 tons of cane. The
work included laying and shifting the portable
lines, and hauling the trucks out and in. The
actual cane handled was 3 tons 7 cwt. per man per
day, and the wage earned amounted to 11s. 5Jd. per
day, including the cook ; and there’s a 10 per
cent, bonus to come for those who conclude the
season. The silence of the large Queensland cane-
growers who said that white men couldn’t work in
cane, no matter what price was offered, is grow-
ing quite depressing in the face of such records

“ David H. Richards ” : Some time back
a heavy-weight squatter was taken ill, and on
I icing brought to Sydney for treatment was told
that his case was hopeless—
he couldn’t live more than
a month or two. To make
the best of matters he
arose, and went out on a
final burst ; and then,
being particular about his
wooden mackintosh, he
looked up a ghoul, and
was measured for a gor-
geous polished oak “cask-
I et,” with silk lining, etc.
The box was built and de-
livered to his hotel, and,
after much trouble with
the manager, it was
| smuggled upstairs, where
Mr. Jumbuck packed it
with his wife’s purchases.
He left for the homestead,
and his luggage caused

' man put his future resting-
place under the bed, and
used it to keep his clothes
in. But several months
elapsed, and he exhibited no
signs of an early demand for the
box, so he decided that better use could
be made of so expensive an article. He sug-
gested to his other half that it would hold her
dresses very nicely, but the lady disapproved of
the idea. So he stood it up on end, put in a
few shelves, fitted hinges on the lid, and made it
into a grog-cupboard.
* * * *
There was to be an inquest in the morning
at the Dingo Plotel, on the Dry River, near
Perishville, and the body which had blown out
its vital spark lay in the “parlor,” so the talk in
the bar was restrained and lugubrious. The
archaic human with a frayed swag said that the
sight of a corpse always upset him since a certain
incident, and after considerable drink he yielded
to popular pressure. It seemed that he was pad
ding it on the Western plains, making for Carinda,
when he came across a man lying beside the fence.
Closer inspection convinced him that the man
had given up the ghost. “I jist took a casual
look over him,” said the ancient padder, “but he
didn’t seem to have nothin’ o’ value, so I left him
as he was, an* come on and told the police. Next
day the traps brought the body in, an’ I’m blowed
if they hadn’t found thirty odd quid in his hip
pocket. Since then the sight of a corpse has
always made me miserable.” “My oath ! I should
think so,” said everyone.
“ Tilga ” : Cave-guides’ statements are not
likely to be upset by “Pluto” (B. 11/7/’O7).
Their calculations, allowing a margin for imagina-
tion, are based upon careful measurements. The
amount of carbonate of lime held in solution by a
drop of water in a cave is much less than that con-
tamed by a similar drop under a
station arch. Water percolating
through limestone takes up much
less carbonate of lime than
water passing through mortar.
Let “Pluto ’dissolve equal
quantities of limestone and
mortar in water from the
same tap and he’ll see.
In reality, 'city water
is a more powerful
solvent than cave
water. The greater
the proportion of
carbonic acid
gas in water,
the greater the
amount of car-
bonate of lime
it can hold in
solution. Pure
mountain air
and city air
contain re-
spectively
about 2 "5 and
3'5 parts per
10,000 of 0.02.
Rain thus car-
ries down much
more of the said
gas in the city
than in the cave
country, and the
action of the
city drainage upon
lime is correspond-
ingly greater. Again,
the cave drop hangs
in motionless air of a
very low temperature,
whereas the arch drop is sub-
ject to the action of wind and to
greater heat, both of which tend to make evapora-
tion, and consequently the deposit of carbonate
of lime, more rapid. If the formation of stalac-
tites in our caves were as rapid as “Pluto’’argues,
there vvould be no caves—they’d have become solid
stalactites before Adam went looking for accom-
modation.

“F.H.R.” : It seems pretty evident that
Mr. “ Cross Nibs ” (B. l/8/’O7) has never seen any
ringbarking done, or he’d never have listened to
his rabbity friend. They took a contract (no less)
for ten acres of ringbarking. Bear me, what an
enormous job ! Now, I’ve “rung” that much heavy
timber in two days, and, as the rate was eighteen
pence an acre, I wasn’t making a wharf-lumper’s
wages, at that. I might mention that eighteen
pence is a big price for ringing, and I have seen it
yone for as low as threepence per acre. I guess
“ Gross Nibs’ ” friend was well paid with the two
dollars, considering he hadn’t finished his colossal
contract of ten acres.
“ Flinderino ” : The fishermen on the
Victorian coast are petitioning to have an
open season proclaimed for seals. A few
years ago the number of seals on the
islands along the coast was estimated at about
200; now they are as plentiful as debts or stink-
wort. The fisherfolk declare that at some places
it is impossible to see the islands for seals. These
seals follow the fishing boats, and snap the barra-
coota off the lines as fast as the men hook them.
Where netting is being carried on, they tear the
nets and collar the fish. The toilers of the deep
say that unless something is done to reduce the
pest the fishing industry will cease to exist, as the
seals will scare all the fish from the coast; and
they assert that one of the gluttonous creatures is
capable of accounting for as many fish in one day
as would suffice an orphanage or lunatic asylum for
a month. The seals have been protected all the
year round in the past, but the Inspector of
Fisheries now suggests a close season of six months
only—from November to April.
» * * *
“Sr” endorses the new fire-kindling
method :
Last Sunday, as the old man was getting ready to go
for his Sunday morning walk, I started experimenting’
with a handful of sugar on the fire, to test the correct-
ness of an “ Abo.” theory, that sugar is better than kero -
sene. The test was thoroughly successful. I set the
chimney of our humble cottage on fire.
The whole family went hysterical. The old man rushed
into the kitchen and yelled, ‘‘Shut the blessed doors 1
Shut the doors ! You’ll have the whole house afire ! ”
He then climbed on the roof and called for a bucket of
water. I couldn’t reach the bucket to him, so I tried to
lift the bucket up on the end of a broom and nearly
drowned myself.
“ Send in for Mr. Smith ” was the Superintendent’s
next order.
Smith is fat and fussy, like the old man. Smith rushed
in like a mad fire-cart.
Smith was then hauled on to the roof to give the old
man a leg up, as he couldn’t reach the chimney top. In
trying to balance the old man and the bucket, Smith col-
lapsed, and then there was a scramble on the roof that
filled the old tom-cat with envy. He had fought a hun-
dred fights on that roof, but had never made one-tenth
the row and bellowing of Smith.
Then they got an old wet bag. The old man descended
and instructed Smith to throw the bag down the chimney
when he sang out “ All right.” He cautiously entered
the kitchen, and began looking up the chimney for the
psychological moment. The bag came down. Smith
hadn’t waited for “the office.”
The old man rushed out, an astonishing picture in
black and white, his face, shirt, and Sunday suit be-
grimed with soot, and the bag on his head.
He addressed Smith from the yard in language that
you would not dare to publish. Smith got so excited
that he lost his hold on the chimney, slithered down the
roof, and fell. The cat escaped by a miracle. It had
been looking up and admiring the show.
The rest of the day was taken up with inquiries as to
how it happened. My dignity, as the eldest daughter of
the family, was grievously dispersed. I spent three hours
in the witness-box giving false evidence, and by the time
I was discharged.my best boy had got tired of waiting at
the corner, and had gone home.
Sugar is a better combustible than I thought.

“ Hamer ” : Correspondents who seem to
admire the fighting qualities of the ant should
turn their attention to the domestic flea. I once
put two specimens in a bottle and the combat
*•* “ was as refreshing as.ay.hins that
that vessel had ever held—it was a whisky flask,
too. Directly they found that the glass yielded
no nourishment each seemed to decide that the-
other was its meat, and got to holts. It was such
a prolonged conflict that I reckoned I would have-
tea and come back for the last round, but when I
returned the fleas had vanished. Presumably
they had eaten one another.
A SUNDOWNER’S SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Come, mates, we’ll tramp the open track,
“Matilda” up, with swinging stride,
Though cookies curse and doom may crack,
Our knees are not yet old and slack.
Out where the skies are blue and wide
Bush birds cry “Spell, oh,” all the day,
Over the hills and far away.
Close streets we shun, and factory’s smoke
In towns where man but buys and sells ;
The foundry’s flare, the engine’s croak,
And sweated Labor’s heavy stroke
That beats to clang of clocks and bells.
But we work not by wage and shift ;
Like thistledown we drift and drifts
We love the air, we love the sun ;
We scent good tucker tracks, and tramp
The road that cuts through range and run,
To sneak at night, when work is done,
Hound shearing shed or mining camp.
From hand to mouth we live, nor wish
To toil with shears or digger’s dish.
We’re wastrels all; but whilst the trees
Shake glittering boughs, and gold’s the air,
When earth and sky breathe primal peace,
Honey we gather like wild bees, ■-
That buzz in sunshine here and there.
Unbroken Bush, unbroken Man
ltule, proud as when this world began.
Come, mates, ’tis time to tramp along
This lazy lane till doom my crack.
’Tis time to join the travellers’ throng,
Now through the Bush rings Freedom’s song,
Down ways that quit the beaten track,
Go twisting, shining, night and day,
Over the hills and far away.
VIC.
LOUIS ESSEX.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Another brother of “ Bill ” Lane, founder
of the New Australia foolishness, is back from S.
America. He went to Paraguay, too, but soon
left. Then he drifted on to Argentina, and
now comes back to tell the old tale that the man
who quits Australia for any other land is a fool
from Lis feet right up.
MR. BOOKER , who has just retired from the
position of Superintendent of Adelaide Fire
Brigade.
Frederick Oliver Trench, third baron—the
Irish peer who wasn’t blown up the other day,
but might have been —is a Galway man, born in
1868, and succeeded his grandfather in 1894. His
“seat” is at Woodlawn, County Galway, and he
has “ a place ”in the County of Tipperary. He is
cousin to Robert Le Poer Trench, one time Attor-
ney-General in the Berry (Vic.) Ministry. One of
the family, who was a land agent, wrote “Reali-
ties of Irish Life,” from the rack-renting land-
lords’ agent point of view. One motto sported by
the family reads, “ If God be for Trench, who is
against him?” Come to think of it, the same
might be said of Smith or Briggs.
There seems, after all, to be a dash of the
gay old Profit breed in the (once) unkissed Glad-
stone Dowie. Till the age of 30, this awful young
man poseed, from behind a thicket of what
was really whiskering, but looked like imitation
Astrakhan fur, as one who had never been em-
braced by any female. Then he married Miss
Hofer, Swiss heiress, and about the same time
Zion filed its schedule, and went out of busi-
ness as a paying religious concern. Since when,
Gladstone, according to the Yankee papers, has
held the billet of manager of a “summer hotel ”
—and is no longer unkissed. He is, in fact,
altogether too popular for his wife’s liking, and
the has packed up her things and returned to
Switzerland. It is a big moral drop from the posi-
tion of totally unkissed saint to that of over-
hugged summer publican.
For almost the whole of the last fifteen
years Frederick Illingworth has been a member
of the Westralian Legislative Assembly. For
nearly the whole of his membership he has drawn
extra salary as a Minister, Chairman of Com-
mittees, deputy Speaker, or something rosy and
remunerative. He has now resigned his seat for
West Perth, and wants Parliament to give him a
retiring allowance. Of course Premier Moore is
quite agreeable—his deficit is only a quarter of a
million already—and only one member has had
the courage to express disapproval. In the present
state of Westralian finances it is shockingly dis-
honest to give a big retiring allowance to a third-
rate politician—not in the same street with Dibbs,
Berry, and other men who did some real service to
their respective States. Illingworth was a wrecked
land-boomer, who left Victoria when the crash
came—his share of the crash came early—and
found a peaceful haven in Perth. He had just
been elected to the Victorian House of Landlords
when he departed, and for weeks he obstinately
neglected to resign. Illingworth was returned three
times for various places in the Murchison—
mostly the same place with the name changed at
each election—and in his early days he and Vosper
(now dead), De Hamel (likewise dead), and G. T.
Simpson (also dead) made things very unpleasant
in the House for a very angry Bigjohn. Illing-
worth is a permanent and incurable teetotaller,
and is head of the “Church of Christ” in West-
ralia.
Francis Adams, for nearly 25 years general
manager of the Australian Joint Stock Bank, has
just resigned. “He leaves it a wealthy man,”
the Sydney dailies say, in a slobbering notice.
Considering the awful smash of the A.J.S., and
the wretched progress it has made since, it would
probably be more to his credit if he had left it as
poor as are the shareholders, whose affairs he was
supposed to look after. They sank, however;
he swam. He saved himself ; he couldn’t save
others—or, at least, didn’t. In the way of smash
what this bank accomplished was something
colossal. Two brothers obtained, on five stations
in N. S. Wales and six stations in the N. Terri-
tory and Queensland, advances of no less than
£707,421; and when two brothers owe a bank the
size of the A.J.S. £707,421, the bank doesn’t boss
them—they boss the bank. To nine people the
awful concern lent £1,214,319 ; and a few years
ago, when the thing was reckoned up inside the
bank, it was computed that £611,569 of this was
dead loss. The amazing part of it all was, that
the man who was general manager of the concern
with this awful record used to turn up pretty
regularly at bankers’ meetings, and let them know
how banks should be run.
“ Melb.” writes :
Our esteemed Rev. Evelyn Snodgrass, of St. Mark’s,
Fitzroy, returns here with a new name, that of Douglas.
The Bible says (Revelation 3, 12): “ I will write upon him
my new name.” Yet the old one had been honored in
Victoria. Peter Snodgrass was a brave man, engaged in a
fight with the earliest Victorian forerunners of Ned Kelly
and co. He became a well-known M.L.A. Miss Snodgrass,
afterwards Janet Mrs. Olarke, was a relative of his. She
had been governess in the family of W. J. Olarke, and is
mother of Clive Clarke. I rather fancy that Evelyn’s
relation to Janet Missus had a little to do with
his transference from quiet Balwyn to Fitzroy.
However, he is now the Rev. Evelyn Douglas. They say
it costs about £7O to change your name in legal form, as
he has done. The historic case is that of Joshua Bugg, a
fashionable hotelkeeper, of Brighton, or one of those
places. He changed to Norfolk Howard The conse-
quence is that when incoming tenants fear there may be
a need for insecticide they inquire, “ Hum—ah ! Any
Norfolk Howards about?” Henry Irving legally did
away with Brodribb, so that H.B.'and Lawrence Irving
are not Brodribbs.
Died lately in Melbourne Hospital, Richard
Charles Luscombe, aged 77 years. He was an
architect, but not a successful one; also, he was
one of the earliest of N.S.W. Protectionists, and
was vigorous if not eloquent. He was once in
N.S.W. Assembly as member for Northumber-
land, having got in on a small minority
vote, against a host of candidates, at a
by-election. While in the House, Parkes, with
the airy wit for which he was famous, called him
“a withered tarantula.” Next general election
deposited Luscombe at the bottom of the poll
with 176 votes. Dibbs made him Inspector of
Scaffolding as a reward for political services.
There was much need for some such official, but
Dibbs didn’t realise the need, and regarded the
appointment as a humorous way of providing for
a supporter. So no Act was passed to give the
new inspector any authority, and the old man—he
always looked older than his years—was laughed
at by obscene contractors.
Old Shareholder writes :
So Francis Adams of the A.J.S. Bank has gone out of
the business. Thank God ! He, and Gyles Turner of the
Commercial of Australia, and Drury‘(deceased) of the
Queensland National, managed the three banks which,for
size of smash and long continuance of misfortune, put up
the record of the last two decades. True, the Bank of
South Australia is gone utterly, likewise the City
of Melbourne, Federal, Colonial of Maoriland, Mercantile
of Sydney, Mercantile of Melbourne, Bank of V.D.L.,
and most of the land banks. But the biggest of these was
at best a comparatively small affair. And the B.N.Z.
made almost a record smash, but then it has also made a-
record recovery. The Awful Three haven’t fully re-
covered, and they still live, but the living of the
A.J.S. Bank is the least ornamental of all. Drury
died recently, and his Q. N. Bank is getting into
better order. Gyles Turner retired in course of time,
and his bank also shows signs of improvement; it seems
to improve much better under his successor’s manage-
ment than under his own. (Gyles Turner, by the way, after
hisbank had made about the record smash and reconstruc-
tion in Australian history, had the insolence to heave
the brick of scorn at honest, well-meaning little Jimmy
Mirams and others, whose wrecks were a mere shadow
beside that of the Commercial.) And now Adams goes. His
great bank made a profit of £1450 last half-year. It doesn’t
judged by the profit figures, seem to recover worth a
cent. Fourteen years have passed since the date of the
Great Smash, and among the institutions which came
out crippled, and yet survived the wreck, the A.J.S.
presents about the most dispiriting balance-sheet. In
its gaudy days its one ambition seemed to be to be bigger
than the Commercial of Sydney, and it established
branches at incredible places like Boat Harbor, Guyra,
Lake Cudgellico, Menindie, and Oberon, and had 31
offices in Sydney and suburbs, and paid 15 per cent, divi-
dends, and put up much bloated and hollow gorgeous-
ness. Perhaps the A.J.S. Bank will recover under Mr,
Adams’ successor, as the Commercial of Australia and
Queensland National are doing under their new manage-
ment. I see that Mr. Adams retires a rich man. Well, if
he had put his money into the shares of his own bank he
might easily have retired a beggar. Apparently he
didn’t put his money into shares of his own bank, but
other people read the balance-sheets and did.

Hon. Daniel O’Connor, IC.C.S.G.
Ex-Postmaster-General,
N.S.W., Australia.
Another link with the days of the Great
Pro-Consul has passed over in the person of
“ Clutha ’’ Thomson. Mr. J. W. Thomson lived
to the ripe age of 78 in the wild, wet, windy
climate of Clinton, in Otago (M.L.). He replaced
“Rebel” Macandrew in the House of Reps., and
became Hori Grey's Minister for Lands. It was
he who wrecked the Stout-Vogel Ministry in
1884, and Governor Jervois sent for him to
form a Cabinet, but he. was too demo-
cratic for his .time, and could not get a
working majority. So Atkinson got a show,
only to be broken inside three days by a fresh
Stout-Vogel combination. To the best of
writer’s recollection, Thomson was defeated
only once, and that was when Tom
Mackenzie, a tremendously affluent Balclutha
storekeeper, allowed the whole country-side
to get into his debt, and then ran for Parlia-
ment. He was put in by cringing debtors, who
hoped Mack might wipe off the liability if they
worked conspicuously for him. But Mack didn't.
Next election Thomson was elected for Bruce,
and later on iregained his old seat, which he held
till the infirmities of age compelled him to retire
from political life. Personally, he was a fine old
gentleman, with an amazingly well-stored mind,
and a great affection for children.
J. H. Hinchcliffe, who had been dodging in
and out of the Australian “Inky Way’’for 40 years,
dodged out of it finally in Queensland a week or so
back. While employed on the railway at Man-
Chester he studied shorthand, being one of
Isaac Pitman’s early pupils. In 1858 he
became a newspaper reporter, and stuck to
that job for four years. Next he took a
hand at land surveying, and then moved to
Queensland (1863). He went “on the and ”
in the Logan district, and when he wasn’t
on the land he was surveying, and be-
tween the two he kept himself out of the
ink till 1865, when he joined the Bris-
bane Courier. After that he became a
cotton-buyer for three years, then he turned
cane-grower, and finally bent himself double
over the ink-pot again, first for the Darling
Downs Gazette, then as editor of the
newly-established Logan Witness, pub-
lished at Beenleigh. Later on he became
owner of it. After running it for 14
years, he closed it up, and soon after re-
tired to private life, and the study of
astrology, in Brisbane.
A Melb. newspaper par, last week, gravely
announced the death of a local Syrian person
(father of a Bourke-street hotelkeeper), at the age
of 101. Also, the grandfather of the deceased was
said to have achieved “ the exceedingly rare age
of 125 years.” If The Bulletin could accept
these figures as facts, it would welcome them as a
warning to white Australian girls. Never marry
a Syrian with the hope of being widowed. But
the statements re Mr. Abbotomy Michael, the
colored ancient in question, include the allegation
that he entered the Syrian army in 18G5 (when he
was 59), and “ led a strenuous and active life as a
soldier for 20 years’’ (till he was 79, that is).
Also, that “one of his earliest recollections was
the invasion of Syria by the Emperor Napoleon.”
It was in 1799, long before he was crowned
Emperor, that Napoleon Buonaparte invaded
Syria, and the late Mr. Abbotomy Michael, if he
died in 1907 at the age of 101, was obviously not
born till 1806. It is a wonder he didn’t remem-
ber Saladin.
The timely use of Chamberlain’s Cough
Remedy will prevent pneumonia.*
Varney Parkes, who, failing Carruthers’
nomination, is now splitting the Deform vote at
Canterbury (N.S.W.), was never quite pleased
with the conduct of any of his Parliamentary
leaders. He signed a requisition once asking
Reid to stand against father Parkes on the
ground that the old man had been at the head of
affairs long enough. At the same time Varney
had no doubt, he explained, but that Reid was a
lamentable clown. This candid-friend business
doesn’t make for popularity in politics.
Died recently, at Wellington (M.L.), aged
67, C. O. Montrose, at one time private secre-
tary to George Grey, the great Pro-Consul.
He served with the 40th Regiment in the Maori
war, and wrote several books on the campaign.
At one time he was sub-editor of the Maoriland
Herald, and subsequently editor of the Observer
and the Star (Auckland). He had the Crimean,
African and Maoriland war medals, and was
specially mentioned in despatches by General
Duncan Cameron, the commander who so notably
muddled the Brown Man’s war in Maoriland.

A limited company, to be known as
Archer Bros., Limited, was recently registered
at Rockhampton (Q.). The names of the direc-
tors, with their addresses, are Colin Archer,
Larvik (Norway); James George Lewis Archer,
Larvik (Norway) : Robert Stubbs Archer, Grace-
mere (Q.): David Archer, Croydon (Eiig.);
William Archer, King’s Langley (Eng.); George
Lindsay Archer, Sydney (N.S.W.); and Edward
Walker Archer, Targinnie (Q.). Evidently the
habit of living out of the country where one’s
money is made runs in families, like the habit of
never having any children.
UNBENDING BENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Premier Bent did not go near to the tomb of Napoleon
in Paris, because, he says, it is so constructed that every-
one approaching it must bow, and he “ refused to bow io
anything: in Paris.”
Most grateful news for Melbourne ears—
Their splendid Tommy Bent,
Whom every bad man hates and fears,
When on the Continent
Would never deign to bow his head
To any creature, live or dead,
But went through Paree’s wicked brood,
A monument of rectitude.
He found their comic opera blue,
And coldly looked away ;
Their music halls were risque too—
He simply wouldn’t stay.
To poor Napoleon ’neath the sod
He scornfully refused a nod.
Now Brighton people must bequeath
To Tom another Judkin wreath,
Inscribed : “To Bent, whom foreign splendor
Could not beguile into a bender ! ”

Besides the year’s profit shown above, £10,781 was spent
on renewal of tramways ; another waj' to treat that would
be to include it as profit and then write it off. Of that
£10,781 revenue was charged direct with £7314, and the
other £3467 came out of the renewal reserve. The co.’s
reserves now stand as follows
1905. 1906. 1907.
Capitalised profits.. ..£230,000 £230,000 £230,000

But, in addition to its own reserves, it provides a sinking-
fund for the Tramway Trust. That Trust, consisting of
the Melbourne and suburban municipalities, raised
£1,650,000 to build the lines, and when it had built them
it handed them over to this co. The co, pays the interest,
provides a sinking fund (now £45,000 a year), and is to
hand the undertaking back on June 30,1916. The profits
shown above are in addition to this interest (£74,250) and
sinking fund.
The company’s own capital was originally
£250,000 ; but subsequently £230,000 of undivided profits
were capitalised. The capital actually in the undertak-
ing is about £2,050,000, of which £1,650,000 is the Trust’s,
and £400,000 the co.'s. On this the return was :
Profit as shown above £137,661
Sinking fund for Trust 45,000
Trust debenture interest 74,250
Total £256,911
Dess Interest on co.'s investments .. 25,598
Net revenue of undertaking.. .. £231,313
That means that the public pays £lO ss. 7d. per cent, on
the capital invested. That is one of the privileges of
getting Private Enterprise to work what the public has
pledged its own credit to provide.
It was mentioned just; now that the co. has
about £400,000 in the business. Its whole possessions
can be grouped in this way :
June,’ol. June,’o6. June, 07.
Liquid assets .. £400,303 £876,443 £900,773
Freeholds .. .. 253,455 250,903 249,146
Other property .. 154,436 143,431 144,653
£808,194 £1,270,777 £1,294,572
Less debt 67,347 286,295 243,757
Surplus.. .. £740,847 £984,482 £1,050,815
To the total of £1,050,815 as at June 30 add £12,300 for
leaseholds, which probably run out before the under-
taking passes over; total £1,063,115, which is the amount
of the co.’s capital and reserves as shown above. The
“ other property” is mainly'ears, grips, etc. An interesting
luestion for the investor is: Will that and the freeholds
oring the price set against them in the balance-sheet?
They probably won’t intrinsically be worth so much ;
iut the investors who have been buying “Melbourne
Trams ” heavily the last 12 or 18 months argue that, as the
Trust won’t be able to electrify the lines in advance, it
will have to buy the co.’s stock to go on with, or permit
itself to be squeezed by the co. into some concession or
other. Five years ago this month the shares (960,000)
were worth 17s. They are now worth about 345. The co.
has nine years more to run. In the past six years it has
added, as shown above, £309,968 to its surplus of assets ;
if that goes on, it will have added another £464,952 by
June 30, 1916. Add that to the present surplus, and
there is £1,515,767, or £1 lls. 7d. per share—or 2s. sd. per
share less than the present price. This assumes, how-
ever (1) that the next nine years will be as profitable as
the past five ; and (2) that all the assets (equipment, etc.)
will realise 20s. in the £of present book value. And as
to the first supposition two things are worth considering
—that in the five years 1898-1902 the profit was only
£373,101 as against £582,146 in the past five years; and
that the sinking fund, which was only £33,000 till 1905,
is now £45,000. There is, however, an offset to the latter
increase of outgo : the return from the co.’s investments
is also increasing, without of course affecting the actual
tramway earnings. No doubt if £1 lls. 7d. can be re-
turned in 1916, and if the recent dividends can be main-
tained, the 345. can be justified: for at that price a
dividend of 24 per cent, on the actually paid capital is
equal to an investment return of a shade over 7 per cent.

The Commonwealth Insurance Co. (Mel-
bourne), which began with a £5OO deficit, representing a
goodwill paper asset:—
Bonus to Added
Policy- to Re- Re-
June, Profits. Dividends. holders, serves, serves.
1904.. £3683 8 p.c. = £ll2O £416 £2147 £1647
1905 .. 2182 6 „ „ 1177 360 645 2292
1906 .. 2048 6 „ „ 1186 415 447 2739
1907 .. 1529 6 „ „ 988 541 3280
The reserve fund apparently includes part of the provi-
sion to cover unexpired risks, and is not, therefore, all
true reserve, the premiums not yet having been fully
earned. It is true that the formal reserve fund (£2000)
is not this y’ear described as a reinsurance reserve; but
it is to all appearances the same reserve as was described
that way last year. The co. began very badly indeed,
paying an excessive dividend ; now it has got down nearer
the right thing, though it is very doubtful whether the
lay-by is even yet sufficient, in view of the microscopic
reserves. Still, in proportion to premiums (and therefore
presumably risks), the capital is large; so that the
policy-holders are as well protected as in most offices.
The net premiums were £10,071; capital and reserves,
after paying dividend, are £23,044, or 229 per cent, of the
premiums, and that is well above the average. The his-
tory of the co. is further set out here :
Net Ex- Loss Expense
Premiums. Losses, penses. Rate. Rate.
1904 ..£8,813 £3758 £1905 42-6 p.c. 21‘6 p.c.
1905 .. 7,590 4207 1944 55-4 „ 25-6 „
1906 .. 8,359 4712 2376 S6‘3 „ 28'4 „
1907 .. 10,071 6649 2654 66'0 „ 25’3 „
The lossrate is very heavy; the co., it is understood,
devotes its attention mostly to marine insurance, and it
has been a very bad time for marine underwriters lately,
both Australian and English. The expense rate is
moderate (marine insurance is in that respect, anyway,
less costly than the pursuit of the 10s. fire premium), and
the securities are of a first-class character.

The Queensland Woollen Manufacturing
Co. (Ipswich, Q.): —
Dividends and Written To Re- Re-
June, Profits. Dividend Tax- off. serves, serves.
1898 .. £3169 Nil £3169 £10,212
1899 .. 3883 Nil 3883 14,096
1900 .. 4168 Nil £12,000 6,263
1901.. 4151 2£ p.c. = £1225 2926 9,189
1902 .. 2140 Nil 5,000 6,329
Loss
1903 .. 1386 Nil 4,943
1904 .. 2880 Nil 2,063

1905 .. 2795 Nil 7,175 7,907
1906 .. 446 Nil 5,733 14,086
Assets written down and losses written off £5546 19,632
£23,339 (one-half the capital) written off, leaving- 3,707
Loss
1907 .. 411 Nil 3,296
So that is what the reduction of the old Queensland
State duties has ended in, so far as this co. is concerned —
the loss of one half the capital; while the other half
isn’t able to earn anything, and, unless a better tariff is
adopted, that half is as good as lost too. Under the
State tariff the co. had done fairly well ; though certainly
during the drought and depression it did not feel justified
in paying any dividends. But it put up with
Queensland industries mostly had to. In the past few
years of great prosperity it ought, however, to have
shown substantial profits to make up in part for those
lean years ; instead, thanks to G. Reid and Cook and the
Freetrade misrepresentatives of Queensland, it made very
heavy losses. In the hope that Australia would have
more sense when it saw the effect of its tariff-
lowering, this co. has kept its mill open and dis-
tributed about £12,000 a year in wages, salaries, etc.,
and put up with an annual loss of about £2500. If it
could largely increase its output—in other words, if a
market were for a while secured for it—it could, the
directors say, work at a very great advantage compared
with what it can do now ; but to do that requires more
capital; and unless it gets a tariff vastly different from
what it has had, who is likely to be fool enough to put
more capital into a concern which in a few years has lost
half of what it had ? If it could get that tariff, and could
then get the extra capital it needs (and it would then
have a fair chance of getting it), though the cost might
go up for a while, the promised advantages to be derived
from working on a larger and freer scale are so
great that prices would very soon come down
again. One current opinion is that it is absurd to
put a high duty on tweeds, blankets, and other
woollens because the Australian works now are
going “ all they know,” and can’t cope with a bigger de-
mand. The fact, is that the works Want to expand, and
under the old tariff can’t, because they can’t get the
money ; so they potter along in a small, cramped way,
buying from hand to mouth, paying through the nose for
everything, yet, in spite of all, turning out goods that
they can well be proud of. It is quite true that for a few
months they may not be able to cope with all the de-
mand ; but secure them the market and they soon will.
If that isn’t done, then this co. and many more may as
well give the thing best. That will mean sacking all
hands and losing the rest of the capital; but without a
high tariff the time won’t be long when all hands will
have to be sacked, ar.d all the rest of the capital will be
definitely lost, anyway.

For many years this co. paid 10 per cent, dividend, but it
is pretty obvious that it did it at the expense of capital.
Now, though 10 per cent, is again paid, 4 per cent, of it
is described as bonus, which is probably the directors’
way of hinting that they are in no way committed to
more than 6 per cent, as a general thing. The progress
of the co. recently is further illustrated by this :
Liquid
Liabilities. Assets. Surplus.
June, 1905 .. £25,070 £40,194 £15,124
Dec., 1905 .. 22,263 43-,557 21,294
June, 1906 .. 19,206 46,816 27,610
Dec., 1906 .. 18,866 51,665 32,799
June, 1907 .. 22,898 55,099 32,201
After providing for the half-year’s dividend, there re-
mained a surplus on Dec., 1906, of £29,130 ; so that during,
the past half-year the position, so far as liquid assets is
concerned, has improved by £3071; the other £4475, to
make up the total profit of £7546, has gone into additions
to works. Apart from current expenditure on renewals,
nothing appears, therefore, to have been provided for
depreciation except the sums added to reserves ; and
that being so, the reserves are not true reserves, and the
profits shown above are not true profits, unless
the patched and renewed plant was worth as
much at the end of the half-year as it was
at the beginning. The directors propose to add £2OOO to
depreciation fund out of the profits ; but whether that
represents their estimate of the half-year’s depreciation
they do not say. If it does, then, of course, the true
profits come down to £5546. Besides the depreciation
fund (now £15,141) there is a renewal fund (£8864) ; pre-
sumably that is also a provision for depreciation. Under
these circumstances, it has to be remembered that, pro-
fitable though the business is, the balance-sheet figures
are inflated, and that, later on, there will have to be
another re-valuation, and such another writing-off (of
reserves, that is—not necessarily again of capital) as there
was three years ago.

The Denton Mills Hat Factory Co. (Mel-
bourne)
Writ-

1898 .. £3628 10 p.c.= £2330 £1298 £2674

New share premiums 31 3416
1900 .. 3114 10 „ „ 2450 664 4080

1902 .. 2955 10 „ „ 2450 505 4992
1903 .. 3268 10 „ „ 2584 £1045 4631
1904 .. 3117 10 „ „ 2990 127 4758
1905 .. 3208 10 ~ „ 3000 208 4966
1906 .. 3363 10 „ „ 3000 363 5329
1907 .. 3684 10 „ „ 3000 684 6013
This is one of the few Australian industrial concerns that
show consistently good results. It got a good start
under the old Victorian State tariff. Moreover, the Vic-
torian wholesale softgoods man, though he had started
as an importer, soon recognised that Protection had come
to stay; wherefore, instead of importing hats from
France and Belgium and other foreign parts, he accepted
the inevitable, and started importing them from Colling-
wood. In fact, he turned partly into a manufacturer
himself; and that is how this co. comes to have had a
career almost unique among Australian manufacturing
enterprises. Also that is why the ledger debts are stated
gross, “no provision for bad or doubtful debts being
necessary ” —which has also contributed to the success.
The profit is shown above as “ net.” That means that
something is written off for wear and tear of machinery,
etc.—£sso, or about 12J p.c. last year. But the freeholds
and factory buildings remain in the books at the 1903
valuation.
Castlem.aine Woollen Co. (Vic.)

This leaves the net profit for the year £1292, which is a
bare 4 per cent, on capital and reserves combined ; and
the slight improvement, which is apparently due to the
prosperous times and the larger buying power of the
people, has been seized upon to put up the dividend
again to a very moderate 4 per cent., in spite of the fact
that for the previous two years, when only 3 per cent,
was paid, nothing was apparently provided out of profits
to cover wear and tear of plant. Also, in order to in-
crease the profit, shareholders have had to take on some
further liabilities, with their attendant risks ; there are
debentures now for £4500 as against £2900 twelve
months ago ; though, certainly other debts have been re-
duced from £7464 to £7174. This little co. is paying
away now about £11,500 a year in wages, freights and
incidental expenses, and about an equal amount for
wool ; and a shade more Protection will give it the com-
mand of a market big enough to make even those ser-
viceable figures look very small.

The improvement is probably largely due to a smaller
provision for bad debts. In the Jan.-June half of 1906
“salaries, expenses, bad debts, etc.,” ran to £6164; the
gross profit (working account) on sales was £12,563. Now
the gross profit on sales is only £11,938 ; but the lumped
expenses are down to £s624—and, as remarked just now,
that severe shrinkage is probably due to smaller provision
for bad debts. Also “Repairs to houses” has been a
smaller item. Therefore, much importance cannot be
attached to the improvement in the profits as disclosed ;
the business, in fact, shows no actual improvement.
Whether anything is being written off freeholds is uncer-
tain ; there certainly has been a slight shrinkage in their
book value:—

Twelve months ago freeholds stood at £186,946 ; but there
have been sales of land since then—the co. has a fine
estate, which it announced some time ago it would
gradually dispose of. The debts are heavy ; therefore,
the payment of a dividend which absorbs almost the
entire profits is a very risky policy. If nothing is written
off properties beyond the small amount shown, the policy
is especially risky.

Standard Mutual Building Society (Mel-
bourne), which had a cleaning-up in 1900, and wrote
off £49,600, being one-half its capital :
Profit on

Profits. Sold. Dividends. Off. serve.

This society faced the situation boldly, and wrote down
properties so resolutely that mostly a surplus is shown
over and above the revised book value. Its present
reserves consist of a formal reserve fund, of £12,000, un-
divided profit £1521, and a contingency fund (£5163) set
aside to cover possible losses on properties in possession
or sold on terms. It is out of this fund that the amounts
shown above as written off have come. The extent of
the business, and to some extent the need for this re-
serve, are shown by these figures (the first column refers
to the period immediately following the cleaning-up) :
Jan.,’ol. July,’o6. Jan.,’o7. July,’o7.
Deposits, etc. .. £37,015 £24,422 £26,579 £27,751
Advances .. .. 19,998 31,761 36,796 39,263
Foreclosures .. 72,305 58,597 56,351 54,883
Cash, etc 2,778 1,013 1,297 1,840
Though the deposits and other debts have grown a little
in the past 12 months they are still very moderate com-
pared with capital and reserves. Good progress has been
made in clearing out the foreclosed properties ; and what
are left are giving a net return of very nearly 6 per cent.,
so that evidently it is not the unprofitable sediment that
is left in the society’s hands. Altogether the Standard
looks to be nowadays a very prudently managed concern.

The North Shore Gas Co. (Sydney)
To Re- Re-
Profits. Dividends. serves, serves.
Dec., 1901 £3776 7 per cent. =£3719 £57 £5,522
June, 1902 3742 7 ~ „ 3790 5,474
Dec., 1902 6453 7 „ „ 3937 2516 7,990
June, 1903 4724 7 „ „ 4153 571 8,561
Dec., 1903 6495 8 „ „ 4997 1498 10,059
June, 1904 5366 7 „ „ 4408 958 11,017
Dec., 1904 6782 7 „ „ 4768 2014 13,031
June, 1905 5484 7 „ „ 4812 672 13,703
Dec., 1905 7229 7J „ „ 5270 1959 15,662
June, 1906 7474 7£ „ „ 5465 2009 17,671
Dec., 1906 8014 7£ „ „ 5622 2392 20,063
June, 1907 8997 8 „ „ 6000 2997 23,060
This is one of the Gas cos. which give shareholders and
the public just as little information as they can in their
published accounts. Only N. S. Wales Gas cos., as a
rule, adopt this practice ; you may not know whether
the Victorian and Queensland cos. are providing enough
out of revenue to cover wear and tear and pay for
renewals ; but, at a,ny rate, you know how much they are
providing. Practically all the N. S. Wales Gas cos. are,
however, tarred out of the same pot; they may be making
a great deal more than they say they are, and “ socking”
away in works the profit that they don’t show ; or they
may be showing practically all the surplus of revenue over
working expenses as profit, and providing nothing to re-
place the plant, etc., that is gradually wearing out. This
co.’s alleged profit and loss account consists of the one
solitary item —“Net profit on sales of gas, etc. (after
allowing for reserve and depreciation), £5997.” The
amount put to reserve is £3OOO : what other amount is
put aside to cover depreciation there is no way of finding
out. Whatever it is, some of it has no doubt been
spent on extensions of mains, etc. Six months ago
this item stood at £184,535 ; it now stands at
£184,195. That looks as though £340 has been written
off. But meantime if £5OO has been spent on extensions,
it would mean (supposing there have been no sales of
mains and plant) that £B4O has been written off out of
Erofits. Six months ago the co.’s debts (including that
alf-year’s dividend) totalled £35,387; they now total
£26,607, so that debts have been reduced by £B7BO in
six months. The assets apart from land, mains, and
machinery were six months ago £26,398; they are now
£28,068, a growth of £1670. Add the- £B7BO and £1670
together, and we get the improvement in what may be
called the cash position in the six months—£lo,4so. Of
that £l2 is capital got in. Thus £10,438 plus anything
spent on extensions, and paid for out of revenue, is the
total excess of revenue over working expenses. The
alleged net profit of £5997 may therefore be taken with
a grain of salt—or a spoonful, if a grain isn’t enough to
swallow the yarn with.

“plant and buildings of every description in action”
are set down at c05t—£32,419. On the other
side is a reserve for depreciation of plant,
£6458. That is practically, therefore, a reserve
for extraordinary renewals : it is a set-off against the
wear and tear which isn’t yet quite bad enough wear and
tear to call for the workman’s hammer or the scrap-heap.
It is, therefore, by no means the best sort of reserve ; it
isn’t something the co. owns above and beyond its capital
—it is rather that part of the capital that is represented
in the works by rust and worms. The new issue of
capital has improved the cash position. Before that it
was weak, because, for one thing, all the reserves are sunk
in plant, etc. At June 30 there were cash balances
amounting to £3044 ; the half-j-ear’s dividend had to
come out of that. And as the co. is extending its works,
the balance will probably soon be absorbed. In spite of
the extension, profits are not what they were in the pre-
vious year ; and, as reserves have to be drawn on to pay
the dividend, it looks as though a mistake were made in
putting it up to 7 per cent.

while business keeps up, the dividend isn’t in much
jeopardy. The accounts are presented in a very clear
and complete manner.

This is the co. that went on paying big di vs. for years and
forgot to lay by any revenue to provide for plant re-
newals, until the electric light part of the undertaking
collapsed. Then the ordinary share div. had to be cut
down to 1J per cent. (2| per cent, paid every other half-
year), the surplus being used to write off plant. The
electric-light plant, mains and fittings are still in the books
for £9264 ; yet that part of the business resulted in a
loss last half-year of £lB. The surplus profits, written
off, are being' applied to wiping out a debenture debt,
which has been reduced from £25,000 at the end of 1903
to £17,000 now. The co. has still a good deal of worn-out
and wrongly-distributed capital to replace; but it is
making very fair progress.

The profit is shown, it is said, “ after providing for de-
preciation and a proportion of the cost of the renewal of
mains and services.” The mains, services, buildings,
etc., were down at £11,864 six months ago. They
are down for the same £11,864 now. Something
may, of course, have been written off ; for extensions;
(as distinguished from renewals) may have been
paid for out of revenue, and that would be equivalent to'
writing-down the balance. But the balance-sheet shows-
as an asset “new mains account, £318” ; and that and a
vague remark in the report suggest that not even re-
newals have been wholly paid for out of revenue. If
that is so, the position, put plainly, is like this : the co.
has worn out its mains (that is, it has used up its capi-
tal), and now it is charging renewals to capital also.-


MINE MANAGER.
APPLICATIONS will be received by the undersigned
at 375 Collins Street, Melbourne, up to 5 p.m. on
Friday, 23rd August, 1907, from persons competent to fill
the position of MINE MANAGER of THE DUCHESS MINE,
near Clonpurry, North Queensland. Applicants must
submit testimonials, and state age, experience and salary
required. A liberal salary to a competent man.
By Order of the Board,
EDWARD H. SHAOKELL, Secretary,
The Hampden Cloncurry Copper Mines, Limited.
If these new mains are renewals, the only honest thing to
do is to write off reserves to that extent. Also, if other
mains and services have depreciated, though they haven’t
yet depreciated enough to require renewal, it would be
an honest thing to write off a further amount equal to
the depreciation. If there are not enough reserves to
provide for that depreciation, then it will be obvious that
some of the capital has gone; which means that it has
been paid away in dividends. The chances are that a
good many Australian gas and electric-light companies
are in that position, and it is only a question of time
when the unpleasant fact will have to be faced. In Queens-
land the Rockhampton co. is in that street already.
Shareholders in these cos. have been content^ with the
most absurdly inadequate particulars concerning their
own property ; and if they fall in as a consequence, they
will only have themselves to blame.
• V * •
“0.K.”: It is a fact that the Union Bank in
Sydney, some three months ago, received some scrip
from Munroe and Munroe, of New York, for distribution
among the local purchasers of shares in the Marconi
Wireless Telegraph Co. of Canada. The few people whose
names appeared on the scrip have duly collected it, no
doubt; and now the other persons, who placed their cash
and confidence with Horace G. Robinson, would like to
know what method of allotment was adopted by his
principals, Munroe and Munroe. Many thousands of
shares were subscribed for in Melbourne ere Robinson
moved along to Sydney; yet the Union Bank in Mel-
bourne can give ho satisfaction to local enquirers. How
comes it that the vouchers of a few Sydney investors
have been exchanged for scrip, whilst the earlier inves-
tors are left unsatisfied ?
