In addition £703 net has been added to the fire and acci-
dent insurance fund, which now amounts to £6066 and is
not included in the reserves set out above ; also £1145
spent on the new shaft has been treated as ordinary ex-
penditure. (The co. charges it to capital account, and
then writes the amount off.) The dividends for the half-
year were at the rate of 99 per cent, per annum on paid
capital (£161,576). As a former honest old S. Australian
Leg. Councillor once said, when he came to his peroration
on a new bill—“ That lily don’t want no paint.”
The past, however, is only of much practical
value so far as it is a guide to the future ; and what about
that? Lead has averaged £l9 17s. Id. during the half-
year, the directors report. The lead in the concentrates
totalled 11,118 tons. Silver averaged 2s. 9 9-16 d. ; there
were 276,322 oz. of that metal in the concentrates. Of the
profit £9583 came from payments made by the Zinc
Corporation on account of old tailings. Deduct that first,
and £93,798 remains. Then we can get these variations,
assuming metals to average the prices mentioned here-
under :
Lead. Silver. Profit. Per Share.
£l9 17 1 .. 2-9 9-16 .. £93,798 .. £0 9 4
17 17 1 .. 2-9 9-16 .. 71,562 .. 0 7 2
15 0 0 .. 2-9 9-16 .. 39,830 .. 0 4 0
15 0 0 .. 2-6 .. 35,765 .. 0 3 6
13 0 0 .. 2-6 .. 13,529 .. 0 1 4
That is the profit per half-year without deductions for
wear and tear, but on the other side without allowances
for zinc material, which mayor may not be valuable in
the future. Also, of course, it is on the basis of the past
half-year’s production at the past half-year’s costs,
which were a little higher than usual on account
of heavy development expenditure. What the metal
markets are going to be in the future nobody
knows. But in the past 10 years lead in only five years
averaged more than the £l3 quoted above as a minimum,
and in the 10 years before that it only averaged £l3 or
upwards in three years. And the average for silver for
many years past has been slightly under 2s. 6d.
The B. H. South’s ore reserves are now esti-
mated at 3,000,000 tons ; but the figures are probably
only a rough approximation. The past half-year’s ex-
traction was 101,385 tons; so, at that rate, there is
nearly 15 years’ work in the mine down to the 970 ft. level;
and at the 970 ft. level the ore is proved for a length of
1000 ft., and the test crosscuts give it an average width
of 117 ft., and an assay value of 13 per cent, lead, 7£oz.
silver and 11-8 per ceiit. zinc. The average value of the
ore mined during the past half-year was 15 - 2 per cent.
lead, 5 - 2 oz. silver, and 12T per cent, zinc ; practically all
this came from above 825 ft. The ore at the deep level is
therefore poorer in lead but richer in silver. A mine with
15 years’ore above 970 ft., and with a lode 1000 ft. long
and 117 ft. wide going underfoot at that level, has no need
to worry about ore supply so far as quantity goes. But
the metal markets make all the difference.
» » * *
British Broken Hill expects its Thompson shaft
workings to hatch something worth sending to market,
and the chances so far are favorable. The mystery of the
ore cut in sinking the shaft below the 300 ft. level has not
yet been cleared up, for the water came in and the men
went out, and the guessing competition as to whether
the ore is an isolated bunch, a small seam, or the top of
a big shoot still goes on. Marsh’s 600 ft. north drive, which
was to have cut the lode coming down from the 500 ft., has
been hung up for over a week owing to the inadequacy
of the winding engine, which led to the waste accumu-
mulating in the drive. This drive is now in about 200 ft.,
and no ore, except a small seam (indignantly declared to
have no connection with the sought-for lode), has yet
been cut.
• *• * *
The North Broken Hill is trying to pick up a
continuation of its 800 ft. ore body, dipping north.
Present course of the lode would carry it to the east of the
Victoria shaft, where there are hopes that it will be
found some distance below the 800 ft. The North is look-
ing very well underground just now.
* * ¥ *
The new granulation plant erected by the Sul-
phide Corporation at the Central mine, Broken Hill, for
the treatment of the slimes purchased from Block 10, is
not working well. Possibly readjustments may lead to
better results.
**- * *
The De Bavay zinc-extraction plant on the
North Broken Hill dump has been closed down to allow
of some material alterations. This appears to be the fate
of all the zinc extraction plants erected to treat purchased
tailings. The Gillies plant is now an empty shell of gal-
vanised iron, surrounded by piles of dismantled pans and
other condemned machinery. An entirely new plant is
being installed.
» * * *
Oobar North (N.S.W.) is the neighbor of the
big mine. Much work has been done in sinking and cross-
cutting from several shafts ; No. 3 is the most likely, and
it is proposed to deepen it to 1250 ft. to try to pick up the
Cobar lode now. being worked about 600 ft. away. It
doesn’t seem far ; yet the Great Cobar Co. has been
working for years and hasn’t got nearer than 300 ft. to
the northern boundary. There is some excitement round
Cobar because copper carbonates have been found east of
the big mine. Cobar, like Cloncurry, should have got
used to discoveries of showy carbonate ore by this time.
* * * *
The “ good thing ” that it was reported a couple
of months ago might happen to the North Bulli Coal Co.
has happened, and shares have sprung from about 13s. 6d.
to 235. 6d. The good thing turns out to be an increase of
capital by the issue of 75,000 new £1 shares, of which the
big Adelaide Steamship Co. is alleged to have taken
50,000 at par, while the rest have gone towards clearing
off debentures. The available new shares didn’t quite
get rid of the debentures ; about £20,000 of the new
capital has also to go that way. The North Bulli hasn’t
been in the coal combine, and has been handicapped that
way ; but if the Adelaide S. S. Co., which is a big coal-
user and retailer, takes a kindly interest in it, it will be
on a very good wicket, nevertheless. One weak point
about it is that another co. owns the freehold ; it is only
a leaseholder itself. •
* * * *
A smart Victorian crowd took it into their
heads not long since that Mount Lyells were not stand-
ing up well enough in the market. So they started to
buttress them, but didn't reckon on the metal market.
Copper is, however, down over £3O a ton already. No
low-grade show like the Lyell is able to stand up against
trouble of that sort, and go on smiling. The little share
move, too, was not helped by a journalist in London, who
shot out an estimate of the strength of the Lyell reserves.
So, between one thing and another, Lyells have fallen
from over 50s. to about 30s.—a shrinkage of a million in
market value. Then, as the price of metal has eased £3O
a ton, there is a shrinkage of income of £234,000, on the
basis of last half-year's output. As Lyell ore itself is
down to o’6o per cent., work would have to be stopped if
that average material alone had to be handled. Fortu-
nately, North Lyell carries 5'35 per cent, copper, and in
the proportion used, the two, plus the metal-bearing flux,
pan outat about 2 percent. Last half-year, the profit was
£282,938, and metal averaged £lO6 9s. 3d. The copper
produced was 3796 tons ; that works out at £74 10s. Sd.
per ton. The metal is now bobbing about between £76
and £79 ; so, on last half-year’s basis, there is precious
little profit left—working, that is, as the co. worked
last half-year. Of course, the North ore is richer.
That ore is used, roughly, at the rate of 125,000 tons a
year, and there is proved three j'ears’ supply on that
scale. Even taking divs. at the same rate as last half-
year—ls. 3d. and a bonus of 2s.—the present market gives
five years’ life to the mine. But the existing price of
copper won’t permit of such a bonus. So a man has to
be possessed of great faith in the mining unseen to buy
now. It required even more faith to buy a few months
ago, yet many did so. North Lyell, too, has had the
experience of nearly all other mines; the ore does not
improve in value with depth—a little factor that may
upset more pots than one when the next levelis opened.
• * * »
Briseis and New Brothers Home No. 1 (Tas.)
are present-day tin-mining anxieties. To the mind of the
average man outside Brothers Home No. 1, the high reef
struck in the bend of the lead means a short life for the
claim. The rock is granite, and shelves slowly. Derby does
not doubt that the gutter has gone beyond the
boundary into the Briseis. Does it make back again?-
that is the delicate little problem that the proposed
boring will settle. Is it too much to ask whether Briseis
Clark has put down any bores to find how the land lies in
his claim? He is such a pleasant man that, even if he
had, he wouldn’t wound Joske’s feelings by saying so.
Still Bowes Kelly might be let into the secret, if there is
one. The worst is that Kelly is the sort of Irishman that
doesn’t pass things like that on ; so no one is quite sure
how the cat has jumped. Clark may want one of Bowes
Kelly’s winks to induce him to start. If that has not
been given, it is time it was, because it is as momentous
to Briseis as to Brothers Home that the gutter should not
narrow. Given a contraction and 50p.c. can be written
off the life of both claims. In the meantime, while some-
one is fiddling, a lot of rats are scuttling.
* * * *
According to the annual report, the cost of pro-
ducing copper from ore at the Wallaroo and Moonta(S.A.)
last year was £72 11s. per ton ; the metal leached out of
the old dumps was got for less, but there is no great quan-
tity of that. If those are normal figures, then Wallaroos
look pretty sick at even the present price of copper. But
it has to be remembered that for some time past this co.
has been fighting over the question of what constitutes
profit—that is to say, on what basis income-tax ought to
be assessed. Taking earlier reports and balance-sheets,
the income-tax people claimed (and the S.A. Supreme
Court has since unwisely supported them) that certain
wear and tear deductions may not be made. It is at
least probable, therefore, that last year the co. “got
back ” by doing a quantity of renewal and replacement
work, arid charged it up to ordinary working account.
Anyhow, mining, concentrating, and smelting costs,
which were only £55 14s. in 1904, jumped as high as
£7l 6s. 7d. in 1906. Probably, even now, figures could be
reduced to £56. The output is between 7000 and 8000 tons
a year, and the metal (fine) is worth about £3 per ton
above standard quotations. There are 160,000 shares in
the co. ; so it requires £2O a ton profit on an output of
8000 tons to pay £1 per share. But he is a sanguine man
who looks for a steady £76 for copper. For 25 years
past the average has been well under £6O.
« * * *
Eaglehawk Consolidated, Maldon (Vic.), has
come to a full stop as a dividend-payer, not through dear
work, but for want of gold. Costs are under 7dwt. This
is good for Maldon, where light stamps and roundabout
methods are the rule. The co. has a nest-egg in cash of
about £6500, but it has less than a half-year’s good ore in
sight above 950 ft. Shaft-sinking has to be faced, with
the same hunt after golden stone that is taking place at
the South German, though the outlook is not quite so
dreary as it is there. The best about the Eaglehawk
Board is that it works and doesn’t talk.
* * * »
Ballarat Sebastopol Plateau mining has shrunk
back to where it would have stuck if Sewell and Bent
had not fraternised to the tune of £5OOO for the sinking
of the South Star shaft. Ballarat East therefore holds
the district’s belt to-day. This is where the royalty king
rules in happy affluence, and where the tributor struggles
for bread. An instructive list shows how these so-called
hard-up claims have profited by a system of mining that
ought to be wiped out even more drastically than the
royalty evil. North Woah Hawp, on a paid-up capital of
7s. per share, has declared divs. of 335. 6d. ; Last
Chance, paid up 255., divs. 365. ; Victoria United, paid
up Bs., divs. 16s. 6d. ; Llanberris No. 1, paid up 215.,
divs. 255. ; New Normanby, paid up 16s. 10d., divs. 20s.
» • •* »
Visick, one time of the Prentice and Southern,
has returned to Rutherglen to manage the Chiltern
Valley Consols, after having tried to make a do of that
Tasmanian tin waster, the Briseis Extended. He is
punching out nearly 13dwt. wash at the Consols, and
would make ends meet had the mine not lacked
development. The claim was for a long time run on penny
calls, and was scoffed at as the outsider of the district.
Doubts existed whether the Chiltern Valley lead ran into
the claim at all; and boring hasn’t proved it so far.
Visick knows Chiltern of old, and he speaks with the cer-
tainty of the veteran declaring that the co. has 5000 ft. of
the gutter to work. “ In my opinion, your company’s
lead is one of the best in the district.... From the present
line of blocking to your southern boundary is about
5000 ft., and I believe the lead will be found to traverse
the whole distance In conclusion, I feel confident that
with both ends opened up, your mine will be one of the
largest gold producers and dividend payers in the
district.” Yet there are dead shares in the co. This fact
makes us ask whether Visick has ever been confident
before.
* * * *
There is a wealth of detail about the reports of
the Gordon Gold Mines, Gordon (Vic.), that you don’t
often get from the no-liability concern. Mine manager
John Walker’s report is that 200 ft. of driving north
should cut shoots of stone “ worked to water level, and
which I am informed on reliable authority yielded up to
12oz. and 16oz. to the ton.” How much 12oz. to 16oz. dirt
did the old-time miner ever leave behind, or, if you like,
ahead? The Board doesn’t look to 12oz. dirt, though ;
it expects, instead, to work, “ 26ft. of 4dwt. stone.”
Already the co. has got a surprise, hitting on a body
of quartz, 60 tons of which gaye 58oz. This is not
at all bad, and the work being done at the mine is cheap ;
and, of course, it needs to be if, apart from little
accidents like this strike of ounce quartz, it has to rely
upon 4dwt. “ tack.”
# * « *
New Dempsey’s, Gaffney’s Creek (Vic.), is up
to full value at 20s. on the short shoot of stone that it
has at present. The winze from the top level was stopped
when only 19ft. deep, because the gold was going out at
one side of it; they are now in a new winze further
ahead, so as to cut the shoot again at a point where they
may be able to take it down 40ft. or 50ft. before it goes
out again. Though the shoot is estimated to be worth
from 2oz. to 30z., still it must be short when a winze
19ft. deep will go through it diagonally.
* * * *
Victor’s Quartz, Wood’s Point (Vic.), is still in
trouble, and things are practically at a standstill. A
possible mine badly muddled.
r ¥ * # «
The Three Jacks, Stawell (Vic.), wnich has
been suffering from nervous prostration for some time,
because certain people in England haven’t felt inclined to
put up more cash, has had a clean-up from its new
cyanide plant. Rumor—the only person who locally
knows anything about the Three Jacks—loudly asserts
that the clean-up totalled about 3oz. from 300 tons, which,
if true, should give a shock to the people at the far end
of the cable. Anyway, the plant has, without undue
ostentation, closed itself down.
* * * *
The Bulletin had a suspicion that the Boulder
Perseverance’s whoop about its low working costs was
not justified, and the suspicion is deepening. In the old
days, when only “ounces” were sought after, a lot of
the stuff broken down was left behind in the stopes ;
while residues from the old incompetent treatment plant
were dumped underground to keep the sands company.
Latterly the ore sent to the mill has been leavened with
a lot of this filling; and as it can be dug out for very
little, costs have benefited considerably. By and by the
stopes will be emptied of their contents, and costs
will go up. Then shareholders will be treated to some
other yarn.
■* * * »
The recent fall in Kalgurlis (W.A.) is probably
due to poorer values in the bottom. All the newspaper
explanations try to cloak this speck on the mine’s pro-
spects, and rush on to tell of this or that face being
streaked with rich telluride. Shares, however, do not
drop from £l2 to £8 just for exercise. There is a reason
for such a fall, but probably “ the time is not yet ripe ” to
let the general body of shareholders into the know.
* * * #
First clean-up from the Gwalia South mill
(W.A.) will take place at the end of August. The co.
started crushing with about £4OOO to the bad, but the
first two months’ profits are expected to wipe off this
debt. It is estimated that further development will
show that the mine has enough ore down to 600 ft. (where
the shoot passes into the Sons of Gwalia) to pay the
present market value of its shares in dividends, and also
allow' sufficient for redemption of capital. But the esti-
mates are based on guesswork.
» * # *
The Golden Pole (W.A.) lingers on. Values are
principally confined to a small, erratic shoot whose twist-
ings and turnings give the management no end of trouble.
If two or three thousand tons of picked stone were taken
out, it would leave the mine as bare of pay ore as Mother
Hubbard’s cupboard was of hones; but as the use of
rock drills necessitates breaking down more than treble
the width of the reef, the w'hole lot is sent to the mill. It
is on this basis that the tonnage of ore reserves is sup-
posed to be reckoned. Half-monthly progress reports
issued by the management still read w’ell; but as the
mine seems to go from bad to worse in depth, the end of
profitable mining cannot be very far off.
¥ ¥ ¥ ¥
“Cover” dupes got a nasty wrench over the
fall in Lancefields to Ss. The difficulty at this mine still
rests with the treatment, but that is only temporary.
The mine is all right, and, some day, the shares will be,
too. It will take time, however, to clear off liabilities
and put the property on a profit-earning basis.
* * • *
The report of the Boulder No. 1 for 1906 states
that the year’s work resulted in a profit and loss debit of
£1147, battery earnings having been small, on account of
ore-supply for crushing having practically ceased. The
management speaks -well of prospects in the north-east
corner of the Kalgoorlie lease, but points out that a suf-
ficient length of ore has not yet been exposed to justify
the co. resuming operations, and recommends that the
tributors be left in possession for the time being. The
fact that the extension of the Ivanhoe east lode, now
being driven on at 1500 ft. in the adjoining Boulder block,
has a decided tendency to pitch north, is also noted, and
hopes are entertained of the shoot continuing into Boul-
der No. 1 ground at depth. A short reference is made
to the co.’s newly-acquired St. George leases at Magnet,
where, according to the management, the ore to
the 90ft. level will give enough profit to pay the purchase
money and provide the necessary treatment plant.
* * * *
Every steamer going Nor’-west from Fremantle
is crowded with miners for the tin and copper fields
between the coast and Marble Bar. Prospectors recently
dow r n in Perth from Roebourne, Port Hedland, and other
places, assert that copper has been found over a stretch
of country 250 miles long. A Bill authorising the long-
talked-of railway from Port Hedland to Marble Bar—
about 100 miles—has just been introduced : and, provided
the boodle can be raised, a couple of years should see its
completion. A lot of men are profitably employed tin-
mining at Wodgina, and it is proposed to deviate the
Marble Bar line to that district. The pick of the
Wodgina properties, embraced in the Mount Cassiterite
group, were offered to the William Knox crowd in Mel-
bourne, but no business eventuated. Port Hedland,
which has already four pubs, is a mere collection of gal-
vanised-iron shanties huddled together on loose white
sand bordered by mangrove swamps, and with a teeming
population of sandflies and mosquitoes.
0 m * »
Gregory Hills (W. A.), where Whitford’s Reward
original leases are situate, is a dead letter. The Whitford
co. sold its plant, tools, etc., to the State Government,
and the Government loaned them to prospector Mergher,
who, with his mate, Duplock, discovered the Green and
Gold, the rich-looking surface show of the Hills. At 15ft.
the gold cut out in the shaft; but sinking was continued
to 39ft., when the reef pinched to nothing. The place is
now abandoned. Whitford’s two 14 x 5 prospecting shafts
and Bob True’s gravestone are all that is left of another
W.A. boom.
* * * •»
Erliston and North Erliston districts, north of
Laverton (W.A.), are also on their last legs. There are
eight complete milling plants (53 head of stamps, all told),
and not one working. There does not seem to be any
chance of a revival either, for prospectors are giving a
wide berth to such a patchy field.
* * * «
Mt. Molloy (Q.) has fallen into disfavor because
it hasn’t found much ore at the 370 ft. level; it was a long
time, too, before ore was found at the level above, and
the shoot there has not been developed for more than
180 ft. Some of the- faces are looking well, and the
shallow ground may be expected to produce a lot of good
ore yet. Bridges have been built along the proposed
tramline, so presumably the directors consider the show
has some claim to permanency. Still they don’t always
know. Mt. Garnet, for instance, got a railway.
* * *
The battery at the Mount Usher, near Rock-
hampton (Q.), is being removed to Emuford, in the
Walsh and Tinaroo district. Mt. Usher is another in-
stance of the awful hash J. Bull makes with a fair, but
not mammoth, mine, and an over-sized battery. “ Then,
gentlemen,” the chairman used to say to Mt. Usher
shareholders in far-away London, “there is the Golden
Cave, from which has been extracted the splendid total
of so many hundred ounces. Can it be said that a com-
pany with a property including such an item as the
Golden Cave ought to feel despondent?” And various
retired colonels would enthusiastically shout, “ Hear,
hear,” and the usual vote of thanks would follow. The
fact that the wonderful Golden Cave was a very blank
cavity was somehow or other overlooked. The amount
of cash J. Bull has spent in acquiring cavities that at one
time contained a certain amount of gold is something
appalling.
* * • *
“ Rataroa ” : The Talisman, Karangahake
(M.L.), is in Bewick-Moreing’s stable, and is con-
spicuous among M.L. mines for publishing every month
detailed accounts of expenditure, including development
and office costs. Its directors, however, on the re-forma-
tion some years ago, fixed up an agreement eminently
satisfactory to themselves, by which they draw £lOOO a
year and 5 per cent, on all divs. up to £60,000. This per-
centage they have drawn for the last two years. As
220,000 out of the 300,000 shares are held in Maoriland, it
struck the Maoriland shareholders that they were fairly
entitled to a representative on the Board of five. They
nominated an attorney and a committee in England, and
forwarded their proxies accordingly, in the expectation
that the annual meeting would not be held till June 25,
the earliest date as yet. The English Board, however,
evidently smelt something coming, called the meeting
for May 30, refused to postpone, and threw out nomina-
tions and proxies as informal. When it is remembered
that the Talisman directors drew £4OOO last year on a
dividend payment of £60,000, while the Waihi directors
drew only £2400, and the Bank of New Zealand crowd
only £l6OO, comment is superfluous.

The reefs at Kamarooka (Vic.), that were
worked (with a good deal of crooked business) about a
quarter of a century ago, are to be again tested. A Mel-
bourne and Bendigo co. is shortly to begin on the site
of the old Homebound mine, and a Ballarat and Bendigo
co. has already started in a small way to open up the old
Doubtful workings. There are people who believe that
these reefs are the continuation of some of the Bendigo
lines.

The Aerial Tramways which have been erected
for the British and Sulphide Corporation (Central) Mines
at Broken Hill are fine examples of present-day skill and
technical knowledge. The Mines will effect considerable
saving by means of these Aerialways as compared with
any other known mechanical contrivance for handling
ores on a large scale. The British Mine will economise
to over 2s. per ton. These two plants have already been
subjected to severe tests, and have amply demonstrated
their worth and ability. Both Aerialways were supplied
by W. & J. Lampriere, of Melbourne, who represent A.
Bleichert & Co., of London, the largest firm in the world
for the manufacture of transport appliances.*

v • • *
After conducting exhaustive tests in the con-
centration of the ores at the Broken Hill South mine, the
manager, Mr. Wainwright, has finally decided to instal
the Wilflev type of table, modified in the direction best
adapted to his particular local requirements. The great
advantage with this type of table is that it admits of so
many slight variations which adapt it to meet all classes
of pulverised ores. Provided the pulp for treatment is
graded it can be used to treat all varieties of ore from
slimes up to coarse grades. When treating slimes and
lightly mineralised ores, care must be taken to have the
slope of the table accurate, and a fine, smooth dressing
surface beyond the feather edged diagonal termination of
the riffles. When treating coarsely ground ores or ores
heavily charged with mineral, as at the Broken Hill South,
the riffles or grooves can be carried further along the table
and in some instances right up to the concentrates dis-
charge end. Mr. Wainwright ordered six tables as an
experiment, with fewer riffles or grooves than customary,
and with some of them —the lower ones—carried to the
extreme end of the table. So successful has this type of
Wilfley table proved that he has ordered 50 more from
the Austral Otis Engineering Company. This seme firm
has been placing several of the patent Callow Screens at
Broken Hill and elsewhere. This ingenious and simple
mechanism has solved the problem of grading prior to
concentration.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
The salaries of Federal members have
now been raised to £6OO a year. It isn’t enough
—£looo a year would be a fair price for the sort
of intellect that the country wants to buy—but it
is an improvement, and something to go on with.
Apparently the small beginnings of the
Australian Navy are to be built in this country
after all. It would be nothing short of a crime to
follow any other course, but for a little while
weird rumors suggested that the crime might be
committed. It is pleasing to find that there is
nothing in the rumors, and that Australia is going
to do its own work.
THE MODERN
MOLOCH.
Touching the complaint that
the children in many parts of
the country are practically
offered up as a sacrifice to Com.
A country that was ready for them might
pick out a fine asset from among the 10,000 men
who have lately been dismissed from the Wool-
wich arsenal, in England,, They are the men who
could not only build Australia's navy, but could
also make Australia’s machinery, and its pots and
its pans—if only Australia had put up the tariff
in time, and thus got ready to receive them. If
Kingston’s duties had- been adopted, Australia
might have got hold now of a : ready-made army of
skilled artisans ; and the Australian farmer would
have had the job of feeding men, who make the
bulk of that £10,000,000 worth of metals and
metal manufactures which the Commonwealth
now buys from the foreign manufacturer, and had
no 16,000 miles freight to pay bn the food either.
Mothers need have no hesitancy in giving
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy to- the little ones.
It is perfectly safe,*
The Devil was the first
secessionist. He seceshed
from Heaven, and now runs
Hell as a separate State.
A wail arises from all
Melbourne’s land agents
owing to the Vie. Govern-
ment s proposal to start, in
the Lands Department, a
“Freehold farms branch,”
which will register, with full
particulars, a list of all
freehold farms offered for
sale. The Minister hurries
to say that the State does not
intend to do the work of
selling, but merely wishes to
bring, the intending pur-
chaser (especially if he is an
immigrant) in touch with
“ the accredited agent.”
The squeal of the agent arises
from the fact that many ven-
dors put their properties in
the hands of many agents
at once, and as there are in
Parliament many enterpris-
ing land-agents, skilful in
the art of biting the Minis-
terial ear, besides many out
of Parliament with whom
certain Ministers are par-
ticularly chummy, the plan
may lead to a careful feed-
ing of these firms by Go-
vernment. When 17 agents
are trying to sell one farm
the Department can’t put
the prospective buyer “in
touch ” with all of them ;
and they all want to know
how the “accredited ” shark
will be selected. It would
be better if the Ministry
gave purchasers the option
of going, right through with
the State as sole agent.
Land-agents (mostly Tories)
should not be afraid of the
competition of so “costly
and blundering” a business
concern as they always de-
clare the State to be.

probably because he couldn’t. And as there was

tile persons of great draught and tonnage, who
accompanied Rogers, things are no “forrarder.”

Last week there was formed in Sydney
“The Commercial and Industrial Federation of
Australia.” Its purpose is to fight like a herd
of devils against the new tariff, and presumably it
calls itself “industrial ” to create a false impres-
sion that the manufacturers are in the movement,
and that it represents real Australian industry!
The names of the organisers of said movement
follow
D. S. McPhail, Importer.
John Shorter, Importer.
Henry Howell, of Howard Smith and Co., Steamship-
owners. r
J. E. Edmondson, Importer.
W. S. Bradford, Property Agent.
W. Harding, Agent and Income-tax Adjuster.
A nice “industrial ” lot! What a heap of brawny
Australian industry is represented by W. Harding
income-tax adjuster, and D. S. McPhail, i m !
porter!
From Sydney Town Hall there has been
issued a return dealing with the Wexford-street
resumption. The owners of the filthy locality
claimed £228,000, which seems a good deal to ask
for a street of such disrepute that no one could
acknowledge he lived there without implying
that he had slipped off the last rung on the social
ladder, and dropped with a wallop into the sludge
of the Abyss. The Council valued the collection
of hovels and tenements at £138,000, which, in a
clean and sane community, should also have’ been
viewed as ridiculous. When a place becomes so
filthy, unsavory, insanitary and tumbledown that
a public authority has to resume it for the public
safety, it should have no value—that is to say, the
property and something over should be taken from
the owners by way of a line. To possess a £5OO pro-
perty that is an eyesore and a danger to life and
health should be worth a £6OO fine any day. So
far, the Council has settled £160,000 worth of
claims for £105,000, equal to about 13s. in the £.
The inference is that the Council has been badly
taken down.

drunk and incapable.

Iceberg Irvine grounded in rather shallow
water during the debate on the proposed increase
of Federal members’ salaries. He was making a
cold and pallid but lofty remark about the neces-
sity of consulting the people before doing things
like this, when he was suddenly asked if he con-
sulted the people before his famous disfranchise-
ment of the Victorian civil service. The question
was so peremptory that the Iceberg couldn’t
think of any brighter thing to say than “No.”
The Australian Postal Department has
come to a wise decision regarding the Tasmanian
cables. Four years ago the Eastern Extension
Co., which has a monopoly, and a subsidy and a
revenue guarantee for the service, offered Aus-
tralia its two cables for £70,000. The department
reckoned that £40,000 would be a verv corpulent
and portly price, and cried off. Since then
“ Eastern ” has been repairing its cables for
about 12 hours out of every 24. Interrup-
tions are almost continuous on at least one
line, and business gets frightfully congested.
The Government is setting out to lay its
own cables. ‘‘ Eastern’s ” contract and agree-
ments expire in March, 1909, so that it is
plain that after the date the Commonwealth
will get its plant for a song—if the Common-
wealth is independent of the company and
if the plant is worth buying as old junk.
However, Tory papers and Tory politi-
cians are already out urging the Government
to buy “Eastern’s” cables, and pointing out
what a benefactor “ Eastern ” has been to
Australia in the past. Isn’t it time that
somebody rose up and said that Eastern was
the beneficiary and not the benefactor ? It
charged Australia exorbitant rates for Euro-
pean cablegrams until the Pacific cable came
in and forced the price down to something
reasonable. The Eastern Extension Co.
has fattened on Australia for ever so many
years, and it could afford to hand over its
Tassy cables to the Commonwealth as a
present. But it is a powerful corporation,
and holds the championship belt for intrigue.
Look at that contract it got from N.S.W.
Postmaster-General Crick !
The Tory press, which so vigorously pro-
tested against the passage of the Federal Anti-
Trust Act, is at present making good copy out of
(a) the Tobacco Trust, (6) the Meat Supply ring,
(c) the raising of fares by the Shipping Combine’
and ( d) the Timber famine, engineered by the Tim-
ber Association. Some action under the Anti-
Trust law is contemplated against two of the
above combinations, and if it is found that the
law doesn’t reach them, it is already time to make
it more stringent.
The High Court has decided against
John Downer, K.C., and his client Vardoti in
their attempt to upset the election by the S.A.
Parliament of Laborite O’Loghlin to the Senate!
Downer demanded that the Court should order
the S.A. Governor to hold an election for a
Senator, but the Court replied that the Governor
was off its beat, or at least he wasn’t its drunk,
and it had no power to lay violent hands on him,’
or shout with a violent mouth in his ear. So
Downer was blown out without the real question
the validity of O’Loghlin’selection—beingreached.
The High Court also gave permission to every-
body to pay his own costs.

1 other day, in the House of Reps., that
staunch Freetrader, Bill Wilks, whose constituency
is a shipbuilding one, gave notice of his intention
to move that the bonus system should be extended
to the iron and shipbuilding industries. It was
ever thus. Theoretically, the Australian Foreign-
trader hates bonuses as an invoice fakir hates
honesty; in practice, when any Protection is
about he arrives invariably with a full-sized pan-
technicon van to collect as much of the Infamy as
he can lay his hands on ; and doesn’t turn Free-
trader again till some industry he isn’t interested
in is threatened with the accursed tariff heresy.
Simultaneously with its scream at the
tariff as “a Fiscal Outrage,” which makes the
public Pay! Pay ! Pay!!!” Sydney Tele-
gkaph momentarily forgets itself and tells the
truth :
The starting- of the furnace has had the effect of materi-
ally reducing the price of pig-iron in Australasia, so that
the assistance given to the firm through the tariff would
be more than made up by the advantages that users will
have m gettmg their iron as they require it, instead of
holding a stock of imported material, and obtaining the
local article at a lower price.
It is tolerably confused, but the facts are there.
From North Sydney
Sydney Telegraph made a bad break on Saturday last
by stating that Attorney-General Wade had “ delivered
an effective speech ” the previous night on behalf of
Carter, the selected Deform candidate for St. Leonards—
the truth being that Wade (though billed to do so)
never turned up. Things in St. Leonards haven’t been
over-bright, Carruthersly speaking, since Carter burst on
the scene. Half the anti-Laborites of the con-
stituency don’t want Carter ; and the rest don’t
know what they want. The main prejudice against
Caa-ter arises from the fact that he once openly
sold milk, or Still sells it in furtive fashion
or (even if he has stopped selling it for the
moment) is one who is liable to break out and sell milk
agrain on slight provocation. There is no suggestion that
the article Carter dealt in, or deals in, is, or ever was
other than good ; simply, Carter has milk-stains on him*
and in upper-class St. Leonards milk-stains are worse'
socially and politically speaking, than either birth, beer'
or blood stains. Most of the crowd that now objects to
Carter because of the milk taint in his record, objected
just as bitterly to Creswell—possibly because Creswell
had no connection with milk, or if that were not the
reason there was some other just as good. Meanwhile
the feeling grows that if things don’t simmer down a bit
amongst the Best People in this electorate, the Labor man
may slip in, m which case a number of stout villa-residents
will quite likely commit suicide, which would be most un-
sightly and lamentable.
Following remarks re the tariff by M.
Meagher, a director of John Meagher and Co
who run big general stores at Bathurst, Wyalong,
Barmedman, and other N.S.W. western towns,
in the Freetrade Bathurst Argus
Of course, there will be some alterations in the tariff
but, taking it rig’ht through, I think that in a few years
it will be found to be altogether in the interests of Aus-
tralia. We hear a lot about prices advancing, but we
heard that cry when the duty was put on cement. We
were told at that time that the price of cement would go
up, whereas, as a result of the duty on cement, we have,
within 50 miles of Bathurst, a great cement works em-
ploying 400 or 500 men, and producing an excellent
commodity at as low-, if not a lower price, than
that paid before the duty came into operation
Take another item on the list—blankets. Somebody
has said that the tariff imposition on blankets will send
up the price of these articles. This is an absurd state-
ment, because, for the past year or two, there have
scarcely been any other than colonial-made blankets
sold in this market. Blankets and other such lines are
made.in Victoria, where the people appear to be more go-
ahead than those in our own State. I hope that as a re-
sult of the tariff New South Wales capitalists, instead of
crying about Victoria getting the lead, will wake
up, and go in for industries and the building of factories
to compete with Victoria for the trade of our own State.
All of which, coming from one who is himself a
principal in a firm of importers, makes very
pleasant reading.
AURORA AUSTRALIS.
Dismal Dick Butler is getting tired of
the apparent permanency of his job as leader
of the S. Australian Opposition, and it isn’t un-
likely he will hand it over to Sudden Solomon,
who long ago was Premier for days, one even-
ing about breakfast-time. Every night, Butler
kicks himself as high up as he can reach without
being a contortionist because he didn’t leave the
wily Jenkins when that picturesque (Atlas) poli-
tician started to be Jenkins Iscariot. If he had
followed present Lands Minister O’Loughlin’s
example, Butler would probably have become
Premier of a Liberal Government, supported by
the Labor Party. The spectacle of Price, com-
fortably and firmly seated on the Ministerial
benches, is almost more than he can bear at the
salary—it’s only £2OO a year in S. Australia.
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy is a medicine
of great worth and merit. Try it when you have
a cold.*
’EAVY BURDENS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The Heavy Tariff: All the great primary industries
are hard hit.... There must of necessity be hard fight-
ing to prevent the burdens on the back of the husband-
men ... being increased.—Argus.
O, see the pore ol’ husbandman with burdens on his
back—
The man who shoves our tucker-van with burdens on his
back !
“ ’Oo, blazes, ’Lizer ! Misery an’ woe’s in store fer us !
Me cheque-book’s workin’ hours’ll be somethink eriorin-
i-ous !
Kid gloves is put up ten per cent., an’ perfoom’s outer
sight.
Lawd gimme back that quid I spent ter put the pigsty
right! ”
Ah, must he sell his slice of soil and perish on the
, track—
This stricken son of honest toil—with burdens on his
back?
Pity and pray for him—the one with burdens on his
back,
Who ponders, when the day is done, with burdens on his
back:
“ Tin-fish comes ’igh, now ; fresh er none our Friday
fish’ll be—
More work for me, ’neath blazin’ suns, ter catch crick-
trout fer tea !
Manillers are fer gentlefolk—it’s rough on me an’ Jim ;
He useter like ter watch me smoke a good cigar with
him ! ”
Ah, see the goodly stacks in rows around his eight-room
shack.
The sorrow of it! There.he goes with burdens on his
back !
“ My sufferin’country ! ” hear him groan, with burdens
on his back :
Ah, listen to the old man moan with burdens on his
back !
A thousand thoughts his trouble brings all swarming up
for speech :
“ Good gosh, Elizer ! Fashion tilings’ll rise clean outer
reach.
I see me fate in front o’ me ; it’s plain root-hog-er-die.
Alars! that I should ever see our good luck runnin’
dry.”
In vain the crushed one tries to choke his sorrow down—
alack !
He knows he is a pore ol’ bloke with burdens on his back.
VIC.
FRANK HENTY.

COPENHAGEN.
Premier Kidston lately decreed that the
forking day in Bananaland’s Government service
mould be eight hours. It was something of a co-
mcidence that at the very moment when he was
coming to this decision the Labor party was fatu-
ously struggling to wreck him.
The Anti-Sosh Victorian cow-farmers have
struck a new idea in Sosh : they want the State
schools to instruct their youngsters in the
mysteries of milk and cream testing, and the
Department is invited to supply each school with
£5 worth of apparatus. “ History,” says Cow
Fat —“what’s the good of history and this yere
jograph.y ? Teach ’em about butter.” Poor little
beggars! There’ll only be their dreams left to
them soon; and even their dreams are liable to
be streaked with cow-yard slush and poddies.
The Westralian wheat statistics begin to
tell the old story. The average yield per acre
still keeps up pretty well (it was 13'6 bushels in
1903-4, and it was 11‘0 bushels last harvest,
according to the official reckoning), but the
country lately taken up is mostly averaging only
seven or eight bushels. Katanning and Wagin,
whence the bulk of the new settlers have gone,
both averaged under eight bushels last harvest.
Yet Katanning is as much as 225 miles from
Perth; and, as a rule, it takes from £3 10s. to
£4 10s. an acre to clear the land there. Consider-
ing that Westralia has still only about 250,000
acres under wheat, it is becoming pretty evident
either that Westralia isn’t a wheat-growing
country, or that the land that will grow wheat is
shut against settlement and use.
It was the Beauty of Judith that ravished the
eyes of Holofernes, and brought about the relief of an
army. It was the beauty of Desdemona that caused
Othello his first and only compunction. Beauty is, above
all things, powerful. Then, Ladies, be Beautiful I Go
and have treatment at the Valaze Massage Institute, a
pretty suite of rooms at 158 Pitt-street, Sydney, or 274
Oollins-street, Melbourne. Here all disfigurements can
be overcome, and loveliness substituted by the power and
efficacy of Electrical Massage. Hollows are removed by
by the new paraffin treatment, and a fresh complexion
adds a charm to the personality.*
One of the easiest things in the world is
to plant a tree—it is as easy as cutting it down ;
and easier still it is to sit on the fence and watch
the tree grow. New South Wales is trying to
find out this fact by means of a forestry commis-
sion ; Victoria has found it out, and is putting the
fact in an Act of Parliament. Vic.. Forestry Act
proposes to dedicate forestry.areas in perpetuity,
subject to reduction by nothing less than a reso-
lution of both Houses. Unhappily, under the
Bill, the Ministry has five years in which it may
excise “settlement areas” before the restriction
operates. If greedy landgrabbers don’t eat up
too much forest in those five years, Victoria
should have the chance of a grand timber asset.
There is money in trees. Even at present rates,
an acre of pines, after 15 or 20 years’ growth, is
worth £150, and wood grows dearer all the time.
Premier Moore (W.A.) held forth in his
policy speech on the subject of the great things
the Government had done for the mining indus-
try. Whereupon the hungry prospector mostly
grunts out his scorn. The Government has
helped all right, but Fat has got most of the
help, and when it hasn’t been Fat, it has been
somebody with a “pull.” For instance : The re-
presentative of a corpulent Bull co. bought a
mine at Higginsville. W T hen it had got down
80ft., and was thinking of a battery, Bull’s repre-
sentative got the State to spend £4000 in searching
for water and bringing it to the mine, on condition
that now and again for two years the co. would
crush for propectors. The two years will end next
May ; then Bull will have the £4000 water equip-
ment, but the prospectors and little mine-owners
won’t have a battery—they will have to pay just
whatever the Bull co. likes to charge.
Beauty. —There is no topic interests women more
and no possession they prize more highly than beauty. It
has been so through all the ages, and while human nature
remains unaltered will still be so. Hence the Valaze
Institute established by Mile. Helena Rubinstein here in
Sydney caters to a distinct want—and caters thoroughly.
All the advances in beauty culture made by the great
Complexion Salons of Paris and Vienna are practised at
the Institute, and there is scarce a blemish that mars the
human face divine but Mile. Rubinstein has a treatment
to successfully remedy it. Red noses, double chins,
superfluous hairs, flabbiness, u'rinkles, crowsfeet, are but
a few of the unlovelinesses that are permanently removed,
and even the eradication of birth marks and such like
serious blemishes are successfully treated. Ladies should
call into the Valazelnstitute when in town —even a sitting
of face massage had there will result in benefit to one’s
looks. The address is 158 Pitt Street, on the second floor.
Also at 274 Collins Street, Melbourne. Ask to see Mile.
Rubinstein herself.*

shop" front of the well-known building at 395 George,
street, Sydney, and that front, like the lady’s new-open-
work peek-a-boo blouse, is a thing of beauty and a jo r for
ever.*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, August 20, 1907.
My Dear Arint,—
Wandering Australians comment admiringly
in their home upon the dressing of the Queen.
At a recent splash—the opening of the Union
Jack Club by the King—the female monarch
wore a gown of deep purple velvet, with a helio-
trope chiffon stole, and a toque with clusters of
violets and a tiny jewelled Union Jack. Lately,
the royal lady wore a biggish picture hat (at a
race or a garden party—some out-of-door frisk),
and that section of the public which is more con-
cerned about the dress of royalty than the rags of
England’s submerged nearly died of complex
emotions, mostly made up of delight and surprise.
Hitherto, no one has seen her wear any but
toques and the like small deer on her head. But
she hasn’t repeated the wearing of the biggish
hat. She is back under the small one.
A two days’ sale of the valuable household
furniture of Lieutenant and Mrs. Rawson is
announced. The pair have been motoring—and
having influenza—in Queensland, but they are
better, and back at “ Wallaroy,” packing up their
wedding presents in readiness for England. Their
friends say that “he is so nice to her.” In this
commercialised, unsentimental age, the bride-
groom of six months who continues to help his
wife lingeringly into her theatre coat, and talks to
her during intervals at the theatre instead of
going out to see a hydrant, is voted a “ dear.”
The beauteous Mrs. J. B. N. Osborne (who
was Miss Maud Jeffries) leaves America to
rejoin her sheep-owning spouse on the 13th
of next month. The lady has been “down
South ” on a month-long visit to her parents, who
are said to own broad acres and many horses.
Horses are “down south” what sheep are in
Australia. A man’s value down there used to be
estimated by the size of his drove of niggers, but
that was before those famous disturbances at Big
Bethel,_ and Chickahominy, and Murfreesboro’,
and Chickamauga, and Chattanooga, and Spott-
sylvania, and all the other places where the name
on the station is so long that two trains can stand
in front of it at once.
“Mr. Hopkinson,” now running at Sydney
Cri., is, despite its absurdity, literally drawn
from life. London’s “smartest” flocked to it
when it first appeared, and shrieked approval of
the careful character portrayal to be found therein.
The Duchess, whose part was toned down later,
in deference to the wishes of some royalty or
other, was originally the Devonshire lady to the life
—the merry old soul of whom it has been said that
she “ knows more about cards and racehorses than
any man in England.” The fascinatingand chronic
co-re. was at once and enthusiastically accepted
by all who knew anything of the great world
about the English Court as an Earl whose appear-
ance in the Divorce Court was for long one
of the features of any really successful London
season. The gallant nobleman timed things in
thoughtful fashion ; he selected, As a rule, the
early summer as his time for being co-respondent,
that being the time when all his friends would be
in town to share the fun. “Hoppy” was vari-
ously identified as Lipton, “Solly” Joel—
S’African millionaire —and an ex-Lord Mayor of
London, who made up in good red gold what he
lacked in the way of mere aspirates. But it was
generally conceded that there were so many like
him amongst the Best People that it was impossible
to definitely fix Hoppy.
Miss Victoria Lyne, daughter of New Tariff
Lyne, is returning by the Mooltan. But she’ll
pause in her return at the green-and-red city of
Colombo, and stop there for a month.
In unobtrusive fashion Mr. Henning, the
millionaire racing owner, has arrived in N.S.W.
He is not here in search of horses, but to look
after some mining interests he has. In Cold
Country racing circles not to know Henning, at
any rate by sight, is to argue yourself a confirmed
and utter Jud. In those quarters, Henning is, to
a certain extent, It. Till recently, his ambitions
did not lie in social directions, but of late the
position has altered somewhat, and the man of
many shekels is said to propose entering the
sacred fane, if he has to use an axe over the job.
Henning is a bachelor, and would be a good sort
to marry ; so local maidens should be on the alert,
with pearl powder dry and ready, and the hypno-
tic smile at hand.
Art tor Art’s Sake, Art for Life’s Sake,
Art. for Sydney’s Sake—Mayor Hughes dealt
most deftly with them all when opening
the Society of Artists’ Show in the Q.V.M.
on Friday. The Society has cut itself off from
the old Royal Art Society, and this kind of ampu-
tation has to be diplomatically handled. Amongst
the women exhibiting are Misses Ethel Roberts,
Muskett, Quaife, Stephens, Vale, and M’Whan-
nell,. and they give a good account of themselves,
especially along color and decoration lines. A result
of Douglas Fry and Sid. Long’s collaboration is
“The Valley”—a star performance, although
the horsey-artistically inclined are “ saying
things” about the principal steed’s anatomy.
Howard Ashton’s “End of the Road” shows
him a long way on the bumpy and cobbled-stoned
track to Fame. All his work satisfies eye and
heart and judgment. Howard is now on the
S.M.H., and already his “ littery ” work is taking
the public eye. Sid. Long’s new clutch of cranes is
excellent, and Harry Weston’s Australian friezes
have always an audience of brides-elect (such a
lot of them about now), who would like “ our new
house decorated by him.”
Miss Grace. Palotta will have an At Home on
Friday afternoon at the Society’s rooms, and on
Monday Mrs. Hugh Ward will hold the after-
noon’s reception.
Mrs. Blanche Arral in afternoon street frocking
is an epitome of Paris style—big hat with the
new round, filet net veil, striped blue and white
blouse, with green velvet buttons, smart belt, and
short, dark-blue skirt, zouave of white aftd blue
serge, and a tawny white boa. Her benefit
concert will occur next , Tuesday night,
at Sydney Town Hall. Most probably the
Governor and the military will irradiate the
function. Miss Alice Bryant, Chester Fentress,
Laurence Godfrey Smith, Staell, and Sykes are
amongst those who will revolve on the platform
around the brilliant French singer.
Miss Rawson will open a Bazaar in the
Y.M.C.A. Hall on September 3—the proceeds to
go in aid of the work of the Sisters of the Church.
Woollahra, Potts, Darling and Piper Pointers—
‘‘pointers” in this connection has no dishonest
significance—will take stalls, and will be assisted
by Mosman, Neutral and Elizabeth Bayers and
Lane Coves. Contributions for any stall thank-
fully received.
An American paper containing a portrait of
Madge Corcoran is to hand. Victorians will
remember her better than N. S. Welshers. (I hear
quite a lot of people speaking of us lately as
“ Welshers,” and it gives me a bit of a shiver. Is
it an indelicate allusion to our Mr. Carruthers’
attitude towards the other States?) She was
one of a musical and dramatic family, and
a pupil of Mrs. G. B. Lewis, on whose
premises she had Eugenia Duggan for a fellow
student. Madge was as pretty a girl as any
in Melbourne, but though she climbed the pro-
fessional stage, and was acting off and on for
years in Australian capitals, she never seemed to
rise above average amateur level. Some six or
seven years ago she went to America, married and
acted herself into a good position. A few years
back she was a memher of Sothern’s co., and un-
derstudying the leading woman. Her portrait
shows her as Mercia, the chased lady in “The Sign
of the Cross.” A critic says splendid things of
her in the character which Maud Jeffries made
so tearful. Pretty Madge is now a widow.
Other mummer news from New York is that
Maggie Moore is to support Thomas Jefferson,
playing the part of Gretchen in “Rip van
Winkle.” Mabel Tagliaferro, now Mrs. Thomp-
son, is doing something wonderful in a circus
play.
At the recent Horse Parade and Show: Old
veteran to another, who wants to sell him a horse
for £400: “I’ve never yet seen the horse for which
I’d give £lOO a leg.”
News of the death of David Christie Murray
recalls that he married as his second wife a Mel-
bourne girl. Miss Alice Dudley. Her mother had
some billet of trust in the Victoria Coffee
Palace. Alice was a nice, round girl, much
younger than her “Dave.” They metduringone
of Harry St. Maur’s disastrous theatrical tours.
She wanted to be an actress (is there a girl who
doesn’t or didn’t ?). He wanted to be an actor —a
queer taste for a man to develop in middle age !
After the tour, which left him almost penniless,
he wrote the serial story, “Bob Martin’s Little
Girl,” in a few weeks. His barnstorming gave
him sufficient Australian backgrounds. I think it
ran in the Age or Leader, and Mr. David Syme
gave Murray enough for it to take him comfort-
ably off this continent.
A Sydneysider, writing from London, mentions
that she occasionally sees there the Queensland
squatter, Vicomte Oscar de Satge, and his wife
and a couple of infants motoring about. The
marriage of the unobtrusive aristocrat a few years
ago to a little Sydney typiste, who hadn’t put
her hair up or her skirts down, caused a ripple or
two for a week or two. Then the waters were
again calm, as if no big fish had been landed.
Some gossiper talks of the engagement of the
second Loch girl, Hon. Evelyn, to one of the
Gordon-Lennox family. When the Lochs ran
the vice-regal show in Victoria, the girl, whose
engagement is just announced, was a short-
frocked youngster (the Peerage coarsely states
that she is now 31), and gossip says that she,
her mother and elder sister had enough hair for
six. It was sleek and straight, and of that shade of
fairness that looks almost grey—an ashen blonde.
Good complexion, poor teeth and large features also
were theirs, but the second girl was a jolly little
soul, and if she were present on a dais when Pa,
the Governor, was opening something with a
speech, she would emphasise his points by wink-
ing at members of the staff. Loch had a more
beautiful beard than those of Dan O’Connor and
Editor Ward in combination.
Saw Mr. Warburton Gamble, of the Ward-
Willoughby Co., on Saturday afternoon, an hour
before he became the husband of little Miss Scaife,
of the same co. He and Willoughby came into a
tobacconist’s where I was buying clay pipes (for a
young friend to blow bubbles), and the bride-
groom-elect, whose teeth are as good as Captain
Share’s, was as cool as a fish. In fact, as
Willoughby mentioned, they’d “just been buck-
ing him up with a fish for lunch next door at the fish
shop.” Could anything be more respectable and
more free from the slightest suspicion of carousal
or frenzied dissipation ? Willoughby was mildly
reproachful about his “ alleged daughter.” The
stork handed him a boy, he says—“a big fat
boy.” At least I can plead in defence that I
guessed it the second try. The fat infant takes
after his pa, although Willoughby isn’t more than
half as opulent in the waistcoat as he was when
Sydney first made his acquaintance.
Senator and Mrs. Macfarlane, of Tasmania, are
in Sydney for a space. So is Mrs. Robert Walker,
of the same sober little island.
Miss Molly Smith, of Manly, and Mr. Willie
Dunlop, a member of the well-known Edwards-
Dunlop firm, are announced as candidates for the
“Wedding March ” to be played at them, and
expect to be elected by a large majority.
A man of my acquaintance tells me that he has
solved the mystery of the oyster patfi of the sub-
scription ball, which has no oyster. He had begun
to think that the caterers omitted the oyster, but
at the supper table of one of this winter’s charity
balls, he saw an old campaigneress lift the crust of
a whole dish of patties, withdraw the oyster from
circulation, eat it, and then replace the crust and
restore the empty sepulchre to its niche on the
dish. Well, as some ecclesiastical person remarks,
“ All wisdom cometh from the Lord.”
One of Sydney’s prettiest and cleverest and
froekiest girls is to take the star part in the
marriage drama about Easter. The man who
will play lead is a good-looking rising young
Australian. They are not telling anyone just yet,
which is the reason why I carefully avoid men-
tioning the matter. Only about 500 intimate
friends are in the secret.
To procure funds for the Mosman contribution
to the Australian Exhibition of Women’s Work,
the Mayoress of Mosman, Mrs. Leahy, presides
at an uncommon form of concert to-night
(Tuesday). It is to be conducted entirely by
women. The example might be followed by other
seekers after funds for the Ex.
One of the Telegraph’s lion cubs has been
nuzzling Mr. Bent into talking of “Paree.” The
gentle Victorian spent two days and three nights
in “ Paree,” and his observations narrow down to
diamonds, motor-cars —thousands of those —the
opera, hats worth £lOO, and the long flat feet
of the Frenchwomen. He merely mentions these.
The statements are rather bald as descriptions,
y,ut made humorous and intellectually significant
by Mr. Bent’s various adjustments of his top hat
—the moments of fervor when he took it off, and
the moments of scorn when he put it on straight,
and the moments of passionate abandon when he
dashed it sideways over one eye. But readers will
shy at Frenchwomen’s “ long flat feet.” Fashion
in French footwear is the long and narrow.
Often the Parisienne’s big toe has two or more
inches of unoccupied shoe territory ahead of it.
Length and narrowness are “ de rigger” and very
“degaggey”; but the "flatness” Mr.
didn’t stop long enough in “Paree” to see
French feet. The footwear he saw must have
been worn by strangers in the land.
An old lady who deserves recognition in the
Woman’s Column died last week at Neutral Bay.
Mrs. Beattie was known in Sydney as being at
the head of fashionable dressmaking—the Worth
of Sydney, in fact. For many years she was m
partnership with Mrs. Doak, in Wynyard-square.
She kept on the business right up to a few months
ago, when she was stricken with paralysis. She
made it a condition of her work for “swell wed-
dings’’that she should attend at the house and
see the bride dressed for the sacrifice.
The claim of G. H. Druce, of Maoriland, to the
Portland dukedom begins to look like business.
Druce, who has formed himself into a Limited
Co. to prosecute his suit, alleges that the late
Duke—who was a lunatic, by the way, and capable
of doing any old thing alive or dead—was not
buried where he is supposed to have been, but
lived to perpetrate him (G.H.D.), the Rightful
Heir. The present Duke (when he condescends
to discuss the matter at all) suggests coldly and
malevolently that Druce borrowed this yam
from Bilanderson of the Sydney Cri., and
begs that the preposterous topic be suffered to
drop. And it does drop, but the trouble is that
it won’t stay dropped. For years the highest
brand of London society has striven heroically to
take the same lightsome view of the matter, but
of late waverers have made their appearance
even about the very Court where the Duke is
Master of the Horse and the Duchess Mistress
of the Robes. Druce’s solicitor, one Coburn, a
coarse Australian person with no respect for his
betters (solicitors in the Motherland are, by the
way, on much the same social plane as tripe mer-
chants), now intimates that the case will come on
within the next two months; and when it does
come on, says Coburn, the revelations will be
awful and hair-raising.
Miss Constance Usher, a nice girl and a pretty
pianist, is engaged to the chief officer of a Jap
liner. He isn’t a Jap, though the liner is.
Mrs. Eileen Mitchell O’Moore (Bessie Doyle),
who is back in Sydney, teaching the violin,
married an unusually wealthy man—Mitchell—
someone of the steel-king tribe. But Fortune’s
wheel qamefull circle, and ill-luck was there. The
violiniste resumed her art, but her health went
steadily to the bad; hence her return to her
native land.
Arnold Gange, the basso of the late Amy
Sherwin co., with his white face, brown eyes, and
faint Scottish accent, will sing in Goulburn in
September. Talking of Miss Clara Butt, he
doesn’t think the Sydney Town Hall organ could
dominate her phenomenal voice. Her artistic
singing of Needham’s “ Husheen ” seems to dwell
in his memory. Miss Irene Aynesley, the Maori-
land contralto, whose career Melba backed so
warmly, is making large, round sums by singing
at Music Halls. This sounds to us like a descent
in the lyric ladder, but Gange swears by his best
mauve tie that it’s quite a high rung in old
England.
It has come at last. In sooth, in times of stress
the truth bursts into the open with all the inevi-
tability and force of the harried business man
who has returned from the office to find his
political wife discussing the tariff with 17 women
leaguers and one small, pale male enthusiast, in
the only room that has a fire in it. At a meeting
of baronets in London last month —called owing
to the perfectly sickening habit that assassins and
other social impossibles have of claiming member-
ship of the guild—the Main Rabbi, or whatever
he is called, of all the barts., one Richard
Harrington, uttered the plain, bald truth in
regard to knighthoods. A knighthood, said
Richard in effect, was no honor; in fact,
it was a badge of shame. He, Richard, would as
lief be a company-promoter as a knight. Had he
his way, certain offenders against law and order
should be made liable to knighthood as an alter-
native to the indeterminate sentence scheme.
Then, in broken tones, he told of a friend of his
“in a high position, who wished he was a
baronet, because it would save him from the dis-
grace of being knighted.” Whereat all the other
barts. said “hear, hear”; and “shame,” loudly
and decisively ; and wandered home explaining
to one another the absolute noisomeness of the
average knight; and how the whole institution
had dropped flowering, and gone to seed utterly
and for ever.
My Brisbane correspondent writes :
The week is ended, and the 10,000 visitors who thronged
the city during the Show, have “ blewed ” their last bean,
and tumbled themselves, with their wives and bundles,
into trains and steamers, and departed for Cow Land.
What a record it has been ! Record crowd, record show,
record weather 1 Every one was jolly. Mr. and Mrs.
Cocky had a lot more cash than usual to scatter this
year, and were quite well dressed, while the Panama
brigade from the stations indulged in motor cars and
champagne all day, and held chariot races up and down
George-street by night.
The popularity of your sailor Governor is not confined
to your own State. The Admiral Excellency has quite
captured the hearts of the Brisbaneites, and the sight of
“Miss Dimples” moved hardened squatters to tears as
they thought of their vanished youth and innocence, and
of what might have been but wasn’t. Father and daughter
have been a piece of bright relief in a rather icy and
formal vice-regal domicile. The truth of the matter is
that Her Firmness is a very sick woman, and without her
there has been little inclination on the part of His Frail-
ness to go about.
Beginning with the opening of the Show, at which Mrs.
Excellency didn’t appeer, he was kept busy apologis-
ing for her non-appearance all the week. Still there was
an imposing crowd from Government House. His Ex. and
Sir Harry, with Captains Newton and Wilson, filled the
landau ; Miss Rawson, Mrs. Villiers, the Du Cane, and
Verney the Sublime were squeezed into the barouche ;
and Col. Goad—he of the Indian Remount Department—
and the sleek Villiers occupied a hired conveyance
drawn by two grey shadows. Miss Rawson smiled
in a heliotrope cloth composition, with a white hat
swathed in velvet; while the stately Villiers dame
chose an opaline green and a hat with greenish
plumes and golden brown roses. The Du Cane lightened
her black and white robes with a big cluster of purple
on her breast. Villiers, M.P., in the course of a speech
in a cool bush shed, confessed himself “ flabbergasts d ”
at the resources of Australia. “Good Heavens!” he
passionately reminded them, "only foster your in-
dustries, and you can have the world at your feet.” And
we’re going to do it.
On Tuesday evening’, his weak, tired Excellency, with
Miss Rawson in a dream of a white satin robe, and. the
Du Cane, graced Mrs. Murray Prior’s dance, given, it is
understood, as a farewell before the hostess goes to Eng-
land, and makes the grievous mistake of arriving there
just as the autumn fogs begin to be a thick muddy
blanket over London town.
The last of the exclusive Assemblies flickered in a
partnerless State on Wednesday night, and the distracted
hostesses were hard pressed to find dancing men to go
much more than half round the bevy of bright country
girls that filled the room. The decorations were a
dream.
On Thursday evening the Government House Ball shone
with a splendor that had but one blot upon it, and that —
speak it not above a whisper—was the whisky. It dried
up. Lady Chelmsford made her first appearance of the
week in a shimmering grey satin and gauze, and the rest
of the household shone in brave attire. Mrs. Jim Robert-
son, in blue and silver, and Miss Leslie Harris took the
prize for clothes.
On Friday, Ted’s representative and Sir Harry left to
stalk the wily and dangerous snipe at Dellilah. The
women folk again drifted Showwards, and on Saturday
afternoon they took in the “Mother Goose ” matinee,
along with the Chelmsford hopefuls.
And, by way of a hurried wind-up, here are three
items. (1) Pretty Florence Waldron Taylor (with her
husband in close attendance) has returned from Europe,
and looks bigger, brighter, and bonnier than ever. (2)
The Villiers couple unfurled their deck chairs, and silently
stole away to Jappy land by the Yawata Maru on Friday.
(3) One of the best-dressed women at the Q.T.C. Races
was your Mrs. Ted Simpson. She wore a wonderful
gown of green silk and oriental appliqufi on Friday, and a
blue-braided velvet on Saturday.
Yours Affectionately,
Akenehi.

The Hand
With September, The Lone Hand begins color-
printing, and a reproduction in colors of Long-
stalfs famous “Breaking the News” will,fitly
mark the new departure. In future it is hoped
that, with its covers and frontispieces, The Lone
Hand will give to every Australian home a little
picture gallery.
The sweet breath of the pittosporum tells Aus-
tralians in September that Spring has come. The
Lone Hand will mark the month with special
Spring matter. Humor by “Hop,” verse by
Roderie Quinn, a charming idyll by Norman
Lindsay and Hugh McCrae, will sprinkle the
Spring number with smiles.
David Scott Mitchell was one of the unknown
great men of Australia. His recluse life shunned
publicity. The first revealing of his personality
is in The Lone Hand article, by Arthur W. Jose
(September). It is alone worth the
price, but is only one of several interesting
articles —“The Prime Minister at Home,” by
“F.R.,” “ Hunting in N. S. Wales,” by “First-
Check,” and “Politics and Poetry,” by “ J.B.D.,”
among them. There is matter to please every
reader—from the “sport ” to the literary student.
Alf Vincent has so far been absent from the
Lone Hand pages. He was away in Asia collect-
ing impressions. The first of his articles, full of
humor and bright pictures, is in The Lone Hand
for September.
Impossible to say all that is in—or nearly 1 all.
But order early. It is going to be a rushed
number.

Melbourne Age opposes any increase of
the salaries of Federal members, but admits that,
after long and honorable service, “ the political
derelict is often brought face to face with the
bitter necessity of making a fresh start in life,
with the weight of age on his shoulders, with
used-up energies, and with his earning capacity
sadly diminished.” Had the paper been favor-
able, it might have pointed its moral with'the
story of Graham Berry and the men who fought
shoulder to shoulder with him to secure for the
Victorian democracy liberties which would be
cheap at millions. Every man of them, barring
James Munro, died poor, some of them neglected,
Berry himself in receipt of a niggardly pittance
wrung from a reluctant Parliament in a manner
that must have made the charity very bitter to
the grand old veteran. Munro still lives, but he,
too, is a poor man.
