CORSETS
MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, August 19, 1907.
Mr Dear Yareik.—
The officers of the Sixth Australian Infantry
Regiment gave the whirliest sort of ball last
Friday. If a dance is to be endured at all, it is
just as well to pick a regimental one (that is if you
are Someone enough for an invitation). Military
men dance so well —and as for flirting who is
better than a sojer ! Then they do look so nice
and straight and gold-laced, and the dangling
little medals and jewels on their manly chests
are so fascinating—so much more interesting to
gaze at in the whirl of waltz than a plain
black coat. The newly-appointed Victorian Com-
mander—Colonel Stanley—shone as the particular
star. Colonel Evans has his pretty little Sydney
niece, Miss Mcßoberts, staying with him. She
“debbed” on Friday night, and didn’t the Subs
rush her! It was rather a manless dance—
though the fact was scarcely noticeable—the men
that were there took up so much room in the pic-
ture. The frocking was brilliant, and didn’t take
any second place with the gold lace. Two gowns
stood out conspicuously. One was beautiful. It
was soft twilight-purple crepe, and all the lacey
soft white frills showed glimmering through. The
other was hideous, vivid orange silk—it was a
parrot-scream of color over the warm shout of
regimental scarlet.
It’s quite an old and monotonous moan of young
girlhood that the widow and the smart married
woman gaily attach the available men and the
nice boys, while early spinsters have to be con-
tent with the downiest and pinkest and most
callow of youth. The contrast is made more
painful by the woeful precocity of the Australian
she-lambkin. But things are reaching a high
pinnacle of shriek when grandmothers take up
the running. The widow of a wealthy Going-
going-gone citizen is the immediate ancestress of
two smart chicks, prosperously married, who
have junior chicks fluttering at their heels. But
grandmamma doesn’t indulge in side curls and an
armchair. Her curls are the newest “extras,”
and she seeks the cosiest corner of Cafe Paris for
afternoon tea or supper, and “takes notice”
quite frivolously with one of Melbourne’s very
prompt lawyers.
Mrs. Reed, of Kenly, Elsternwick, gave an At
Home on Thursday (August 15), in honor of the
marriage of her sister, Miss Eugenia Stone. The
wedding was celebrated in London at 2 o’clock,
and the evening reception of Miss Stone’s inti-
mate friends in Melbourne exactly corresponded
with the hour of the event. It was a warm-
hearted gathering. Incidental to the drinking
of the distant bride’s health, pretty speeches were
made by the Hon. F. C. Mason and Dr. Harbin-
son, and warmly responded to by Mr. Stone. The
bride’s initial in wattle, and the bridegroom’s in
oak leaves, were twisted into a huge monogram
and hung over the board that the food was on—
the festive board, as the daily paper loves to
call it.
Now that the “Madcaps” have gone, Melbourne
misses a tievy of enthusiastic golfers. Many of
the chummy little lovey-dovey co. were players,
but Miles Clifton and Mrs. Daisie Wallace Clifton
and baritone Kenneth lived every spare moment
at Fisherman’s Bend with a devoted caddy, and
grubbed round happily for lost balls. “Majah ”
Clifton even developed “golfer’s wrist,” which
seems to be worse than housemaid’s knee. The
spirited “ Majah ” had a knot cut out from his
wrist —but went on with his dance and song never-
theless. He was about the only mummer of the
crowd who did not draw on his understudy during
the season.
The fluffiest and eldest of the clutch of the
gilt-edged Horsham Youngs (who ship horses to
India) departed last week on the Yorck for a con-
tinental trip. She nestles under the youthful
wing of cousin Lily—Mrs. Wallach, a smart St.
Kilda dame. Mr. Wallach goes also. He’s an
ideal husband, and is warranted to fix the chairs
at the right angle, and pile the rugs and cushions
at their cosiest.
The “Hurraw! Hurraw! Hurraw!” boys
finished up their career in these parts by scoop-
ing in all the goals at hockey-on-the-ice. But the
Australians were not jealous—the Canadians had
skated since their babyhood, while the local
hockeyites have had barely two winters in a
Glaciarium. The ice rink did the game proud
with soft-colored lantelns and a good band, and
a Governor or so to applaud.
The bewitching Mexican lady who com-
placently puts up with much lorgnette-pointing
at theatres and such, just now, has an interesting
Spanish husband. Hubby, however, has to take
a minor place in the theatre box. There is a third
member of this attractive family—a very small
maiden of four or five years.
The reconstructed Stirling-Fenner engagement
is booked for a wedding-bell climax this week.
Dr. Stirling’s lily-fair daughter has been getting
together many frills in honor of the event. Lilies
of every kind are to figure profusely as the em-
blematical wedding flower, and Macedon gardens
have been raided for a truck-load of the pure
white blossoms. Talking of which, one is rather
reluctant to remark, in parenthesis, that the New
Testament lilies, whose array of loveliness out-
classed the spectacle of Solomon-in-all-his-glory,
were very different from the emblems of purity
that get credit for a sweet scriptural advertise-
ment. The lilies that put Solomon’s regal clout to
shame were the gorgeous crimson-scarlet variety—
short-stalked and bell-shaped—which exactly fill
the average Hebrew idea of choice raiment. And
they bloom not in the spring worth tuppence.
There are still rumors of Amy Castles’ d4but in
grand opera. That poor debut will be worn out
before it arrives. The last hearing was a Berlin
appearance, postponed because the maiden
developed German measles in London before sail-
ing. Sister Dolly, who came into prominence
later, and studied with so little fuss, is getting on
famously in small, ’cute, comic opera parts in
London.
The Naval and Military Club is the centre of
most serious discussion. A huge ball is on the oil-
cloth —a combined shivoo of the gold-laced of
land and sea. Derby night is the suggested date,
and the tone of it is to be so swagger that
there is likely to be a pale rose aura round
whatever hall is chosen for the festive whirl.
Just now plans are chaotic—battles, and the roar
of the artillery (which isn’t getting its own way)
threaten to smash up the peace of theclubites, and
cast a veil of smoke over the event itself.
Janet Missus’ return to Cliveden was the signal
to commence operations concerning the Newsboys’
Society’s annual ball, which will happen on Sep-
tember 3, in Prahran Town Hall. The Dowager
is treasurer, and Misses Muriel Cumming and
Jessie Landale join forces, with a brace of
experienced male persons as hon. secretaries.
Mrs. Forrest will guide a flock of debutantes into
the hurly-burly.
Harry Sweeney, the young man beautiful who
replaced Carter Pickford with the Flemming Co.,
becomes leading man in the Holloway Co. anon.
His pretty wife also recommences mumming in
the same crowd, and will play grand dame parts
when necessary.
The Fisken family returns almost immediately,
owing to the ill-health of genial John. All the
time he has been “abrawd” he has been ailing,
and like a sensible man, he concludes that Aus-
tralian air is the best cure obtainable. Mrs.
Fisken dutifully accompanies him ; but the
McCaughey daughters linger awhile in Fogland
enjoying the novelty of a sunless summer, and a
nation which says “ Aw 1 ” before it can make up
its mind to say “ Reahly ! ”
Mrs. Tittell Brune really and truly leaves us on
26th September. Mr. Tittell will accompany her,
and the public will know neither of them for a
litt'e while. The lady expects to be back again in
March, 1908; meantime she will meander about
the Old World, seeing famous actresses playing
the parts that will make up her new repertoire.
On Thursday, the Athenaeum Club indulged in
a festive afternoon. The occasion was the hang-
ng of Justice Holroyd—or, rather, an admirable
portrait of him, painted by Leslie Wilkie. Mr.
McDougall “ chaired ” the proceedings, and
everyone said complimentary things to the artist
and the original about the picture. His Honor
was quite touched by his own obvious popularity,
and by the amazing resemblance of the picture to
himself, and said so very gracefully. Then he
was given a smaller replica of the club’s acquisi-
tion, which, it is suggested, should one day revert
to the National Gallery.
Miss Beatrice Irwin, the superior young person
whose well-advertised grandmother, Lady Hil-
liard, “ is not yet reconciled to her grand-
daughter’s stage career” (poor old lady !), has
some relatives of polite significance to the city of
Sydney. She is either a niece or cousin of your
judicial G. B. Simpson, and will probably be the
guest of his family what time she play-acts in his
neighborhood. Inquiries about the well-bred
young woman’s histrionic capabilities are replied
to with a full and particular account of her
ladylike deportment and her disgusted granny.
But, nevertheless, the blessed Beatrice’s mum-
ming promises to be the happy mean ’twixt a
walking lady and a star tragedienne—which
means that she’ll be natural, pretty, and hard-
working.
Barrister G. A. Maxwell, of the eloquent
Scawtch tongue, and his charming missus, are the
latest pair who need to decide upon a nice name
for the new baby. The names of John, Henry
and William are barred by circumstances over
which the baby has no control; but Juliet might do.
It would seem that we are reverting to our
grand-parents’ habit of marrying before we’ve
been in and out of love more than three times.
During this month no less than four couples, all
of whom are only skirting the mid-twenties, have
battened themselves down in matrimony.
Even society Bridge players have their virtues,
as one specially proficient performer has learned
by experience. The player in question is a pro-
verbially bad loser, and one day, awhile ago,
when the cards were going dead against her, she
created a diversion by walking off with the
character of a popular nurse. Said nurse had a
friend at the next table. The lady rose in her
wrath and demanded an apology, which the Cat
vixenishly refused to give her. “Very well,” re-
marked the avenging angel, “ I leave the house.”
Things were whirly enough to demand the ser-
vices of a referee, who gave unqualified judgment
in favor of the nurse. A quiet, but firm, hint of
future proceedings brought Cat to her senses, and
the necessary apology was forthcoming. Next
came apologies to her fellow guests, play being re-
sumed after a stormy interlude. The Cat still
arches her back and does great enlargement of the
tail if you mention nurses in her hearing, but she
got a lesson that, most of her acquaintances are
sure, will do her good.
“ Human Hearts,” with the much-boomed
Leicester as the tall and good-looking hero, was
wedged into the Princess’s crowded dates on
Saturday for a twelve nights’ run. The audience
was shown much of the blackness and ’ardness of
the ’uman ’eart, but the management neglected
to advertise the bellow-drama as a tragedy, and
the hard-hearted house took the scenes as purest
farce. Anyhow, it’s better to laugh than weep.
Bent —the rotund, the newly-returned—was in
the manager’s box. What a reception he got!
Handclapping everywhere, and cheers and '"ells
of laughter from the gods.
A malicious member of the Devil’s Own Infantry
tells of the occasion upon which a legal light of
impeccable habits went forth to buy his better-
half a birthday petticoat. It was a difficult
matter, so he enlisted the aid of an eminent K.O.
who has a roving eye and is credited with a store
of esoteric knowledge. Off went the pair to an
emporium where linrierie fin is obtainable at
astounding prices. The junior Gentleman One
left the question of selection to his experienced
leader, and merely paid. “Would you like a
card enclosed?” asked the sales-dame. “Of
course,” said the experienced counsel. Where-
fore, a heap of scandal, an angry matron, and a
fusillade of jeers.
Sing a dirge for the days when a pampered cat
may flaunt her handsome furs and daintily warm
raiment to the mental discomfort of the utili-
tarian cats whose fur is just plain cat. Spring is
in the next street to us, and it’s easy to scent her
near approach, whereat the Sex experiences true
joy. In the spring, “A young man’s fancy
lightly turns to thoughts of love,” chortled Gov.
Tennyson's papa. In the spring, we women set
about offering man sufficient inducement to think
seriously of matrimony in the autumn. All
winter, we’ve felt the pinched blue tips of our
noses chilling our ardent or amiable souls (one
woman can’t combine both qualities). Even cats
who are girded with nice, decorative wraps, feel
the saddening influence of winter. But the luck-
less ones who paddle round in "serviceable” (hate-
ful word !) winter garments are absolutely crushed
by them. Now that spring is so near that a well-
aimed brick might hit it at any moment, we all
feel better, and unanimously rush the season’s
rag-shows, to copy, bargain, or buy. (I believe
there are even people who take things when the
sales-person isn’t looking.) And the pleasure of
it—to know that becoming frocks can be bought
for any price, from twenty shillings upwards ! And
the possible results of calling attention to one’s
figure in its strictly financial bearings. I really
think that many men have been dragged from
bachelor-grace by the girl’s protestations that
everything that is between her and the outer
world cost less than £2. It gives him a chance
to dream of_ domestic happiness on the cheap.
But, as a general rule, there is no cheap domestic
happiness in practice. It costs all that there is.
The fluffy little rose-clad maiden who makes a
hit in theFrenchy “Lady Madcap” dance is a very
young munmieress and a local chick—Molly
Wilson, granddaughter of dear old Dr. Gray.
Her entry into the “profesh” was made in the
“Children’s Scene” of the “Ghastly Wedding,”
where the benevolent Dutch master of the revels
used to introduce her as “ Madcap.” Molly isn’t
mumming just for the fun of the thing, either;
although the Wilsons were wont to roll in
sheepy wealth, their many thousands of woolly
acres slipped away in desolating drought time.
The squattocracy is subject to change in its
individuality, and the changes are not always for
the better.
The dashing demoiselles who glowed and
glid (or is it “glode” or “glidded”?—any-
how they glade) round Sydney Glaciarium
during its earliest days, are finding the
local refrigerator rather a warm place just
now. Which paradox has evolved from their
inter-State success. The Cats who weren’t en-
listed to make a skating pace for Harbor-siders
unite in looking fiercely upon the luckier lasses,
and infer that it’s shocking form for people with
polite pretensions to do things better than their
peers. There’s nothing more annoying than the
other Cat’s success—even in such a personal
matter as the maintenance of her rectitude in a
slippery situation.
In the story of a recent Melbourne (Toorak dis-
trict) dinner-party there is a striking suggestion
for the third act of a melodrama. The host, not very
long married, sits at the head of the table, faced
by his young wife, radiant and beautiful, in her
most seductive gown. On his left his familiar
bachelor friend keeps the dinner-dressed com-
pany up to the fashionable mark. Everything in
the room is lovely. Host is perceived to be
making rather unnatural efforts to appear
gracious and debonair in his manner to
everybody, especially to his wife. Presently
he raises a glass of champagne on high as he
begs to propose the health of a married lady
and a gentleman of the party. With suitable
dramatic gesture he intimates to his uncomfort-
able guests that a certain husband has been be-
tra-a-a-ay-ed by the wife on whom he had doted,
and that the third party is the trusted
friend of the family. He drinks long life
and happiness to the Guilty Pair, and—this
is where the gallery should thunder ap-
plause—he gives them ten minutes in which
to clear out of the house—together. There
were no thunders of applause for the denouement
of this unrehearsed scene. The unincriminated
guests exchanged glances to the effect that they
were more shocked than surprised, you know.
The Guilty Pair, trembling before the accusing
forefinger of the outraged husband, rose from the
table and did actually clear out within the stipu-
lated ten minutes, or thereabouts. Curtain. What
one misses in this widespread story is the popular
deed of polite violence —the flinging of a glass of
wine at the villain’s shirt-front. Seemingly, he
was allowed to leave the house with his broad
expanse of linen unspotted before the world.
“Rosna” writes from Adelaide :
Lady Le Hunte opened the local Women’s Work Ex-
hibition, on August 17, in the presence of the local
nobility and the common and uncommon herd. It was a
great crowd and a frocky one, and the viee-re o al dame
beamed with pride on the goodly result of her labors.
She and the Lieutenant-Ex. Way have bestowed a load
of enterprise and energy on the movement. The decora-
tions, as far as one could judge in a dense thicket of
heads, were rather gorgeous, but more of that when I
have viewed them minus the thicket. There is a pleasant
drawing-room, wherein some of the committee members
will entertain the Rank and Fashion at receptions and
such all this week ; and there are entries by the furlong
and by the ton in most sections.
The stalwart Besses o’ th’ Barn have been dispersing
sweet noises over Adelaide for a week, and the Salt of
the State has come out from its lair and spread itself
over the front seats. The Besses gave two performances
daily, and committed Hallelujah choruses, “Lohengrin”
overtures, and other strenuous melodies, about a dozen
times each concert, without showing signs of disrepair.
What time they were not blowing upon the brassy horn
the Besses were feted by the populace. The city gave
them a formal welcome, and his Ex. went along to hold
out the hand of fellowship. Then the Lancashire men of
the State held a rally for them, and the atmosphere was
heavy with strange Manchester sounds and with weird
cries from Garstang, Whalley and Bacup, likewise Catteral
and Bay Horse.
We are to have a new mayor in a month or two. He is
a bachelor and wealthy, and Adelaide is glad, because it
sees visions of splendid entertainments such as it knew a
few years ago. A mayor with no encumbrances and many
shekels is apt to spend his official salary on the citizens,
whereas the married mayor buys new frocks for Susan
with the proceeds, and takes little Johnny to the panto-
mime. Probably a sister, or one of the pretty nieces, of
the new Worship will be mayoress.
The H. C. Swans leave for a two years’ jaunt in Europe
this week. The name has been a well-known squatting
one since the very dark and early ages of the State, this
branch of the family having originally squqftted at Angori-
china. Of late years, the Swans have cooked their break-
fast chop at the Semaphore.
The School of Mines boys gave their first ball on the
16th, and a great quantity of youth and beauty went
along to trip a measure in the Brookman Hall. It was a
very hilarious affair, and the horde of young assayers,
engineers, etc., in embryo manipulated it with the
beautiful lavishness of youth, to which the shekel is but
a means to an end and not an end in itself. They even
went out and bought a goodly supply of powder, hairpins
and like mysteries, and stocked the ladies’ cloak-room
therewith. The School’s infant effort seems likely to
grow into an unusually popular annual ball.
We put on our best hat on August 16, and went to the
University sports to see our stalwart brothers, cousins
and uncle's achieve high jumps, Gretna Green races, and
other strenuous things. A few first spring frocks dotted
the landscape. The sky was very blue, and the grass was
very green, and the pleasant Excellencies went to the
thing to hand the trophies and felicitations to the
winners. Altogether we were glad we wore our best hat.
The Victorian singing maiden, Lizzie Newbold, whohaf
been persuaded to stay in Adelaide and warble for some
of its suburbs, committed a concert at the Semaphore
last week. Nightingale Lizzie scored a large-sized sue
cess, and had to respond to many encores.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANKA.

THE SECESSIONISTS.
Despite the high level of work shown by the
Secessionists at their exhibition in Q. V. Market
Chambers, Sydney, it is essentially a two-man
show. In the average annual exhibition the
work of these two men —one unknown and the
other already “arrived” —would dwarf interest
in all the other canvases; but the Secessionists
have got together a surprising amount of good
work, and have been kindly drastic to the in-
competent. The “rotters” are singularly few
and as inconspicuous as they shall be here.
But of the two men. Norman Lindsay shows a
representative collection of his genius—rthe word
is deliberately used—that will bring home to
Australia the fact that it has produced an artist,
who, without leaving its shores, has equalled
the best black-and-white work in the World.
This opinion will be echoed by over-seas art-
critics—by-and-bye. Lindsay’s panel of exhibits
includes the best of his Casanova illustrations—
a continuing delight of execution and artis-
tic vision. He has everything that will lead
him to; even greater heights; his inexhaust-
ible energy is combined with painstaking
industry to, a remarkable degree. An apt
illustration of his thoroughness may be given.
Commissioned to do a drawing of a mob of horses
for The Lone Hand— an illustration for a story—
Lindsay got together a small mob of horses and
made his studies from their grouping. Lindsay’s
work is suffused with the joy of life ; his panel is
a riot of paganism, a saturnalia of a not
too spiritual joy. Nymphs and satyrs laugh
and love with a frank abandon, for these are the
lesser gods of Lindsay’s pagan faith. His work
shocks by if.s. sheer realism ; it delights by its
thoroughness ,apd strength. His talent is the
most pagan thing that has come out of Australia J
it will prpbably prove indigestible to the Jud-
kinses. ;His color work is, at present, but experi-
mental, a little stiff, a little crude. There is more
color in his black-and-white. He sees the glad
riot of this world in monotone.
The other man is J. J. Hilder, a young bank
clerk from somewhere out West, Where Lindsay
fprces admiration by his strength, Hilder cun-
ningly solicits it by his tenderness. His work is
but a beginning —little landscapes, absurdly low-
priced, that will be quickly bought by even the
undiscerning. He has an almost unconscious
vision of beauty, and a color sense rich and
strange. In his most delicate moods you know
him to have his masculine reserves. He may go
far. Meantime there is his monthly salary at the
bank. And of the other's, Lionel Lindsay’s
exhibit is quite astonishing in its variety and
interest. He feels color more keenly than
his brother; his etchings are the real thing,
and some of his pen drawings are exquisite.
We suspect that Lionel Lindsay was born in
Spain. His brother was surely born in Greece.
Julian Ashton has a big show of clever and
sturdy work ; and his influence upon his pupils—
Hilder is a brave example—is everywhere notice-
able. Howard Ashton’s main landscape, if a
little scrubby and a little lacking in interest
in the foreground—the critic will admit
justification for the artist’s treatment —shows
a complete emancipation from the pardon-
able imitativeness of earlier years. This time he
is—and greatly—himself. His landscapes are ail
solidly pleasing. Sid Long has pastorals and
decorations full of charm. “Morning Mists”
shows his fancy at its best, though,
strangely enough for a decoration, it fails
to “carry.” With D. Fry, he has produced
one of the best pictures in the show—a
well-drawn horseman at the head of a mob of
horses looking back into a delightful valley.
D. H. Souter is doggedly and finely decorative.
Harry J. Weston, in addition to much fine
poster work—notably a brilliant Arab effect
(hung in the passage) that would have scorched
every canvas near it, and two humorous pictures
of a series representing a ’possum hunt—has a fine
example of his Australian friezes, a patriotic
department of art that he has made his own. We
commend to the tariff makers a bigger duty upon
the imported Dutch canals that meander
so irritatingly about so many of our best rooms
when the real Australia can be so admirably
pourtrayed. Fred Leist, beside some “slick”
water-colors, shows a commendable portrait,
which, though somewhat lacking in the modelling
of the flesh, and with an awkward arrange-
ment of the dress that dwarfs the model,
makes a fine color harmony. Alice J. Mus-
kett’s pastels are the best of the portrait
work shown. The Secessionists are Curiously
shy of portraits, and (with the exception
of N. Lindsay) of the nude. Arthur Streeton
has some half-dozen characteristic sketches of
Cremorne and Mosman —one of Cremorne being
shown in that blaze of sunlight that only Streeton
and the French impressionists know how to
paint. Sid Smith has some clever pen drawings.
Aliington E. Jeffery has an interesting land-
scape that recalls the stiff, smooth, decorative
treatment of Swedish artists. Madeline Duret
has a good flower-study. All the other
flower-studies are horribly like flowers. Among
the other noticeable woman exhibitors, most
of whom display, combined with much charm of
decoration, the almost universal feminine inability
to draw, are Viola Quaife ; Ruby Winckler, who
manages to display an enviable amount of leg for
an absurdly small price; M. Ethel Roberts, whose
work is altogether delightful; and Muriel G.
Nicholls, whose studies of children are full of a
sypipathetic humor. In the vestibule is an
exhibit, from L. W. Appleby and Arthur H.
Adams, of gum-bichromate photographs, whose
inclusion in an art show is fully justified by the
individuality of the work. This water-color pro-
cess enables the artist to subordinate the mechani-
cal workjif the camera to the artistic intention of
the picture-maker.
The Secessionists, by giving the best show of
recent years, have vindicated their secession.
The Iron Industry.—No. III.
The “Great Natural Industries” and
Another One.
This Australia of ours is a bad shape. It needs
more deep gulfs and coastal indentations. If a
few million cubic yards were scalloped out of its
rim and piled up in ranges inland, it would make
a vastly different Australia of it. We should then
have something more than a circular coast with a
mere fringe of well-watered territory. Still, we
have got to make the best of it as it is; for the
construction of gulfs and Himalayas is beyond the
engineer so far. Recurring droughts must, there-
fore, continue to be a dark feature of our history.
Their influence hitherto has been greater even
than it might seem to be on the surface. They
produce—or, at any rate, are the chief factor in
producing—our booms and our depressions. When
the country booms it naturally plunges into ex-
travagance. Works are planned and methods
adopted beyond the country’s real needs. For pro-
perty, pricesare paid and engagements entered into
which average conditions do not justify. Then
come drought and depression. The pendulum
swings back as far as it flew forward. Forced
realisations and ruin follow in hundreds of cases.
Governments, hard pressed to keep the popula-
tion together, pledge their credit and pay high
prices for large sums of borrowed money to spend
on works which will not pay, and which never
would be undertaken if it were not for the sake of
providing work for those whom drought and
disaster have laid idle. So a permanent burden
of taxation is placed on the people—to pay interest
on works which can’t pay their own interest.
* * * *
To a great extent the bad shape of Australia is
responsible for all this. But we might have done
a great deal more than we have done to neutral-
ise the bad effects. Drought is our natural curse;
yet practically we have only pursued those indus-
tries that are the most liable to be knocked out
by drought. For some extraordinary reason, they
call these, nevertheless, the natural industries.
Heaven only knows why wool-growing and wheat-
raising are “natural”; no wild sheep pursued
Captain Cook to bite him, for the pretty good
reason that there was no sheep here when he
landed ; and, if he wanted wheat, he had to bring
it. There is no earthly reason, then, why these
industries should'be held in veneration, and why
they should be extolled as “natural” and all
others viewed with suspicion as exotics. On the
contrary, since they are both so liable to be badly
droughCstrieken, there is a very good reason why
Australia should not depend so much upon them,
unless it has a fancy for violent and disastrous
fluctuations. The chances are indeed that if it
doesn’t establish other industries —drought-proof
industries—the fluctuations will be more violent
than they have been as the people spread out
further from the fringe.
* * * *
But while the wool and wheat industries have
been grafted on to Australia, and could never
have been evolved out of the country and its own
resources, Captain Cook might have found here
everything necessary for a great iron and steel
industry. Not one single essential thing is lack-
ing. And mostly the essential things are con-
veniently placed in relation to one another. J. B.
Jaquet, then, of the N.S.W. Geological Survey,
in 1900-01 examined the available iron-ore deposits
of the State, and gave particular attention to
those of Carcoar and Cadia, which are only about
16 miles apart. At Carcoar he estimated the ore
in sight at 3,168,000 tons. At Cadia the deposits
are “practically inexhaustible”; J. B. Jaquet
put down 39,000,000 tons to go on with. From
Carcoar to Lithgow the distance is but 90 miles,
with rail all the way; and at Lithgow there are
“abundant supplies of coal, which are now being
delivered at the pit mouth at a cost not exceeding
4s. per ton ” ; there is unlimited water available ;
and “ good limestone can be obtained at Portland,
which is distant by railway from Lithgow only
16 miles.” As to the 90 miles which separate
the coal and the iron, Chief Justice Madden, of
Victoria, came back from America the other
week to urge Australia to develop an iron trade,
and he incidentally mentioned, as one reason
why Australia should enter into the busi-
ness heartily and with good hopes, that
ore is now being carried 1000 miles to the great
works at Pittsburg. According to Jaquet, “the
average distance that the ore has to travel between
the [American] mines and smelting works is about
800 miles.” Of the enormous quantity of iron ore
consumed in Great Britain, it is estimated that
over 30 per cent, is imported from foreign coun-
tries, chiefly Spain ; while of the Swedish ore-
still in great request —the greater portion is
carried from 1000 to 1500 miles before being
smelted. Nature set the three great essentials
together within our own doors : and if the use of
them doesn’t constitute a natural industry, it is
hard to conceive what a natural industry can
possibly be.
*■ * * *
Probably if Australia would get right out of
the foolish habit of regarding growing wool and
wheat as the only natural industries, it would be
more disposed to give a hand to some others.
These others might then at least claim the same
consideration and help that wool and wheat have
had. For wool had, for a start, vastly more help
than is generally supposed. It was provided with
land for nothing, and labor —convict labor—for
practically nothing. The wheat-growing indus-
try was established on those same foundations.
In the matter of stock-raising, the Government
bore the whole expense of shipment after ship-
ment. In 1798 “ the ship Marquis Cornwallis
left Sydney for the Cape of Good Hope, under
contract, to procure cattle for the Government.”
That was only one instance. Those who established
the great stock-raising business were given free
land, convict labor, and even then the Government -
helped them with stock. And after all that, in
1830, twelve years before N. S. Wales got its
Constitution, the pioneers of the great wool and
stock growing business, were so hard pressed that
“a duty was placed on all beef and pork imported
into the colony:” These “great natural indus-
tries” that the'Foreign-traders always! assume
never wanted any “ coddling,” did, as a matter
of fact, require and get more help than even the
most uncompromising Protectionist would now
venture to ask for the iron and steel industry.
And those “great natural industries” of wool
and wheat growing, fostered into activity 100
years ago, didn’t have the violent competition of
organised industrialism to tight their way against.
No message like this, published a week or so ago,
was ever sent about foreign wool and wheat pro-
duction
The Steel Corporation of the United States, believing
that Gary, Indiana, will become a great steel manu-
facturing centre, has voted £9,000,000 for the construction
of works there, making its total investment £24,000,000.
It is the business of that Corporation to keep the
markets it has got and win new ones, and to do
it the vast soulless concern has no compunqtion
about crushing any competition that presents
itself. If it costs half-a-million to rid a market
of a rival, it doesn’t think twice about spending
the half-million; the rival gone, and the lesson
of its own giant power taught, it can settle
down confidently to squeeze out the half-million,
and big interest to boot, from the people over
whom it has thrown the ever-extending net of its
. monopoly. That Corporation has its agents in
Australia, tendering for every big order for steel
and iron goods that Australia lias to offer.
Without high Protection what possible hope has
an infant industry in this country got against it?
What possible hope has the unprotected Aus-
tralian of getting this work of iron and steel
making to do? And so long as it doesn’t make
the iron and steel, the secondary industries of
manufacturing iron and steel goods must in-
evitably be trivial.
* * * *
Further, if Australia wants more outlets for its
workmen, and more outlets for the workmen it
hasn’t yet got, but needs, it also wants more out-
lets for its capital. The banks and other financial
concerns have so far looked chiefly to land mort-
gages for their securities. The growth of funds
in the hands of life-assurance societies is, how-
ever, one of the most striking features of Aus-
tralian life; and for the best mortgages there is
keen competition between these societies and the
banks. One insurance society alone has at its
command £23,000,000, an increase of nearly
£9,000,000 in 10 years. So instead of Australia’s
savings being available for the development of
Australia, the money is being driven away to
help develop some other country. For the Aus-
tralian banks, unable to find any reasonably safe
outlet for the large sums which the last three
good years have brought them, have sent millions
of money to London for temporary investment
there. Meantime, Australia has been importing
the bulk of its requirements in the shape of
manufactured goods. In those three prosperous
years its imports of foreign goods, practically all
manufactures, have amounted to £120,097,079.
Thus, while we have been spending considerable
sums of money in telling people in Europe that
Australia is a good place to emigrate to, we have
been sending millions of money to Europe to
finance the industries which supply us with the
things we need, and to pay in other countries
the wages of the people we say we want here.
So the establishment of manufacturing indus-
tries isn’t only a matter for the workman.
It is a matter also for the man and the
woman who have their much or little to de-
posit at their bank, and who under present condi-
tions can generally get no more than or 3 per
cent, for it, while in the United States, where the
banks have the whole manufacturing require-
ments of the country to finance, the same deposits
command, as a rule, 5 per cent., and frequently
more. Also the establishment of industries is a
matter of personal interest to the insurance
policy-holder, who sees the rate of interest
which his savings can command gradually
shrinking, simply because the industries
which Australian money ought to be financing
are being carried on abroad and financed abroad.
Twenty years ago, to go back no further,
the average rate of interest earned by the A.M.P.
Society was £6 2s. per cent. ; last year it was only
£4 9s. 7d. per cent. And every A.M.P. policy-
holder can be sure of this—that if the Society can’t
earn high interest, it can’t pay high bonuses for
long.
* * * *
The tens of thousands of Australians who are
interested in their banks, building societies, and
other financial concerns are on that account also
interested in another aspect of this same question.
When banks, building societies, and the rest of
them collapsed 14 or 15 years ago, they were
loaded up with many millions of pounds’ worth of
city and suburban properties. By calling up
large amounts of capital, and then writing it off
as lost, they were able to write down these assets
considerably; and at the written-down values
they have been able to pass on large numbers of
properties to others. In thousands of cases de-
positors have taken these properties in lieu of
cash. But thousands of other properties still
remain in the hands of the buildingsocieties, banks,
etc. Where they were country properties they have
mostly been got rid of during the present run of
high prices for grazing and farming land. But
there has been very little movement in city and
suburban properties. “Transactions in real
estate are more numerous, ” the chairman of one
of the Trustee Cos. reported to his shareholders
the other day. “ But the larger demand for real-
property investments is in country districts.
There is not much recovery in the value of town
properties, nor much disposition to invest in
them. This probably arises from the small in-
crease in opportunities of work in our cities,
owing partly to the non-expansion of manufac-
turing enterprise.” That stated the whole fact.
* * * *
These things appeal for effective Protection in a
general way, and as an established policy. But
if Australians aim at being a manufacturing
people, using up in advanced crafts the raw pro-
ducts with which bountiful Fortune has provided
this country, they must build on iron. No country
in the world has become a great industrial hive on
any other foundation. There is a curious argu-
ment that, because iron and steel are the tools
of practically every trade, therefore Australia
should put no duties on iron and steel, or iron and
steel manufactures, lest it should temporarily put
up prices, and put back the other trades. Here
is a frank admission of the fact that iron and steel
are necessary to practically every trade; therefore,
according to this extraordinary theory, which
leaks at every joint, put every Australian industry
at the mercy of the foreigner ! The first principle
in every successful enterprise is—“ Get control of
the necessaries.” A thousand times lately Aus-
tralia has been told to “look to Canada ” —Canada,
the country of High Protection; Canada, the
country which, after a trial to do without it,
resolved that it must have an iron and steel in-
dustry, in spite of the fact that for its smelting-
fuel it had to depend upon charcoal or imported
anthracite; Canada, the country whose Finance
Minister (Tupper) declared in 1887 that the Pro-
tection of the iron industry was “ more important
than all the others”—a statement which can be
found in Professor Fuchs’ “ Trade Policy of Great
Britain,” published in 1905 by MacMillan and
Co. In the same interesting survey, those
Foreign-traders who cheerfully assume that
Great Britain had no Protective duties to build
up its manufacturing greatness may profitably
read that “in 1824 the Protective duties on the
most important manufactures were still between
50 and 80 per cent.” ; while those who reckon
themselves good Protectionists, yet stand in some
awe of 20 per cent, duties, may also profitably
read that, under the “great tariff reform” of
Peel, Freetrader and Corn Law Reformer (1842),
there was “ a systematic reduction of the duties
on wholly manufactured articles to a maximum
of 20 per cent. The duties on silk goods alone
remained unchanged at from 25 to 40 per cent,,
ad valorem , as it was contended that this branch
of industry still needed Protection.”

THE LAST TALLY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
His hard face now is calm and pale,
His lips with a smile are riven ;
Tho’ his body lies in the smellful hold,
His soul is aloft in Heaven.
The Tally Book is laid aside,
The pencil now is blunted ;
The hides come tumbling in the hold,
And none of them are counted.
But let the hides smell on—
They’ll not disturb his rest.
He’s shipped a pair of snow-white wings,
And a gold crown’s on his crest.
But he’s very, very lonely,
Tho’ he is among the Blest;
For he is the only Mate there,
And it makes him sore apprest.
For he sometimes gazes downwards,
And among the brimstone blazes
He sees the Mates of other days
In their appointed places.
And he sees old Skippers roasting
In the hottest spots of Hell
And he would almost pity them
Did he not know them too well.

OR the next twelve
months this page
will be devoted to
telling the people
facts about their
coastal marine, and
the affairs of the Five Companies whose
flags are emblazoned hereon, and whose
fleets make up the greater bulk of that
coastal marine. Very few Australians real-
ise how large a part the steamers of those
Five Companies play in the affairs of their
country. Apart from their value as employers of
labor, spending an enormous sum in wages,
repairs, and for provisions, and so forth, it is the
fleets of the Five Companies that make Australia
one country, and reader the Commonwealth a possi-
bility by linking up the scattered settlements of the
continent into one national whole. It is only when
one remembers that the coastline of Australia
measures 7750 miles (which, if drawn straight, would
reach from Sydney to San Francisco and nearly a
thousand miles beyond it) that the mind begins to
realise what a scattered, wide-spread place Australia
is. Our territories are enormous, and without the
fleets of the Five Companies, or those of some other
companies, the continent would be. split up into a
whole series of small, and comparatively poor, settle-
ments. The fleets of the Five Companies bring them
all into close proximity, and make nationhood
possible. These companies deserve well of Aus-
tralians, and it is to be regretted that instead of rush-
ing off to Europe and the United States to see things,
leisured Australians do not spend more time, explor-
ing the wonders of their own splendid country. It is
well worth seeing.
*32?
Steamers in Australia date back to March 31,
1831, when Henry G. Smith, a London director of
the Commercial Bank of Sydney, placed on
Parramatta River the Surprise. This boat was
afterwards sent to Tasmania. On May 16th of
the same year there steamed into Port Jackson
the Sophia Jane, from England. This
pioneer of the ocean liners was only
256 tons and 50-h.p. There are motor
yachts building in
Australia to-day
which are of
larger dimen-
sions. Lieu-
tenant
Biddulph,
of the
Royal
Navy,
who was
part
owner,
brought it out, and the ship was afterwards placed on
the Newcastle-Sydney run, and thus became the
pioneer of the great fleet of coasting steamers
which cruise round Australia to-day. Curiously
enough, the new ideas met with much oppo-
sition at first, many people refusing to commit
themselves and lives to steamers driven by boilers
and furnaces. However, that soon passed, and on
October 22nd, of the same year, the first locally-
built steamer, William IV., was launched at Williams
River by a builder named J. H. Grose. By J anuary
26th, 1842, steam communication was opened between
Sydney and Brisbane, and the eastern coast of Aus-
tralia was properly linked up by regular steamer ser-
vices. Things have grown since then : at the end of
1901 there were registered in Australia 943 steamers
of 203,541 tonnage.
An interesting and reassuring fact to travellers is
that there are less Wrecks on the Australian coast than
any other part of the world. And what wrecks there
are are chiefly those of oversea steamers and sailing-
ships which get piled up about the entrance to New-
castle. To hear of an Australian coasting steamer
getting wrecked is a rare thing indeed. And ever
since the first steamers began to plough Australian
waters they have been singularly fortunate in this
respect. Also travellers who have been round the
world in both directions have affirmed over and
over again in books, newspapers and magazines,
that the comfort, cuisine, navigation, and general
management of (;he Australian coastal marine are
ahead of those of any other part of the world. This, of
course, is easily explained. No other country uti-
lises its coastal marine to the same extent that
Australia relies on the ships of the Five Companies.
In Australia, the movements are north and south,
east and west, and in every case the road lies over
the waves. It is always around the coast. In the
United States the movement is mostly east and
west—across the country, and the Yankee is
a railroad man, not a sailor. The same
applies in a modified degree to the British Isles.
The man who wants to travel from London to
'Edinburgh takes the Flying Scotchman, or some
Other celebrated train. With the exception of the
traffic to Ireland and across the English Channel the
Englishman travels in fast trains, which reduce
distance by getting the inside running of ships. But
in Australia the man. who would go west must go by
steamer, and the man who starts from Melbourne for
Brisbane or any other part of Queensland invariably
takes a boat. It is more comfortable, and is, in many
cases, the only way. And this natural condition has
caused the directors of the Five Companies to study
passenger traffic as it is studied nowhere else in the
world. Their ships are the main arteries of national
trade—in other countries the ships are the second
line. Therefore, the Australian gets better value for
his money than any other coastal traveller in the
world.
If Australians knew their own country better,
they would spend more time exploring its beauties.
The object of this page is to tell them something
fresh about it every fortnight, with the object of
inducing them to travel in the fleets of the Five
Companies and see what their heritage is like. It
will contain things they ought to know, and
occasionally give accounts of the scenes of
beauty that may be viewed from the decks of
the associated fleets. We have as interesting a
country as any race in the world. The pity is that
we don’t, as a people, know more about it. Geologi-
cally, parts of it are among the oldest fragments of
the earth’s surface above water, and can supply the
tourist with interesting observations, obtainable in
no other part the world. To the bulk of the
people living in it Australia is .still the Land of
Mystery that it was before the coming of the English.
Henry VIII. sat on the throne of England when
some little Dago craft came up against some part of
the north or nor’-western coast, and returning
spread the news that the mysterious southern land
of which Marco Polo, the Venetian traveller, 200
years before, told Rusticiano of Pisa he had heard of
while in China, and which was described as a great
round island, larger than even the Flowery Land
itself, which lay beyond Java. It must be remembered
that the Chinese traded regularly to Java. Also, the
old Phoenicians, who sailed and paddled down the Red
Sea, away along the. shores of Asia till the Malay
Peninsula blocked further progress, had heard of it,
and occasionally made maps, in which its northern
fringe was represented as balanced accurately on the
back of a tortoise, or some similar creature.
That northern and nor’-western coast-line is
still almost entirely unknown. The Malay prahu
still haunts its creeks and navigable rivers, and
it is whispered that Chinese junks,
those weird crafts of. bamboo fitted
with great latyen l sails, are running
passenger lines to a terminus
somewhere about the English
Company Isles. For a fas-
cinating trip, that along the
Queensland coast, “ where
the coral grows and the
pearls lie deep,” is
unsurpassable. It
can be taken at
verylittlecost, and
on one of the float-
ing hotels of the
Five Companies
can be enjoyed
with all the aids
of civilisation.
The sea-kings of
old were not in it
with the Aus-
tralian tourist of
to-day.

Their Symptoms and Cure.

intestinal worms. Indeed many adults are
troubled with this complaint, but fail to realise
the fact. The symptoms of this trouble are
various, but are usually very pronounced.
Constant irritation of the nostrils and the biting
of the finger nails are sure signs of the presence of
worms. Invariably the sleep is greatly disturbed
with grinding of the teeth, and an excessive flow
of saliva. Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worms
Pellets are a sure death to worms, and a perfectly
safe remedy for children and adults.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Ideal weather, twenty odd thousand spectators,
ahd a gate worth, in round figures, £lOOO, greeted Pro-
fessional Football’s first appearance in Sydney. The
Maorilanders were a fine-looking set of men, much
superior in appearance, decency and honesty of play to
the amateur All Blacks. There was no elbow work in the
line-out, the scrums were managed with absolute fairness,
and the off-side tactics which disgraced the amateurs were
conspicuous by their absence. The game was a fair lest
Cf Rugby football, and not a struggle to score points by
any and every means whatever. The result was that the
professional team made a good impression on spectators
and opponents alike.
The match itself was a first-class exhibition,
full of interest from start to finish. Both teams were
suffering from lack of combination, due to not having
plkyed together, but as the game went on they improved
greatly in this respect. The Maorilanders gave some
splendid exhibitions of dribbling, and the passing charges
of the N.S.W. three-quarter line were fine. Messenger
eclipsed himself ; he took marks right off the very toes of
the All Blacks, turning their splendid dribbling rushes to
a source of danger, and kicked from the half-way line only
to miss the bar by inches. He seemed to anticipate
the movements of the Blacks in a manner that
suggested thought reading, and when what seemed the
unexpected came off, Messenger was in the right place to
nip it in the bud. Graves, Mable, and Fry, other Blues,
also made fine passing charges. The weak spot in the
home team was the half-back line ; its component parts
frequently forgot that they were there only to feed the
three-quarter line, and tried to break away themselves.
The packs were about equal, although that of the Blacks
had the advantage of strength. The Maoriland full-back,
Turtill, is a gem of the first water, and a man who doesn’t
make mistakes. His kicking is quick as lightning, and
as cool as the proposal that N.S.W. should select the
Australian capital. The N.S.W. full back, Hedley, is also
excellent, but he is not the imposing monster that
Turtill is.
The game commenced with a great struggle
midfield, the players swaying from one side of the ground
to the other, neither making any progress till Messenger
secured, charged, and kicked to the M.L. 25. The
Blacks carried it into N.S.W. territory, and failed at a
field goal. Messenger, rushing it back, took a drop at
goal, and compelled the Blacks to force. A few minutes
later they were again compelled to force. Another drib-
bling rush put the Blues on the defensive, and a shot at
a field goal compelled N.S.W. to force. A series of splen-
did passing charges by both sides filled the spectators
with excitement, which boiled over when Messenger, a
few feet from half-way, barely missed a penalty goal.
Instead of forcing, the Blacks charged and kicked, and
the Blue who took it ran instead of marking, and the ball
being fumbled, the Blacks got the first chance. They
took it, and the score was M.L. three ; t’others nil.
Shortly afterwards Messenger took another shot at
goal from right on the half-way line, and missed
by inches. It was an amazing kick ! The Blacks
stopped a Blue rush in their own territory, and wrench-
ing the ball away carried it right down the field over the
line, making the score six to nil. The Blues attacked
again, and a great struggle was in progress on the M.L.
25 when Messenger suddenly secured the ball, and burst-
ing,through scored a try, which he failed to convert on
account of the angle. When the whistle sounded the
Blues were in full sail for the line. The second half
opened with a struggle on the M.L. 25 line, and Messen-
ger dashed away, but finding himself unsupported kicked
for a field goal. M. L. saved by forcing. A passing ch arge
and a line kick by Messenger were followed up with line
kicking, till Messenger took a mark. The Blacks blocked
his kick, but Messenger stopped their rush by coolly
kicking out while his guernsey was being torn off his
back. As the Blacks came away from the line-out he
again marked, but the angle was too great and the shot
at goal failed. The Blacks dribbled right through the
Waters, but were stopped inside the 25. Here bad play
gave them a mark right in front of goal, and the
score became nine to three. Messenger ran to the
M.L. 25, and centred, and fell on Todd before he
could return, and out of the loose Gann scored :
Messenger converted, and lifted the N.S.W. score to eight
to nine. The excitement became intense. A vigorous
dribbling charge by the Maorilanders was only momen-
tarily blocked in N.S.W. 25, but the Blue three-quarter
failed to gather in his opportunity, and the Maorilanders
gob across and won by 12 to eight.
Not the least creditable part of the match was
the refereeing. Mr. Boss acted right up to his name.
When unsatisfied with the way the ball was pub into the
scrum, although writer could see nothing wrong, he put
it in himself. As a referee there are points about Mr.
Boss. If the professionals continue to play as they did
on Saturday, the Rugby Union is right up against some-
thing very formidable. As before mentioned, the takings
were, in round figures, £lOOO. The critical time will come
with the division of the money between the conflicting
interests. If that can be carried out without causing
ructions, the Union may have to choose between cutting
loose from the English body and going up the spout.
*.* * *
That the N.S.W. so-called professional football
movement was more or less justifiable is proved by the
Union’s proposal, made before the League has properly
started, to liberalise the conditions under which amateurs
play. But, all the same, it is still proposed to fall hea\4ly
on the men who, by their secession, have brought about
the admittedly necessary reform. It is just as though an
employer, when his men had gone on strike, said : “ Yes,
I’ll pay higher wages, and make the conditions better all
round, but I’ll employ blacklegs ; the men who have
brought this reform about shall never work for me again,
nor have any share in the benefits they have created.”
Either the movement is justifiable, or it is not. If it is,
the Union should set its house in order, and declare an
amnesty. If it isn’t, then the Union should stand firm,
and make no “concessions.” This paper’s opinion is
that the movement is justifiable, only it doesn’t like the
men who have started it—Victor Trumper, one of the
cricket crowd that tried to euchre the Board of Control,
and assist the Melbourne Cricket Club to boss the game ;
Kelly, of the same gang, and others.
* ■* m * ,
The old stock phrases of the “ Great Freetrade
and Liberal Party” might have been used in their entirety
to describe the upheaval of the Eastern Suburbs footballers
on Saturday. Theclub that seemed to be an absolute wreck,
owing to the defections to the pros., pulled itself together
in a marvellous manner, made a recovery, and proved
that the great natural resources of this institution were
beyond the wildest dreams of its admirers. Certainly
they were beyond the dreams of the unfortunate
Western Suburbs, on whom the recoverers proved
their mettle by 16 points to nil. Starting against
the wind the Easterns played a solidly defensive game
the whole of the first half, which was scoreless, and in
the second half, with the wind behind them, they stormed
the Western line repeatedly, scoring two goals and two
unconverted tries. Next week East will meet Glebe at
Sydney Cricket Ground in the semi-final, and it will pro-
bably be a wildly exciting struggle. University defeated
North Sydney by 21 points to 17. At the start
it looked as though the hillmen were going to get
the father and mother of a hiding, but they livened
up amazingly, though they had to play one man short
owing to Gardiner having broken a finger. He was full
of sand, and wanted to play on, but his captain ordered
him off the field. He left reluctantly. Glebe-Sydney
match was a struggle between the opposing forwards,
which resulted in a victory for the butchers by nine
points to three, while South Sydney and Newtown played
a draw with 11 points each.
The result is that the beaten clubs and Newtown
drop out of the competition. Newtown played its last
chance on Saturday, and failed to improve its position.
The semi-final takes place on Saturday, when Eastern
Suburbs will meet Glebe at the Cricket Ground, and
South Sydney and University will battle at University
Oval. The two winners will play off on Saturday week.
On points Glebe is leading with a competition score of 20
points, and University and Eastern Suburbs are together
in the second rank with 19 points each. South Sydney is
fourth with 16.
* * » »
N.S.W. Soccer ’bailers are now struggling for
the Gardiner Cup, and, owing to brevity of time, the com-
petition is being run on sudden death lines. The team
that goes down is blown out for ever, as Omar Khayyam
would say. An unfortunate country club —Weston, of
the Maitland District—had the misfortune to meet the
invincible Pyrmont; yet it put up an amazing
struggle. So strenuously did it fight for possession
of the ball that the premier club only won by
two goals to nil. ,Navy met Broadmeadow, and went
under by three to nil. The remaining match of the
day was that between Balmain and Rozelle, at Epping
(Sydney). It was a great contest, and consisted of
a series of charges and counter charges, with every man
on the field going at top speed. But the goal-keepers on
both sides were too solid, and after a heavy struggle the
whistle sounded on a scoreless draw. These two clubs
will meet again next Saturday, as one or the other has to
be killed in the first round.
* * * *
Anyone with a capacity for explaining the
unaccountable and accounting for the inexplicable could
have got a day’s work in Melbourne after Saturday’s
League matches were decided. It was the commence-
ment of the second round, and each of four clubs hap-
pened to meet one two steps further down the premier-
ship ladder. Each score, at the finish, gave a decided
advantage to one team, yet in every case that team was
the one that ought on “form” to have been walloped.
Carlton met South, and up to three-quarter time held a
steady lead of six points. Immediately on resuming it
scored another goal and began to shake hands with it-
self, but the tireless Southerners settled down to a finish
that, completely rattled the Blue-and-Whites, and in 20
minutes piled on five goals two behinds while the others
could add but threebehinds. This left “Skinner’s hire-
ling’s ” 17 points to the good. The ’Wood-Saints discus-
sion was expected to be so one-sided that scarcely 12,000
people turned up to barrack. It was one-sided, but
the unexpected side was on top at the finish—the Mag-
pies having apparently forgotten all they knew about the
game during the week, while the Saints had recovered
the form which, in the early part of the season, made
them the most talked-about team of all the foot-
ball herd. The scores (Saints, 8-19 ; ’Wood, 3-8) are more
unaccountable than excusable. Melbourne, which met
Essendon, and Geelong, which encountered ’Roy, had
still chances of semi-final honors, could they manage to
win ; but they couldn’t. Now their opponents had no
chances of getting into the finals, in any case, and their
wins could do them no real good, and they were just
winning out of cussedness, and the two superior teams, as
they saw their hopes being blasted, got very, very angry,
and played accordingly. Essendon won by 71 points to
56, and ’Roy by 88 to 38. The latter discussion, being
supervised by a determined umpire, was kept within
bounds; but the Essendon-Melbourne disturbance
speedily became a mere rough-and-tumble, and the game
was stopped several times till individual players had
rebuked each other on the nose. Once the matches were
stopped a full five minutes while half the players en-
gaged in a willing, general scrap. Weak umpiring was
also responsible for a couple of chic little mills at the
’Wood-Saints disturbance. So incompetent has League
football umpiring become that there is now a demand
that Crapp shall be brought over from “ the West ”
specially to umpiie the final matches, but the League is
hardly likely to notice that request. Possibly, if that
body increased the central umpire’s fee and tested new
men more freely, it might presently strike a few who
understand the game and care not a cent for any player,
barracker or club in the State. But a competent umpire
is much harder to find than a star ’bailer.
In the Association Melbourne’s chief interest
centred in Brunswick, which met the once invincible
Essendon (A) team and sent it home cogitating deeply,
the skill of the half-dozen ex-League stars being of no
avail against the systematic play which the brickmakers
have learned from their ex-Collingwood coach. The awful
North managed to score three points more than Preston,
which victory removes its name from the bottom of the
list ; though, as there are only its Saturday’s victims be-
tween it and the abyss, the circumstance is nothing to brag
about. The star clubs, Williamstown and West, collided,
with moderately disastrous results to the latter—s-11 to
5-6 being the tally. West, premier team last year, is hereby
adjured to hump itself, or it will assuredly retire from
business when the semi-final draw is made three weeks
hence. Richmond, having won nine matches in succes-
sion, begins to fancy itself—and very nearly fell in at
Port Melbourne. “ Ports ” have had one of their best
players disqualified for life, and are a very angry lot just
now, and lay themselves out to put the equivalent of the
beginning of wisdom into all and sundry. As one
of the team, in the course of a “diversion”
during Saturday’s game, biffed a boundary umpire
on the countenance, he may possibly follow his
club mate into retirement. If anyone wants to
wallow in gore and language he should pervade the
Port ground next Saturday week, when Essendon (A)
comes along to argue the point. Essendon was respon-
sible for the above-mentioned player’s disqualification,
and the Port barrackers haven’t forgotten the circum-
stance. Footscray, which still has a fragile chance for
the semi-finals, and is piling up points to that end, made
a painful exhibition of Prahran, with a score of 101 points
to 26. The struggles of the moderately good teams to do
great deeds when said deeds are of little avail are a be-
lated and sorrowful thing.
* * * *
“ Nulla ” : The spirit firm that offered a £5 prize
for every field goal kicked in the recent Australia-
Maoriland match didn’t stand to pay out much, for goal-
kicking, nowadays, is almost as the arts of ancient
Damascus. T’other Saturday, for instance, out of a total
of 32 tries registered in Sydney first-grade Rugby only
seven were converted, and of those no less than four
were booted by the fleet-hoofed Messenger. As for the
dropped goal—that brilliant flash which of old often
dragged a match out of the mire—well, it is about as
scarce as an honest surplus. For the whole of the first-
class matches in Sydney this season field goals can be
almost counted on one’s thumbs, and, in fact, so far as
Australia is concerned, Rugby football might almost be
defined as a game of ball in which the feet don’t take a
hand.
* *■ * +
Maoriland is taking sculler Webb’s champion-
ship with desperate seriousness. His native Wanganui
has started a “ national subscription ” to buy him a free-
hold residence, and £l6O was put up at the initial meet-
ing. Which is a solid return for the reflected glory that
is Wanganui’s. When the Canadian runner Shaddock
or Maddock or Haddock won the big event at the Greek
Olympic Games, a couple of years ago, his native city,
Hamilton, presented him with £lOOO, a freehold house, and
a permanent Customs billet, and asked him, “ for the per-
manent glory of his town,” to race no more. He didn’t,
so the town owns an unbeaten champion, and will keep
his bones for all time.
* * * *
N.S.W. Pioneer Motor Boat Club is not show-
ing the usual activity of the pioneer, and badly wants
shaking up. Practically only one function has been
decently organised and carried out, and that was the
opening of the club’s first season at Clifton Gardens, ’way
back at the beginning of last summer. That was a sight
worth going far to see, but it has never been repeated,
and there are no indications that it will. Yet there are
big possibilities ahead of a motor boat club. The present
organisation wants developing both as regards social club
outings and control of racing. Among the first things
that should be done is the adoption of the U.S.A.
formula for defining horse-power of racing engines. .The
horse-power developed under the rules of the British
Royal Automobile Club, which have been adopted by the
U.S.A. manufacturers and racing authorities, and that at
which engines are listed by their makers, are very dif-
ferent. Just at present the N.S.W. club contains some
of the fastest boats in the world —in proportion to
horse-power—if the power announced to the world is
correct.
* >- * *
So far, there is no word from Mort’s Dock re
the building of a fast Australian boat to hold the
championship of the Southern Ocean and Australasia.
This splendidly-equipped establishment certainly ought
to build a boat that would sweep Sydney Harbor. It is the
success of its fast cars and its fast boats that has made
the mighty Napier Co. Nobody hears a word of the
Napier Co. in advertisements, but not a sporting paper
can one pick up without seeing in the news columns the
name Napier. Now it is a Napier boat that, with a
damaged shaft, has captured the Pearl of the Mediter-
ranean, and then it is a Napier six-cylinder car that has
run 65 miles an hour for 24 hours without pause or
slackening. And there is no reason why it should not be
in all the Australasian papers that the Mort’s Dock boat,
fitted with Invincible engines, swept the board at the
N.S.W. Motor Boat Club’s meeting on such and such a
date, or that the Mort’s Dock boat, fitted with Invincible
engines, beat the best foreign-engined boats at some
European carnival. There is no earthly reason why
Mort’s Dock shouldn’t argue the point in the Mediter-
ranean as well as any other concern. Practically there
would be no Invincible engines but for the fact that
Relph’s splendid boat made an Australian industry pos-
sible. It is the best sort of advertisement, and turns
every man who takes an interest in the sport into an
active and whole-souled canvasser. It is the duty of the
great Dock to build that boat—a duty it owes to itself
and the nation.
m * #
A field of 95 faced the starter at Bathurst for
the long ride of 133£ miles to Sydney, promoted by the
Wallace-Continental Rubber Co. The event was under
the control of the N.S.W. League of Wheelmen. The
first man left Bathurst at 6.55 a.m., and by 8 o’clock the
scratch men had been sent off. The whole pack struck
trouble six miles outside Sunny Corner, some fiend or
idiot having amused himself by sowing tacks along the
road. The way the riders gathered in those tacks was
just marvellous. The road is a hilly one, and the
men made the pace a flyer from the start,
so much so that those tacks at Sunny Corner were an
absolute godsend to some exhausted riders, whose chances
were nil. But about 30 men were deprived of a real
chance of winning, through this outrage. At 8.52 a.m.
H. L. Orr tore through Sunny Corner like a whirlwind-
tacks and all —and behind streamed a small army of
gasping, sweating riders. At Mt. Victoria the first sign
of their coming was Tumbers, with his head down, and
his legs toiling furiously. And always close behind the
leader, whoever the leader might be, the brothers Aplin
split through the atmosphere. At Katoomba there was
no defined leader, but a bunch of three struggling
to get a break on the field, with the brothers
Aplin running close behind. At Parramatta Wal-
cott was 50yds. in front, with a pained expression on
his face that indicated he was worried by the close
proximity of the brothers Aplin. Six miles from the
finish the frequently-mentioned brothers Aplin drew up
and went like wind." Walcott responded, and a desperate
race ensued, and 4000 people roared with excitement as,
150yds. from home, Walcott and the two brothers Aplin
(who keep coming into this story even after the
repetition of their names has become a weariness) fought
out the finish. It looked like being a dead heat of
three. But a few yards from the tape one brother
Aplin (F.) lifted his machine, and came away. The
other brother Aplin (A.) followed, and Walcott who
was exhausted by having practically made the pace for
many weary miles, could not respond. They finished in
that' order, with inches between the brothers Aplin, and
Walcott only half a wheel behind the second. F. Aplin
won £5O first prize, £3 3s. for second fastest time, and £7
for using Continental tyres ; total £6O 3s. A. Aplin
raked in a bicycle and £7 ss. in-eash, while plucky Wal-
cott won only a measly bike for third place. Which was
hard luck for Walcott.
Of course the real champion in a contest of this
sort is the man who covers the distance in the shortest
actual riding time. This proud position was awarded to
G. Horder, whose time worked out almost as fast as that
of a train. He was on scratch, and arrived fourth, having
done the journey in 7hr. 30min. Ofsee. The train, when
it runs to time, takes 6hr. 27min. However, Mon-
day brought a protest against Horder in his capacity
as the swiftest flyer, the plaintiff being H. Lundie,
who finished fifth. He alleged that Horder was paced by
F. S. Hagney from Penrith to Burwood, and declared he
had several witnesses to prove his allegation. The affair
was to have been decided on Tuesday night, after this
paper had gone to press. The man who sowed the tacks
rather overdid it; he bought such a quantity as to make
it almost impossible to miss tracing him, and when the
League does run him to earth he is going to strike serious
trouble.

This is the day of the female. A married lady
named Riley, who drove a 20 h.p. Belsize (shod with
Dunlop tyre’s), has wbn the Scottish Reliability Trial, the
crack annual motor event of Great Britain. Besides
scoring the most points, the lady also carried off the
special gold medal for the best all-round performance.
The course was 747 miles, and the field 96 cars. This is
the first occasion on which a lady has taken the glory and
the whoop and the profits.

The first meeting of the Southland Coursing
Club at Invercargill (M.L.) on the 7th and Bth of the
present month afforded some cheerful sport—cheerful
from the standpoint of the brute who calls himself a
coursing enthusiast. Twelve hundred people rolled up
and had their fill of butchery. The Post of Wellington
reported : “ The hares did not know the escapes, and it
was a veritable slaughter, only 20 hares being- left. It is
probable that the Open Stakes will be abandoned.” The
Times said : “ Kills were too frequent.. .. and the supply
of hares ran out, leaving both events unfinished. The
stakes were consequently divided amongst the owners of
dogs still left in.” Why not put it briefly: “The stakes
were divided among the hogs left in.”
There’s a growing impression that three months’
hard without the option would be a fair thing for some
of the ruffians who managed to get into the Canadian
lacrosse team. Possibly, so long as a man can play
lacrosse, nothing else matters in Canada. The Australian
authorities are understood to have made a careful note of
certain names with a view to requesting the governing
body in Canada to exclude them from any team that may
come here in the future. Our men went on the field to
play lacrosse, and not to indulge in free fights and be
kicked, tripped, prodded and bashed with the crosses. If
the visitors wanted fight, they should have said so before
going on the field, and then they could have been accom-
modated, but they ought in common fairness to let the
Australians know'exactly what they are looking for.
The prediction in last issue of a better day
coming: for Dyed Garments was fulfilled on Saturday,
when the son of Malvolio won the August Handicap at
Flemington in good style. If Dyed Garments were an
easily-trained animal his present “ form ” wi uld give him
an excellent show to take one or other of the Cups, but
his periods of fitness are not frequent or long-sustained,
and he is likely to be in a tired mood when the big events
come due. A less-expected victory last Saturday was
that of Booran. The champion second-class three-year-
old of last season was probably riper than his people
thought him. Starting at 10 to 1, he scored a brilliant
win from perhaps half-a-dozen triers. This is a time of
the year when the market price of some of the starters
should be 1000 to one, and the stewards of the V.R.C. are
mostly addicted to winking the other eye after every
race. They must have opened both eyes when Booran
flashed in.

* * * *
Tim Hegarty, long featherweight champion of
Australia, and still unbeaten at 9st., came into the game
again at Melbourne pavilion the other night to demon-
strate emphatically that youth will have its way. Frank
Thorn, his opponent, had youth and cleverness. Hegarty
had age and ability, but his energies were not equal to a
20-round engagement, and after 10 rounds, in which he
showed fine style, particularly in evasion, he broke up.
Although he stood out the time it was mainly through
hanging desperately to Thorn’s neck for two minutes out
of every three. Tim certainly went into the ring this
trip with the intention of rehabilitating himself, but he
was up against it. Thorn was an easy winner on points.

“Bill” Squires writes that “there is nothing
much to say about the fight. . . . Burns landed on me
first, and I never got over it till he hit me right out. It
was a disappointing go. I have gone a bit off, and no
doubt the other man is under-rated.” He was, especially
by William, who, according to Barney Wrenolds, “ en-
tered the ring confident of success, looked on Burns with
supreme contempt, charged him, missed him, charged
and missed again, charged again, received a right,
and fell dazed and helpless. He would not stay down,
but rose, rushed, and threw the money in the fire.”
At latest Squires was “ going north ” to belt something
that wouldn’t hit back—timber, to wit. Which, though
useful work, isn’t quite up to Squires’ size in belting jobs.
Burns notwithstanding.
mm * *
The most frantic fight ever seen in Melbourne
under alleged Marquis Queensberry rules was that of
“ Props ” Reader, of Sydney, versus Tom Fennessey, of
Melbourne. These two slugging heavies know the game
well, and realise what is what and what what is, but
finding themselves under a referee who, to put it gently,
had lost his presence of mind, they turned the “glove
contest ” into a Kilkenny-cat fight. Referee and fighters
were mixed up inextricably together, rolling over and
over, both men pounding the referee under the impres-
sion it was the other fellow that was taking it all.
On one occasion Reader threw Fennessey, and after
bumping him gently on the floor, turned him over on his
back, and sat on his chest while he took a breather and
the ovations of the crowd. Both men were down as
often as they were up, if not oftener. The wear and tear
told most on Reader, who, finding his strength gone,
hung on in spite of all orders to break away. Whereupon
the referee, suddenly remembering who he was, declared
Fennessey the winner. The gintleman with the French
name will now be matched with Mr. Joe Cullen.

Ex-Maorilander Dick Arnst looms big in the
sculling world. Within the last couple of months he has
challenged about six of the alleged cracks, and one and
all have side-stepped him. So now Richard has
announced that he will reach for the scalp of the winner
of the Webb-Tressider match, and go straight for the
championship of the world without any preliminaries.

N.S.W. has finished its struggle with unregis-
tered bike racing, and fairly stamped the thing out; but
in Victoria the fight is only just beginning. Quite a
number of unregistered meetings are held in Bent’s
State, and, what is more, they pay the promoters well.
Just at present the Sale-Prospect £l5O Handicap is being
announced.as an unregistered event, and the League is
beginning to wonder how to go about stopping the thing.
As good a way as any would be to hire Mclntosh for six
months. .
