Died at Maryborough (Q.), on August 3,
aged 85, John Walker, who, in conjunction with
James Wood, Thomas Braddock, and W. F.
Harrington, established the firm of John Walker
and Cos., now Walkers, Limited. In its day
it was a fine concern, and may be again;
but under the recent tariff it has had such a bad
time that it was nearly worth while rechristening
it Limpers, Ltd.
George Black has been nominated by the
P.L.L. to contest Carruthers’ electorate, of St.
George, at the N.S.W. State election. Probably
he won’t win, but he should keep Carruthers in
the neighborhood of his own cavern during the
next few weeks, for which he will have the
gratitude of every section of the State —always
barring St. George. And, this being a world
much given to the unexpected, he may win, for
George Black is a political fighter of no mean
tonnage.
“ Wingen ” Abbott, president of the
Pastoralists’ Union, has been stirred up to his
Old letter-writing habit by the Australian Arbitra-
tion Court’s award in the shearers’ case. “A 20
per cent, increase ! ” he moans—“ it is terrible.”
Not that the pastoralists can’t afford it—they
can. They could afford, he admits, anything up
to 25 per cent. But it ought to have been
gradual; it ought to have been “spread over a
series of years”; “Justice O’Connor was not
justified in putting up the wages rate 20 per cent,
in one jump without first trying what a less
revolutionary measure would effect.” In fact,
nobody should do the right thing all at once.
Now, “a bad year may necessitate a very large
reduction in the rate ” ; and “Will such a state of
things be conducive to peace?” asks Wingen,
with pains in his pen. No; alas, no! On the
same principle “Wingen” Abbott is no doubt
selling his wool at the old rate of sd. instead of
the new Is. Id. rate. He sees the danger of col-
lecting the extra Bd.; if he collected this
year he mightn’t be able to get it next year, and.
that might lead to “Wingen” and the buyer
having words. So he only collects the old sd. Or,
perhaps, s|d. —on the principle that the thing
ought io be done gradually.
“Lone Hand” holdeth forth :
“ Vigil,” in his letter (B. 8/8/’O7), is excitedly inaccurate
in some of his statements. It was only a section—and a
very small section at that—of the Melbourne Patent
Attorneys, who complained of the administration of the
Commisssioner of Patents. The majority—and a large
majority at that—of the Melbourne attorneys recognise
that the Commissioner has had an extremely difficult
task in the organisation of the Patents, Designs and Trade
Marks Department, and in the administration of the re-
spective Acts of Parliament. This majority, being
reasonable men, consider that the administration of such
Acts—although not perfect—has been performed by the
•Commissioner as well, satisfactorily and conscientiously
as mortal man could have done. Another point: Con-
sidering that on an average between forty and fifty
patent applications are lodged every week ori inventions
•of electrical, metallurgical, agricultural, and other
widely different natures, “Vigil’s ” suggestion of having
owe expert to frame the inventors’ specifications is about
as sensible as most otherof his remarks. Scores of experts,
draughtsmen and typists, together with agents and
staffs in other countries, would have to be appointed for
the purpose of protecting the “ inventions ” of all the
hare-brained individuals who come along. If “ Vigil ”
will visit one or two of the Patent Attorneys—or get an
opinion from the Commissioner—he will find that the
attorneys well earn the fees they receive. These fees
are, in simple cases, considerably lower than stated by
“ Vigil,” whereas the Government fees on every applica-
tion, whether, it be for a pin or a steam-engine, are £8 ;
so “Vigil ” has been barking at the moon. If, after
making enquiries, “Vigil” is not convinced that inven-
tors get their money’s worth for services, I would advise
him to change his medical adviser.
Edward Wyatt Wickham, who was ad-
mitted to the N.S.W. Bar last Thursday, has
been 18 years in the Public Service, being now
Chief Clerk under the Commonwealth and State
Arbitration Acts. The Wickhams are ah ancient
family in the Ma State. Eighty years ago they
were at Maitland, and a few years later one of
them held an official position at Hyde Park Bar-
racks, Sydney. Eventually most of the Wick-
hams were to be found in Parramatta, where the
mother of the family was post-mistress. Another
member was Town Clerk of Parramatta for about
a quarter of a century, in the days when the
feuds between the Taylors and the Byrnes and
their friends made wild and woolly circus in the
council of the now peaceful little town.
Victorian Statist Drake has just issued his
wheat-area statistics for 1907-8. He shows that
there is an actual shrinkage in the area under crop
in Victoria this year compared with last year—
-2,263,301 acres last year; 2,133,000 acres this
year. In the whole of the Western District there
is only one county—Ripon—with enough land
under crop to be worth including in the detailed
statistics; and even Ripon is down for a miser-
able 69,800 acres, which is actually about 3000
acres less than it was the previous year. Evi-
dently, in the absence of a progressive land-tax,
the absurd prices demanded—whether you rent
land or buy it—have driven men out of the State.
They have got sick of working for the landlord
and the mortgagee.
“ Spring” : A feature of the recent brief
and last session of N.S.W. Parliament was the
resplendency of Ashburnham’s Eden George.
George has such style and grace that it is little
wonder he became a photo man. There is about
him an air of shopman-like superiority that is
immense ; his very feet seem to murmur “ Kindly
step this way.” He is the cheap tailor’s noblest
work. There are three politicians always engaged
facing the camera in this country —Willie Kelly,
B. Smith and Eden George; and of these the
greatest is Eden George. The Ashburnham
electors would do well to enthuse a little more
over their splendid rep. What does an ill-used,
miserable old State want with good men, when it
can get sleek men? What need to search for
brainy men, when shiny men are offering them-
selves broadcast ?
“ Mate ” writes :
The new Navigation Bill, that is to enforce local condi-
tions on foreign ships trading on our coast, will need to
be very explicit, or the English shipowner will wriggle
out of it as far as the higher wages are concerned.
A few years ago there was a ship trading here on time
charter. The crew had an agreement for coasting wages,
drawn up by a lawyer and signed by the master. A
note of this was entered in the official log. Still un-
satisfied, they had the increased rate of pay entered on
the articles, signed by the master of the ship and the
shipping master in one of our ports.
When the charter expired the ship went to a Mediter-
ranean port. The original articles having expired, the
crew were sent to England, to be paid on arrival there.
The owner repudiated the agreement for Australian
coastal wages while on time oharter, claiming that the
master had exceeded his powers. The men took the case
to Court and lost. Of course, the master was the one
they should have gone for, but he was still abroad with
the ship in the Mediterranean. The extra wages
amounted to over £6O each man.
Another case I know of was that of a mate whose extra
wages, for coasting, amounted to about £l2O. The
owners demanded that he should surrender this as the
price of keeping his billet. He held out for a time, and
finally compromised by giving up £75.
Within a week, he was accused of drunkenness, and
dismissed.
Foreign ships trading on the Australian coast should be
compelled to pay wages monthly. Otherwise, all the
seamen will see of the coastal wages will be the tail of the
remuneration disappearing round the corner.
N. S. W. Government recently erected
several workmen’s residences on the Rocks
(Sydney) resumed area, and the houses are now
letting well. To people who were accustomed to
moulder in the dingy dog-kennels provided by
“Private Enterprise,” the modern, well-appointed
State dwellings are a Paradise. Premier Carru-
thers is so pleased with the way things have
turned out that he is going to proceed further,
and build shops on the resumed land in George-
street North. For a Premier who is so staunchly
Anti-Sosh, Carruthers is making considerable
headway in State Socialism. The peculiar thing
is that Carruthers finds that State Socialism pays,
which it shouldn’t do, according to the doctrine
of the people he associates with.
Spruce Myth will really have to go to
England to tell the British Parliament to stop its
beastly interference with Private Enterprise, and
to live up to the Australian Tory idea of it as a
perfect pattern of Anti-Sosh. Now it has just
gone and passed an Advertisements Regulation
Act, which gives the Government power to
remove any hoarding, placard, etc., which, by
reason of its size or interference with the beauties
of Nature, has become the same sort of a nuisance
that Sydney Harbor has impatiently suffered
under ever since it was discovered, or thereabouts.
N.S.W. State politics have reached their
correct level up Bathurst way, where Bill Young,
M.L.A., whose political views are a deep and
murky mystery, bases his claim to a renewal of
the people’s confidence mainly on the fact that
he has got £IOOO spent on the constituency. To
this his opponent, a Deform person named
Miller, makes reply by assuring the Bathurst
electors that if they but return him he will wrest
considerably more than £IOOO out of Carruthers
to prop up the dejected old town. Also, argues
Miller, the man Young has already had £2BOO
from the country by way of screw, while he
(Miller) has had nothing. Also, Young having
got £IOOO for Bathurst, has probably about ex-
hausted his begging capacity, while he (Miller) is
quite fresh.
Out of £90,000 due as rent from private
landlords for the use of closed roads and State-
owned water frontages, under Victoria’s fairly
recent Act, only some £26,000 has been collected.
Cameron’s Works Department is responsible for
the gathering in of road and frontage rents,' and
does the work on conventional lines. Struggling
selectors and village settlers are generally made
to pay up, while many big landowners are
allowed to blankly ignore the department’s little
bill. Two M.’sL.C. are in possession of miles of
State river frontage and unused State roads in
Gippsland, and for these they have declined on
principle to pay anything. Summonses were to
be issued against them, but instructions came
from the Department to withhold proceedings
“till further notice.” There was no further
notice up to time of writing. One of these cases
involves 23 miles, or 300 acres of water frontage.
There are weird tales, too, of how small men are
charged 18s. per acre per annum for the use of
the State’s river banks or closed roads, while large
men are only asked 3s. per acre for as good or
better land. If a Labor Government displayed
such glaring favoritism, how long would it be
before the Tory press raised the cry of “corrup-
tion ” ?
Bruce Smith again notifies that he will not
accept the additional £2OO a year as member
of the Representatives. Notwithstanding a
general plausibleness of manner, there is
in Smith more cynicism to the square
inch than in any other man in Australian
political life. He was something extra-large
and plausible in the way of patriots when
payment of members was proposed in N.S.W.,
and threw many bouquets at himself for his firm
had just hit himself in the eye with the last posy
when he found himself taking the money. Pro-
bably the smooth old cynic will find the same
thing happen to him again.
Master-mariner Charles L. Gittins came
...... , , , . . -r,
to total shipwreck last week, aged lO years,
About 60 of these numerous years he had spent
trading on the N.S.W. coast. Gittins had many
adventures on and in the great deep, and could
remember being in seven shipwrecks. Three
times he was master of the wrecked vessel. Two
small ketches which he skippered were run down
and sunk in a matter of seconds when entering
Sydney Harbor. On the occasion of his last
trouble his crew of one boy was drowned,
and Gittins himself bumped on the bottom of the
Pacific, and came to the surface, sea boots upper-
most. He valiantly maintained that, even at 70
years, a little thing like that couldn’t hurt him ;
but his aged wife died a few months later, and
then old Gittins started to break up.
Many details of the coarse amours of a
certain notorious Australian identity, together
with information as to deeds of official jobbery
incidental to his peccadilloes, have been collated
by his disreputable acquaintances for purposes of
intimidation. The only way of relieving a high-
placed personage of the dread of exposure is to
shift him from his place. His duty to the country
is to die, but such men have no proper sense of
duty.
A simple-minded correspondent recently
asked the Australasian for a summary of the
arguments against compulsory resumption of land
and this is the reply he got
One of the main arguments advanced against coin-
pajsory resumption is that the State is led to pay an un.
duly high price for the land, which prevents closer settle-
ment thriving. Arbitrations notoriously go against the
Government, and in favor of the owner to be expropriated,
If this is avoided, and the land taken at the Government
valuation, that would be distinct confiscation,
So the Australasian’s theory seems to be this:
Closer settlement is wanted badly. The big
landlord is too unpatriotic to sell and make room
for population. To take his land by arbitration is
ruinous because he is such a swindler. To take it
at a State valuation would be confiscation because
the unpatriotic swindler is sacred. Therefore,
there is nothing to be done. The trouble is that
Australia is fully resolved to do something, and
if the Australasian manages to convince it that
the only chance to get land for the people lies in
confiscation, then it will just confiscate.

WHEREAS by an Order of the High Court
of Justice, Chancery Division, made in
, the Estate of theophilus
TRIPE MARKE deceased and in an action of
Rite v. Marke and others 1904 M. 3233 the fol-
lowing inquiry was directed namely
“What nephews and nieces of the said Testator
(other than his niece Mrs. Newcombe) were living
at his decease and what nephews or nieces of the
said Testator predeceased him leaving any and
what children and whether any and which of the
said nephews and nieces who survived the said
Testator and the children of nephews and nieces
who predeceased him are still living and as to I
such of the same nephews and nieces and children
of nephews and nieces as are dead who are their
respective legal personal representatives.” Notice
is hereby given that any persons claiming to be
entitled under the said Inquiry and particularly
any persons claiming to be sons or daughters of
Robert Marke (a brother of the said Testator) or
the children of such of the said sons and daughters
as predeceased the said Testator or the legal per-
sonal representatives of such of the same sons
and daughters and children of sons and daughters
as survived the said Testator but have since died
are by their Solicitors on or before the 15th day
of October 1907 to come in and prove their claims
at the Chambers of Mr. Justice Warrington and
Mr. Justice Parker at the Royal Courts of Justice
Strand London England or in default thereof
they will be peremptorily excluded from the |
benefit of the said Order. Tuesday the 22nd day I
of October 1907 at 11 of the clock in the forenoon
at the said Chambers is appointed for hearing
and adjudicating upon the claims.

July 1817 and was the son of Robert Marke and
Elizabeth Marke Marke his wife who for many

The Melbourne edition of the Stevenson-Inman
billiard match (at Alcock’s Hall) ended in a victory for
the receiver of points. Stevenson having won the pre-
vious game in Sydney, a number of people professed to
know that it was Inman’s turn this time. The men were
expected to play a deciding match, tossing for choice of
town and table. All the same, it is pretty certain that
the two Englishmen will not meet again in Australia. As
for the genuineness, or otherwise, of their “game and
game,” close watchers of the play were fully satislied that
Inman, handicapped to receive 2250 in 9000, is no gift for
the “ champion.” The last time they met in England, on
the same terms, it was Inman who won—very easily.
But inasmuch as Stevenson is a greater master of billiards
than the other man, he gets credit for being able to
“ beat him whenever he likes,” as though a start of 2250
points amounted to nothing at all.
The Westralian Test Race to pick the State’s
representative in the Warrnambool-Melbourne struggle
will eventuate on August 24, over the 116 miles between
Beverley and Perth. In addition to the honor of repre-
senting the State in the great Victorian contest, the
fastest rider will receive £2O from the Dunlop Rubber Co.
The Warrnambool-Melbourne race itself is drawing close,
and entries close with the Dunlop people, at their Mel-
bourne office, 108 Flinders-street, on August 30. The
eo. expects that at least 250 riders will start in this
classic event, which is now the recognised premier road
race of the world.
* * * *
An old lacrosse man writes: A prolonged
acquaintance with the Canadian team’s lacrosse methods
has made S.A. players very thoughtful. The first result
of that thought was that they borrowed from the
Adelaide Racing Club the steel skull caps worn by jockeys
for steeplechasing ; this was only ordinary prudence, in
view of Canada's too “willing” bashing of opponents.
The second result was extreme regret for having-
shortened the ground—as earnestly demanded by Canada
after being once beaten. Any Australian, seemingly can
give any Canadian 3yds. start in 50 ; but while our'dash-
ing wing-men are taking the ball up, all Canada—dis- I
regarding them entirely—hurries down the middle for
home. Asaresult, ourattack men, with full control of the
ball, find a solid mass of four goal-keepers, or six
occasionally eight! They can’t see through the goal let
alone shoot through it. It stands to reason that a space
only 6ft. by 6ft. can be made quite impregnable if you
put enough men on the job. The position is something-
like cricket, without a leg-before rule. It is also clear
that Australia can at least draw a match, if it cares
to adopt the same tactics, and if it insists on a lari>-e
arena it may even win. This will not be sport; but it wTll
save goals. And Canada does it, and Canada is the home
of the game. The real truth is that we now play the
game as it used to be played in Canada ; but they have
meanwhile developed it on lines of simple trickery. (The
deliberate time-wasting drove the crowd nearly frantic in
Adelaide.) And the more nearly the ground is reduced to
the size of a tennis-court, the better it will be for
jugglery and goal-packing.
* * * *
N.S.W. Gaming Act is evidently enticing
Christian Moses from elsewhere, for, while the roll has
swelled in Carruthers’ State, a great fading of Moses is
reported elsewhere—notably in Westralia. Last year the
W.A.T.C. registered 44 to bet in the paddock, now the
list totals only 33 ; and while 53 had authority to operate
on the flat, 28 now have the business to themselves.
Evidentß W.A. isn’t the land of milk and honey for the
layer of the odds that it once was ; either the money has
vanished or the punter is better educated to the advan-
tages of the tote.
* * * #
Adelaide has been looking on at some strange
pastimes lately. A team of handball plavers made the
journey from Ballarat, and were beaten by the school-
boys of the Christian Brothers’ College. Handball is
practically the old game of fives, but to plav it success-
fully you have to be named Geoghegan or O’Rafferty or
Breheny, or words to that effect. Then came the amazing
experience of two Rugby football matches within a
week. Adelaide had not seen two within the last
ten years, but lots of unexpected talent turned
up—Neville Colley, who was lately in England
at school, and there played for Kent when Kent was
premier county ; Professor Angus, and others with Maori-
land experience, and some old Sydney players So
the Challenger men were beaten pointless, by a goal from
a try, and a dropped goal; hut next week the sailors
strengthened their team, and won the return match
Adelaide spectators, accustomed to Australian rules (in
which a free kick -is given against you if you lay a finoer
unkindly upon an opponent) voted the tackling of Rugby
malicious and devilish.
* * * *
The Hog in the Car fell in recently at Wairarapa
(M.L.). First he ran down and severeh injured the wife
of a laborer. Then he talked in an abusive manner to
the husband, who had presented a bill for £2O odd for the
repair of the lady. If she hadn’t been in the road she
wouldn’t .have got hurt, and he’d see ’em all dead before
he’d part a stiver, and so forth. If they weren’t satisfied
they could go to law. The husband did, asking for £49
155., and the magistrate gave judgment for the amount
and costs. The magnate smiled, and, leaning over to the
smart young lawyer who had conducted the case against
him, intimated that he was going to appeal to the
.Supreme Court. “ You can’t! I knew that would be
your game, and purposely made the claim ss. under £5O
which is the smallest amount you can appeal over. You’re
in the mud, sir ! ” And he was. That lawyer is a public
benefactor.
* * * *
The latest number of Spalding’s valuable ath-
letic library to hand is “ The Golfer’s Annual.” Although
it contains only 138 pages of reading matter, the editor
has contrived to crowd into them a record of the year’s
golf in every white man’s country, and a list of the fix-
tures for all 1907 ; also a mass of knowledge re the rules
of the game and the decisions of the Golf'Committee so
that he who bunkers may read. The number of the book
is vol. 11., No. 22.
* * * *
To hand from the publishers, Messrs. Gordon
and Gotch, “ Modern Rugby Football, Maoriland
Methods.” This is the most up-to-date book on Rugby
and is from the pen of A. H. Baskerville, who is promi-
nently associated with the Maoriland Professional Team
about to leave for England. Baskerville, who was until a
few weeks ago, employed in the Maoriland Post and
Telegraph Department, is an ex-Oriental Club (Wel-
lington) player, and knows the game from A to
Z, and his book is one that no Rugby player or
enthusiast should he without. The sub-title, “Maori-
land Methods,” is suggestive. We in Australia
have seen a lot of them lately, and when the book
was opened, this was the first thing that hit the
reader in the eye Note.—The onus is on a side to
see that their opponents do not play more than 15 men
and if such side fail to do so, they must be sufferers
thereby and points scored and time played must be
counted.” This is not Baskerville’s discredit—it is a note
inserted into the rule-book by the Maoriland Referees’
Association, and throws an interesting sidelight on the
methods of the gentle Maoriland Rugbyite. However to
return to the book : To thoroughly enjoy watching a
game of Rugby, Baskerville’s book should be read before-
hand. It is better than a sherry and bitters before
dinner. To players it is a first-class magazine, and there
are quite a number of Sydney’s first-grade players who
will be twice their present value if they read, learn and
inwardly digest it. It hammers in the importance of a lot
of things they do not seem to realise as yet, such as line
kicking and starting forward rushes from the line-out
in both of which branches our men are decidedly deficient.’
Altogether, it is a handy little book that should be in the
pocket of every follower and player of Rugby.

Somebody whose initials are quite illegible
writes to say, in defence of the Maoriland footballers,
that the cinematograph pictures are unreliable • the
rapidity with which they are taken causes them to leave
out a lot of things that happened, and to put in
things that didn’t happen. It may be so, but if it is so,
it is unfortunate that the machine left out the discredit-
able acts of the Australians, and put in so many that
reflected on the honesty of the All Blacks team.

The fate of old-pattern motor cars will shortly
be an interesting question. There are quite a number in
Australia whose disposal is a conundrum to their owners.
Motor houses fight shy of accepting them “
anything larger than a mouse-trap, and ltwillnoi be long
before some of them will sell for the price of a bicycle or
less Probably the new owners will buy trouble, but that
s their look out. Writer knows a case where an old
pattern car went begging at £6O, and the price crawled
downwards tm it stood It £3O. The ultimate buyer
turned up in a wayback cocky, who partly strayed and
was partly dragged into the place, His ignorance of
motor cars appeared to be profound, and the process of
nailing him was a long one. When the money u' as
gathered in, the staff laughed long and heartily at its
fuck in getting £25 for a stranded rattletrap. But when
Hayseed got the car home he took out the engine and
hitched it on to a chaffcutter, and putting shafts on to
the body of the vehicle be had a rubber-tyred buggy to
boot. It was a rather unusual-looking buggy, but for
hard wear it is mighty cheap at the price.

“Cross Nibs”: Here is one way in which an
oil and color merchant packs the dollars on their edges.
He manufactures a line of ready-nnxed paints, winch the
man with the brush can purchase at lower cost than else-
where. Collided with a late employee of said color pro-
vider recently, and poured a torrent of questions against
his sidehead, chiefly concerning the ti'med tints He
depressed his off-side eyelid and whispered that whiting
made a good understudy for white lead, with the addi-
tion of a little glue for adhesive purposes. Dabslappe
discovered that there was a huge profit m the substitu-
tion, also that the majority are hypnotised by a low-price
ticket. ’Twas only the other day l perceived an irascible
individual smiting a bunch of wired bristles against the
front fence of his suburban home. He moved a weary
arm what time he fervently anathematised the can of
material at his feet and the maker thereof.. Glanced at
the label in passing, and noted in conspicuous type,
“Dabslappe’s Durable Paint.” From information re-
ceived, I surmised that the glue was making its presence
felt. The weather-resisting properties of this particular
paint are exhausted in a few months, and a bath of lime-
wash would undoubtedly be just as effective fortmiber
preservation. A chalky stain will be left upon the
fingers, if rubbed upon any surface it has been applied to.
Remember, ve who contemplate the color scheme, that
whiting doth cost but one-fourth the price of whitelead,
and go ye and consider the robbery thereof.
“ Edyson ” : I met a character in Melbourne the
other day. Was taking refreshment with some friends
in a bar when the character entered. He was tall well-
built, and badly-dressed. In one hand he carried a whip,
in the other a noble potato skewered on a carving fork.
He rested his elbow on the bar counter, holding the spud
up for the inspection of the barmaid, but his head was
burned aside, and his eyes were fixed on us. His manner
was gentlemanly, dignified, quiet, and composed. I
presume you are not in immediate need of some excellent
potatoes?” He was addressing the barmaid but his
attention seemed to be on my friends.. The lookwasnot
impertinent, it was not inquisitive it invited confidence
“ No, certainly not,” snapped the barmaid. These are
exouisite potatoes. Potatoes of a similar quality from
the same land at Carrum are provided for his Excellency
the Governor-General. It was a brother to the potato I
now hold took first prize for breeding and behaviour at
the agricultural show. We have potatoes from the same
seeding two of which make a dray load. Cooked
they Ire like balls of snow. Their flavor ,s de-
lirious.” “No.no. Get out. What would I want
with potatoes?” said the barmaid. He did not n ? la "° e
at herlhut moved the spud in a graceful circle, and con-
tinued : “ These potatoes, if left to mature, assume great
dimensions. The small ones culled for domestic use have
all the virtues of the large ones concentrated. I ly ou eat
them you care for nothing else.” “ Oh, go to the devil!
said the barmaid. He continued to look at us ; his voice
was very gentle. “Flowery, full of flavor, sustaining
and patronised by vice-royalty, the superfine, clear-skin
potato. If you do not want to eat them, my d ear, wear
them on your hat. They are being used as parlor orna-
ments bv the best families.” Here my younger friend
succumbed. “Have a drink?” he sa ? d .\
specialist did not alter his manner or his attitude. Thank
vou sir, I will. A large beer, if you please. Two
hours later I was in another hotel passage, and heard a
gentle voice from the bar. “The superfine, clear-skin
potato, the Governor’s joy, fine as flour, mellow asanut.
I looked in. The specialist was leaning on the counter,
rotating his prize Murphy in front of the
maid. His mild, compelling eye was fixed on a w eak-
chinned youth, who was diffidently sipping K”? and
ginger-beer. In the street there was no cart. The PO-
tato plea was a mere stall to enable this bad man to w or
uponanv impressionable fellow-creature within the range
Ks mild, compelling eye When I looked >n agam the
specialist had a maximum beer before him, and his two
hands were full Of lunch.

EIGHT YEARS SUFFERING
A PERMANENT CURE.
THE CASE OF MRS. A. WEAVER.
(BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.
No one can tell the self-denying work which
good men have done in the early days of this
country, neither can we exaggerate the nobleness
of spirit which animates present-day people in a
desire to ameliorate distress as much as lies in their
power. Therefore, the public should be informed
of some of these efforts, one of which was made by
Mrs. Alice Weaver, of No. 7 Louis Street, Redfern,
who replied to the reporter by saying :—
“About this time last year I was living in
Telopea Street, and it was just before I left there
for this place that I was fortunate in casting aside
a complaint that had worried me for no less than
eight years. What a fearful life of suffering mine
had been all that time, to be sure, and to think that
nothing in the world could benefit me made my
spirits droop lower than they will ever be again,
I hope.”
“What made you think that nothing could
benefit you ? ” queried the writer.
“I arrived at that conclusion because nothing
had done me good so far, and I believe I tried
every medicine going. At the time my second
child was born I was under a doctor, who did
everything he could for me, but stilt my health re-
mained as bad as it could be. Indeed, at that
period I was nearly dead with indigestion, al-
though I dieted myself and did all that could be
done to keep my torments in check. It was of no
avail, though, for when I ate anything I could
hardly breathe owing to a feeling which came like
a lump in my chest, from which place a sharp pain
went right through to the centre of my shoulders.
I then remained in the utmost agony until the food
was vomited up again, after which I felt so ex-
hausted that I could not think of doing a thing in
the house until I had had a good rest. It would
surprise you if you onlyknew how poor my appetite
was. You would wonder how anyone could keep
alive with it, and as for my head, oh, my sufferings
with it were awful. The most piercing pains at
onetime, and themost depressingachesat another.
Really, I think it was a marvel how I kept my
senses with the agony I endured in the head ; and
it’s strange that I did not meet with many a nasty
accident through the giddiness which used to over
take me. I was a misery to myself and everybody
around me, and to tell you the honest truth I never
thought I would live through it all. My eyes used
to feel heavy enough to drop out of their sockets,
and I was affected in the mouth by such a bitter
taste that it made me feel sick of a morning, after
putting in the usual restless nights that had done
much towards making a wreck of me. Even in the
daytime Iwasalwaysimagining something terrible
was going to happen, and these were just the very
things that Clements Tonic, which I commenced to
take, showed itself to be the master of ; for it sent
such a vigor through my frame, when I had been
taking it a couple of weeks, that I could not help
feeling joyful in the belief that at last my health
had taken a beneficial turn. I got more Clements
Tonic, and as each bottle was finished, so did my
prospects improve, for I commenced to take my
meals with a degree of heartiness which astonished
my people as much as it pleased them ; and to my
own satisfaction and delight the former discomforts
attending my meals were quickly out of existence.
Oh, dear, what a changed woman I am now to then.
My limbs do not ache from weakness like they
formerly did, and I can go about my work as
cheerfully and as actively as any housewife in the
country. I do not spend any restless nights now;
never dream during my sleep, and the pains that
used to abound have long since been driven away
from every part of my body. Well, Clements

America makes all Her own Pianos and imports none*
Canada makes all her own Pi&nos and imports none.
France makes all her own Pianos and imports none.
Germany makes all l\er own Pianos and imports none.
In all these countries pianos are very heavily taxed, and yet are sold at moderate prices. The average cost price in
Germany of pianos exported to Australia is not quite £2O. Yet these pianos are sold in Australia at £5O to £2OO each.
The great majority of them are sold in Australia at £5O to £7O each, and these are invoiced at £l6 and less. We have the
catalogues and are prepared to publish the quotations.
Australia can make all her own pianos, and make them far better than the pianos bought cheaply abroad and
sold dearly in Australia.
We have produced in Australia 9000 first-class pianos, which stand climates such as those of Fiji, Port Darwin and
West Australia, where foreign pianos made in a cold country must and do crack in their joints, and their wrest-planks
perish.

The' A.l.D'.E.. — Australian Institute of
DkVrrtestic Economy—sefetrts to be making a great
success in a siriall way Of its College of House-
situated iiii i/onsdalfe-sfcteet, Melbourne,
opposite the Haw Courts. There ttas a largely
liriattended co'okery Class at the local Wotkiftg
Meii’S College when the A.l.D.E. started opera-
tions, sirid the old, slow-going ihistitutioh pro-
tested ftgaiiist the idea.of Government patronage'
for the new Domestic Economists; but whilst the'
class was losing its half-dozen members,- the little
college in Lonsdale-street was enrolling all the
students it could do with, and there are now a
number of girls waiting their turn for admission.
The business of the Institute is to advertise the
College of Housewifery with Practical Talks
at the Athenaeum Hall and other places.
Also, it is offering scholarships which entitle
the winners to 12 months of college tuition in
cookery, laundry-work, etc., and a Government
certificate at the end of the course. Scholars are
invited to compete for the “ships” in September,
and a promise of “prizes to the value of £110”
looms large on the A.l.D.E. prospectus. , The
severely businesslike “Rita,” of Melbourne
Herald, has given a lot of honorary service in
furtherance of this scheme of domestic art-culture,
likewise the demonstrations by experts are
gratuitous. It is a scheme that should specially
commend itself to newly married wives and bride-
grooms whose means are limited. An education
in housewifery may be easily and enthusiastically
acquired what time the family consists of only
two persons, and how could Angelina better ex-
press her love for Edwin than by greeting him
with the glad tidings that she has won a scholar-
ship? It costs a shilling a year to belong to the
institute.

Perhaps the most useful of the many
books perpetrated by the late Joseph Hatton was
the least literary and original of the lot. In
“In Jest and Earnest —A Book of Gossip,” one
gets an interesting collection of the old chestnut
stories and anecdotes which everybody of middle
age has heard and nobody has quite correctly re-
membered. Possibly Hatton’s recollection of the
facts and fancies he gossiped of was faulty in
many cases. Certainly his numerous instances of
J. L. Toole’s “ready wit” could have been well
spared. But the volume in other respects is worth
reading and preserving as a scrap book.
George Swinburne, the tall, thin, white-
headed Victorian Minister of Agriculture, who
gave £2000 the other day to the technical schools
of Melbourne, is the strongest and most genuinely
respected man in the Bent Government. His
Water and Hairy Acts, though badly mutilated
by the Tories, are the most progressive and states-
manlike measures Victoria has seen for many a
year. Swinburne is a man of stern and uncom-
promising decision, and a remarkably keen-witted
and resolute administrator ; and at the same time
he is the surprise and delight of all who meet him
for the first time, by reason of his courteous
manner and delightful accessibility. He is uni-
formly kind to pressmen, who vote him a man, a
brother, and a gentleman.

WHAT IS CATARRH?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.

ma. Write for booklet free, or send

TARIFF ITEMS.
The preliminary shots in the tariff battle
have done one good thing. They have brought
the Freetrade contingenter into the open to say
plainly what he thinks of the person he is fight-
ing for—the importer. Johnson, the alligator
sitter, asked Lyne if he knew that wholesale firms
were bleeding the retailer and the consumer by
raising prices on goods that hadn’t paid increased
duties ; and wanted to know if that sort of piracy
couldn’t be stopped. Then Hutchison requested
Lyne to tell the people that the increased prices
were exorbitant, and were not the result of the tariff,
and G. H. Reid interjected, “It has happened
every day since the foundation of the world.” It’s
good to get it on the testimony of staunch Free-
traders like Johnson and Reid that the Freetrade
importer, who is the prop and stay of Freetrade,
is a shark without a conscience, and that he has
been a shark “ since the foundation of the world.”
The new tariff sent the stodgy old Argus
clean off its head, and for a day or two it printed
almost anything. On the Sunday its Hobart
correspondent wired :
There is indignation over the increased taxation ... in
fact, all sections of the community are hit hard. Poli-
ticians are alarmed, because they foresee a further re-
duction in Tasmania’s Customs revenue.
The importer is sure that he will have to pay so
much extra duty that he will be ruined ; also he
is sure that he will pay so little duty that the
country will be ruined. The importer really ought
to make up his mind which it is going to be;
anyway, he ought to see that he only tells one
yarn at a time.
Freetrade England does a good export
trade in one line these times of its humming
prosperity—viz., blacklegs. It has become a fixed
custom nowadays with the sweater of the
European continent, when he needs the really
cheap laboring article, to turn towards the pros-
perous old Mother Land, with its booming Board
of Trade returns, and forthwith, through showers
of bricks, hurled by displaced foreign workers,
press the desperate, hungry-eyed Britishers, eager
for a job anywhere and at any price so it will
keep ghost and meat together. A couple of
months back it w r as Hamburg; more recently it
was Antwerp that absorbed, according to Reuter,
its “700 English workmen.” Given a sufficiency
of Freetrade in the United Kingdom, and the
■world will yet be offered the spectacle of inden-
tured English coolies being shipped eastward to
labor on the railways of protected Japan.
Thus an English paper :
Mr. Walter Chamberlain made a striking speech at the
shareholders’ meeting of W. and T. Avery, of which he is
chairman, at Birmingham, on 2nd July. He said they
had been forced to erect, at a cost so far of £22,000, a
factory in America, for fear their rivals should start
dumping goods here. But for the belated Cobdenism
from which the country was suffering, all this money
might have been spent in England. Other firms would
have to do as they had done. The pedants of the Cobden
Club were fiddling while Rome burned. Foreign nations
were augmenting their work because they were always
sure of two markets -their own and ours—whereas we in
England could not touch them in their markets.
Of course, no bushman dreams of drinking
his post-and-rail without milk; so the S.M.
Herald has been moved to tears over the picture
of Billjim setting out on his boundary ride with-
out the customary case of condensed milk slung to
his saddle bow, all on account of the increased
duty. But it was a grievous waste of moisture,
for there are at least half-a-dozen brands of Aus-
tralian concentrated juice on the market. Also
there is even hope for the fastidious Billjim who
can drink nothing but imported stuff; for the very
issue that contained the wail contained likewise a
remark by H. J. Preston, Australian rep. of
Nestles Limited, to the effect that his firm had
almost completed negotiations for putting
£lOO,OOO into a Queensland factory.
The campaign of Foreign-trade lies com-
menced early after the tariff appeared, and the
S. M. Herald got a flying start. Said the
Herald, amongst other things :
The people will pay more for their tea and kerosene,
so that Mr. Lyne may give a nice present to his manu-
facturing friends.
And there is no duty on tea or kerosene which
arrives in bulk, so, if people pay more for either
pn account of the tariff, some of the Herald’s
importing friends must be swindling them. The
Herald shouldn’t associate with swindlers.
The gentle importer has been at his old
tricks in Maoriland, where he has been affirming
that “boots and shoes can’t be made in this
country !” To back it up, he analysed some
cheap, locally-made footgear, and proved it to be
largely shoddy. Then the loc'al man got to work,
and analysed a lot of high-priced imported boots
and shoes, and proved that they were all shoddy
—that they were not made of leather, but of
paper and other frauds; and, moreover, that
they were shipped free on board the cargo
steamer at3s. per pair. The cheaplocal boot at least
consisted of some kind of leather, while the higher-
priced one was of splendid material. Butin the
imported lines, from the highest to the lowest,
shoddy stuck out like the bowsprit of a pirate.
Now the importers are sorry they spoke, and
there is talk about heavier duties and a Commerce
Act, to ensure that the person who asks for
leather boots shall not be taken down with brown
paper.
“ Father-o’-Five ” : I am in the country,
rabbiting. My wife writes to say that the new
tariff will make her household bills fully two
shillings a week bigger. Do I, a mere hard-
grafter, growl and grumble, and swear that Aus-
tralia is being ruined by Protection, because it hits
my small pocket a bit for a while ? No —I just
grin and bear it, and catch extra rabbits, because
I know that the burden will be lightened by-
and-bye by the blessings that will follow our
encouragement of our own industries. The bulk
of the complaining will be done by the importers
and their satellites and those whose thinking
faculties are atrophied. The exporters won’t
complain—and it’s the exporters who are going to
make Australia.
As the Worker puts it: —
A boilermaker suicided in Sydney last week. For
some time past he had been out of work, and had become
despondent. Meanwhile, the Oarruthers Government
continues to pay workers’ passages from England.
Yes; and, meantime, while a boilermaker suicides
because the work he should be doing in Sydney is
done in foreign countries, the Workek’s party
continues to “sink the fiscal issue.”
It seems now that the tariff is ‘ ‘ ill-timed ; ”
it is absurd, according to various importers whose
views have been raked into print, to put up the
tariff while the country is prosperous and there
are no unemployed. It is hard to please some
people. If the tariff were put up during a de-
pression they would want to know why that par-
ticular time was seized on, instead of waiting till
the people were prosperous and could afford to
pay the duties. Also, they would in that case
point out that new industries required capital,
and they would want to know what sort of a fool
Government it was that tried to force industries
just when the necessary capital wasn’t available.
When the men it interviewed wouldn’t
lie up to specifications concerning the tariff, the
Argus supplied all the desired lying in the head-
ings. For instance (Argus 12/8/’O7): —
PUTTING UP PRICES.
WOMEN’S CLOTHING AFFECTED.
Mr. Crossley, of Foy and Gibson, said the tariff would
not affect that firm’s business at a 11.... In some articles
of women’s clothing there would, for a time at any rate, be
an increase in prices. One result of the tariff would be
that the firm would extend its already large fac-
tories. The firm was putting lines of tweeds before its
customers that seemed to suit them better than any-
thing that could be imported at the price.... Under the
new duties more making up would be done locally, and
there would be an increase of work in the factories.... A
manufacturer of Rheims said he would be prepared to
consider the establishment of a factory for cashmeres,
provided the Commonwealth gave Protection up to one-
third.
That must have been a bitter pill to swallow; but
the Argus put on the brazen head-lines, and
risked it.
A meeting of Sydney piano importers last
week unanimously resolved
The tariff is not Protection, but Prohibition, and the
musical education of the whole of Australia must suffer
if the people are forced to buy an inferior class of
instrument.
What strikes one at first blush about this resolu-
tion is the cheek of the people who thus off-
handedly assume that an Australian-made piano
must necessarily be “an inferior class of instru-
ment.” But a glance at the names of the people
who are so broken up about the injury that is
going to happen to Australia explains a lot. Here
are some of them: Berlin Piano Co., J. Hers,
Weidemann and Co., Hoffnung and Co., Aengen-
heyster and Co., and Feldheim, Gotthelf and Co.
And these people, it seems, are true Australians,
thinking nothing about their foreign pockets, and
only harassed by alarms lest the musical education
of this country may go to the dingoes.
Victorian Premier Bent’s trip abroad gave
him a chance to compare Freetrade Britain’s
wages rates with those of Australia :
He found everywhere that the wages did not compare
with those in Australia, or, at any rate, in Victoria ; and,
besides, the men worked 10 hours a day against eight
hours worked by Australians. At one iarge ironworks
at Rochester, where 2500 men were employed, he learned
that the wages paid were 25 per cent, less for 10 hours’
labor than Australian manufacturers paid for eight
hours. On the railways of England the rates were 20 per
cent, less than the rates in vogue in Victoria. Generally,
the wages of the working class in England were poor,
and he considered that, industrially, Victoria was a long
way ahead of the Mother land.
Thomas Bent, Victoria’s uproarious Pre-
mier, is no angel, but at least there is no stinking
fish in his basket. He had not reached his own
State before he roared in the ear of everybody
who would listen, “Australia is the best country
in the world.” And when he got to Wodonga he
woke that township with ‘‘This Sydney express
is the best-appointed train in the world.”
Goldsbrough, Mort and Co. report re wool: —
There has been an active inquiry during- the past week.
Transactions, however, have been confined to local fell-
mongered descriptions, some fairly large lots of which we
have disposed of at satisfactory prices. The quantity
available for the sales next week will be small owing to
many of the clips at present in store being incomplete.
Reports from manufacturing centres show that trade is
flourishing in spite of an exceptionally wet summer in
England. Tnis is the time of year when those interested
in the wool trade look for some of the larger topmakers
“bearing’’the market; under present conditions it will
require an exceptionally strong “bearing” movement to
influence prices to any great extent. Generally speaking,
market prospects are distinctly encouraging for the new
clip.*

, 'T'HIS wonderful new Forage Plant was introduced into North America two years ago
from Africa. The first year, according to official returns, 74,668 farmers planted
Penicillaria, and their experience was so entirely satisfactory that last year 225,000 farmers
adopted it, and this year over a million have undertaken its cultivation in preference to
I all other kinds of fodder plants. The Ameri<an agricultural newspapers are full of letters
from farmers in all parts of the States, from the cold prairies of Manitoba to the torrid
plains of Texas and Florida, from the Atlantic seaboard to the Pacific coast, all describing
the amazing success of Penicillaria; not a single failure has been reported. The
supply of Penicillaria Seed we have been able to obtain this season is only sufficient for
experimental and seeding purposes, but we hare arranged for a large stock of seed for
next season's requirements. We can. therefore, now only offer sample packets of the
seed to enable farmers to plant test plots, which will satisfy them as to the highly valuable
?ualities of this remarkable plant, and encourage them to cultivate it extensively in the
uttire. Sample Packets of Penicillaria Seed 2s. 6d. posted. Not more than two packets
Will be sold to any person, aa it is our object to have as many farmers as possible give this
plant a trial. Send for Descriptive Circular giving full particulars and directions for
cultivation ;we will post it free. Obtainable only from

The accuracy of recent predictions made by this eminent Astrologer has caused
many of his friends to believe that he possesses a supernatural power, but
he modestly asserts that his predictions are due alone to a scientific under-
standing of natural laws. The many thankful letters Prof. De Astro has received
from people wiho have benefited by his advice furnish ample proof that he is sincere
in his work and has a kindly feeling towards humanity.
Readers of “ The Bulletin ” can obtain a Reading by addressing a
letter to PROF. DE ASTRO, 366 BOURKE STREET, MELBOURNE*
Simply say you wish a Reading of your Life, stating your birth date,
sex, and whether married or single, enclosing Is. Stamps or Postal Note
to pay postage, etc., and the Reading will be promptly sent.

BRONCHITIS AND PLEURISY.
A SEVERE CASE CURED BY TWO BOTTLES OF HEARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURE.
AFTER OTHER TREATMENT HAD FAILED
Mr. Hearne, Chemist, Geelong.
Dear Sir, —Some months ago in Sydney, I suffered from a severe attack of influenza, and was confined to my
room for about a week, at the end of which time, feeling somewhat better, I got up and tried to transact my busi-
ness as usual. But I got up too soon, for the very next day I had a relapse, and suffered tortures from what the
doctor told me was pleurisy and bronchitis. The pain from the former in my chest and shoulders was frightful, and
for four long weeks I was confined to my bed under the care of a well-known Sydney doctor, and all the time his
medicine gave me but temporary relief. The landlady of the hotel (the Cleveland), where I resided, told me of a
medicine—Uearne’s Bronchitis. Cure—from Victoria, which had cured her of a bad attack of bronchitis and pains in
the chest, and begged of me to try it. I did so, and, in thanks and gratitude to you, tell you that, after the second
bottle, my cough had ceased ; but what is more astonishing, the pains from pleurisy entirely left me, and in about
a week I Was able to attend to my duties as usual.—Yours faithfully,
J. BRAHAM, Melbourne Punch Office, Melbourne.

BEWARE OF IMITATIONS! The great success of HEARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURE has induced a number of
unprincipled persons to make imitations, each calling his medicine “ Bronchitis Cure,” with the object of deceiving the simple-
minded, and so getting a sale for an imitation which has none of the beneficial effects that HEARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURE
has. Consequently it has become necessary to draw r your attention to this fact, and to request you in your own interests to be
particular to ask for HEARNE’S and see that you get it.

Biographical item from the West :
During the Fremantle (W.A.) lumpers’ strike six years
ago the local Wesleyan parson, G. E. Wheatley, played a
prominent part as mediator, but his efforts in that direc*
tion were viewed with sour suspicion by the men them-
selves. A little later on he resigned from the ministry,
and started in the land-booming and auctioneering line.
He got tangled up in several heavy ventures, and was
rather severely handled by the Official Receiver, who
suggested that it was not the usual thing for an agent to
receive commission from both sides, particularly when
the estate was in bankruptcy and under the jurisdiction
of the court. Wheatley then took over a big pile of shops
—mostly empty—in the leading Perth thoroughfare, and
transformed them into a big temperance pubbery under
the title of King Edw’ard’s Hostel. He invited the
Ministry and a crowd of public men to hold
forth at the opening on the beauties of total abstin-
ence. Within 12 months afterwards the gentle Wheatley
obtained a full hotel license for his regal coffee house,
and he promptly traded the concern off at a big profit to
an opulent city councillor. Before passing the contents
of the cellar over to the purchaser, Wheatley is said to have
discovered that the bulk whisky was too strong, and to
have pumped considerable quantities of water into the
casks—anyhow the water was there. An excise
officer happened to pay an unexpected visit and made
trouble, and an unsympathetic Bench fined Wheatley a
fiver with many costs for reducing whisky below statutory
strength. Also it remarked that the incoming landlord
had better overhaul the liquor carefully. Many things
are liable to happen to a n'.&cffeeaaUiinltheuubildiy.AMit.t.

By the Oldest Inhabitant .
The Tebbutts, of which Astronomer John represents the
third generation since the family began to leave afoot-
f ... . , , ■ winHonr CN S W 1 for
mark hereabouts, have resided in Windsor (Jn.o. YV.j tor
over a century. The grandfather, John Tibbutts (so
spelled), emigrated from England in 1301, with his wife,
two sons, and one daughter. Soon after arrival the
family settled in Windsor district, and engaged in bucolic
pursuits. John Tebbutt was one of the signers of an
address to Lieutenant-Colonel Paterson, asking him to
hurry up and restore tranquility to the colony. The
address, dated 18th April, ISOB, contains this para-
graph :
“ The whole Government appears to be put into the
hands of John McArthur, Esq., who seems a very im-
proper person, he having been a turbulent and trouble-
some character, constantly quarrelling with his Majesty’s
Governors, and other principal officers, from Governor
Phillip to Governor Bligh, and we believe him to be the
principal agitator and promoter of the present alarming
and calamitous state of the coiony.”
The signers were mostly Hawkesbury settlers and sup-
porters of Governor Bligh. The brothers Thomas and
John Tebbutt, failing to make agriculture pay, started
as storekeepers, or, as they were described in those days,
“dealers,” in George-street, Windsor. The business was
wound up in the early ’forties. John, the astronomer,
was born in Windsor, May 24, 1834. His father, after he
gave up “dealing,” a word strangely suggestive of rabbits
and bottles nowadays, w'ent back on the land at the place
known as “The Peninsula,” where John now’ has his
Observatory.

REMARKABLE
INVENTION
FOR THE
CULTURE
OF HAIR
CHE EVANS VACUUM CAP is a practical invention constructed on scientific and
hygienic principles, by the simple means of which a free and normal circulation
is restored throughout the scalp. - The minute blood vessels are gently stimulated
to activity, thus allowing the food supply, which can only be derived from the blood,
to be carried to the hair roots, the effects of which are quickly seen in a healthy, vigorous
growth of hair. There is no rubbing, and as no drugs or chemicals of whatsoever kind
are employed, there is nothing to cause irritation. It is only necessary to wear the Cap
three or four minutes daily.
60 DAYS’ FREE TRIAL!
The Company's Guarantee.
An EVANS VACUUM CAP will be sent you for sixty days’ free
trial. If you do not see a gradual development of a new growth of hair,
and are not convinced that the Cap will completely restore your hair, you
are at liberty to return the Cap with no expense whatever to yourself. It
is requested, as an evidence of good faith, that the price of the Cap be
deposited with the Chancery Lane Safe Deposit Company of London, the
largest financial and business institution of the kind in the world, who will
issue a receipt guaranteeing that the money will be returned in full, on
demand, without questions or comment, at any time during the trial period.
The eminent Dr. I. N. LOVE, in his address to the Medical Board on the subject of Alopsecia (loss of
hair) stated that if a means could be devised to bring nutrition to the hair follicles (hair roots), without
resorting to any irritating process, the problem of hair growth would be solved. Later on, when
the EVANS VACUUM CAP was submitted to him for inspestion, ha remarked that the Cap would
fulfil and confirm in practice the observations he had previously made before the Medical Board.
Dr. W. MOORE, referring to the invention, says that the principle upon which the Evans Vacuum
Cap is founded is absolutely correct and indisputable.

RECiENT HOUSE, REGENT STREET, LONDON, W.
The Drunkenness of Michael
Strood.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MIKE STROOD was branding his
heifers, and there was an odor of scorched
hide and sounds of profanity in the air.
MikeStrood had been drinking, and was all over
the shop, mad even for him. He had been
drinking for a week, and he got worse every day,
and would be due for an attack of jim-jams in a
very short time if he went on. And the men
reckoned the sooner he had his jim-jams the
better, for he made the pace too uneasy for them,
with his hustling and shouting and swearing
—swearing at the hands, swearing at the
cows, swearing at the fences, and cursing pro-
fusely at himself. And he made Peter swear,
too, when he snatched the brand out of his
hands with “Not that
way you —— fool ! ” and in the bustle
of it scraped the corner of its hot end against
Peter’s forearm. For an instant it looked as if
Mike Strood and his cattle and his farm and
everything that was his were going to be laid
waste, there was such brimstone in Peter’s face.
But Peter controlled himself presently, and was
ordered about and shouted at like the rest.
That was one of the curious things about Peter
—why he did control himself and stand things,
ass as he was in many ways—and for the matter
of that why he fooled on at Mike Strood’s when
he could have bettered himself at half-a-dozen
different places round if he’d wanted to. People
said it was because Peter was an old flame of
Bessie Strood’s that he worked on at her hus-
band’s place. They said it openly, anywhere
except to Peter's face, or in front of Mike
Strood.
Some, men chiefly, said it “ served Strood
right,” whatever that meant. The women, of
course, went the other way, fixing bad names on
Bess, and saying it was no wonder her “ pore hus-
band ” took to drink with a wife like that.
Meanwhile, Mike Strood pranced about with
his branding iron. He was just about fit for the
asylum, and in his performances he kicked over
the old kerosene tin full of red-hot coals. Some
of the contents rolled through the fence and set
fire to the grass on the other side, and there was
about six feet of flame in an instant, and all
hands had to turn to and save the homestead. Then
for about an hour Mike Strood was half sober till
things righted again. Everybody was in a
smother of smoke and smut and sweat, so Strood
sent the lad Tom with a can for beer to the
shanty, about half a mile up the road, while he
got the fire going for his irons again.
Bessie came out when the grass caught fire, and
helped with the rest to put it out, and she might
now have gone inside about her business if she
had liked to. But she didn’t like. Instead, she
hung round talking to Peter and the men, as she
was too fond of doing. She was there when Tom
came back with the beer, and Strood and the rest
drank it in turns out of the same pannikin. She
stood with her bare plump arms akimbo, and
allowing a good deal of herself round the neck
(she was never particular to a button or a hook
or two), talking to Peter and ehyaeking her
husband about setting the place on fire.
Strood got wild, and dug the pannikin into the
beer. He gave it to Peter, who was already
drunk, and told Bessie to go off into the house.
She told him to go and hang himself, and said that
he had had enough drink for one day, without send-
ing for more and “making others as bad as him-
self!”
This he took to brazenly mean that he was
spoiling Peter, a better man, inferentially, and
that made him wilder than before. And by this
time everybody was drunk except Bess, who was
only foolish.
They were all going on like a lot of maniacs,
and no wonder there was a row. Peter stood
grinning in a beery way all the time, and saying
idiotic things to Bess. Presently Strood took
another gulp of beer, and, catching up his brand
out of the coals, he sang out, “ Here ! Run her in
quick and lively ” (he looked at Peter and pointed
at Bess). “ Damn it, we’ll brand her with the
rest of the cows ! ”
Bess curled her lip at him, but the men thought
the jest worth a grin each. Strood winked at
Peter, and that bulky fool took it to mean that
there was a joke on foot.
So Peter, being even drunker than he looked,
seized Bess by the arms from behind and began
marching her up to the rails, and winked foolishly
at the boys to enter into the spirit of the fun.
Bess screamed wildly, but big Peter whispered in
her ear that he' d look after her and not let the
drunken fool hurt her. Yet the drunken fool
was soberer than Peter was, and Bess saw some-
thing dreadful in his eyes, and screamed all the
louder as they hauled her towards the rails in
what they thought was mere horse-play.
She knew it wasn’t play, and she well nigh
went mad. “ He’ll murder me ! He’ll murder
me ! ” she cried. “ Pete, you fool, let me go ! ”
But there is such little difference between the cries
that are uttered in mere play and those of real
tragedy that they didn’t understand, especially
as they were drunk. Peter was too intent on
managing her to watch the aspect of mad Mike
Strood. They were all pretending that they
were pretending to hold her, while there was no
pretence at all about it. Her clothes were being
torn off her back in the struggle, and Peter’s face
was bleeding from a murderous scratch she had
scrawled all down it. And yet the silly beery
lover never saw through things, and still went
on keeping his end uppermost in what he thought
was a clumsy joke, while Strood shouted, “ We’ll
put an S on her! We’ll put an S on ! She
might get lost, and they wouldn't know who
owned her.”
So he pulled her dress down over her arm,
exposing the white skin of her bare shoulder, and
with glaring eyes and set teeth he planted on it
the smoking iron which sizzled on to her plump
flesh amid a little cloud of smoke.
“ Now you’ll bear a mark on you to tell who
you belong to, my lady,” he cackled.
For a second or two the men stood with their
mouths open, still holding her and still looking
at Strood.
Bess, with a last wild yell that still echoed
round, had fainted from terror, and even the
horrible injury had not recalled her to her senses.
She lay now a dead weight in Peter’s arms.
Peter let her go with both hands when he
realised the situation, and both his fists seemed
to crash into Strood’s face the moment after.
Mike Strood went down to earth like a log.
“ You murderous villain,
you ! ” and he stood over him with clenched fists,
a leg on each side, looking down on him and wait,
ing for him to move.
He did move, which was foolish of him, and
then and there Peter killed Mike Strood.
The hot iron, which nobody had thought about,
had fallen harmlessly out of the way.
Then the men laid Bess down on the ground,
and ran for some water.
G. BRON.
The Three Quests.
[FOR THE BULLEITN. ]
FOR a thousand miles as the black crow flies,
and a full three-score beside.
If you go nor’-west by the Wallaby Track
and over the Great Divide,
You will come to a high and a lonely hill, with
never a shrub or tree ;
Though hardly a man of all our men has ever been
there to see.
But only the aboriginal men of the tribe of the
Native Bear
Have scaled the rocks to precipitous heights to
hold corroborees there ;
To leap and to twist like painted snakes in the
light of the languid moon,
To the mystic rites of the tribal dance and the
chant of their ancient rune.
And he that has charge of the Talking Stick that
is carven with signs of dread,
Isold Apura; by right of skill he walks at his
people’s head.
The words that he speaks are few and terse, and
the deeds that he does are grim,
And woe to the breaker of tribal law that falls to
the clutch of him.
There were three adventurous pioneers who
turned to the ways out back,
From out of the town and over the down to the
desolate Wallaby Track;
By bed of river and crest of hill and homesteads
rare they hied
Till they passed the belt of the Saltbush runs and
the ridge of the Great Divide.
Now one had a quest most dear to his breast, and
he followed it fast and far ;
He urged it south by the harbor mouth and north
by the northern star ;
It led him into the tropical zones, and into
Antarctic cold,
And he said, “ No rest till I find my quest, the
mountain of solid gold.”
And one had a quest most dear to his breast; he
followed it far and wide ;
By Tiber and Seine he sought in vain; by marvel-
lous Ganges’ side;
By Papuan hills and Asian rills, in the fountain
of Tears and Truth,
Saying, “ Never I rest till I find my quest, the
Spring of Eternal Youth.”
And one had a quest most dear to his breast; he
followed it everywhere,
By the Bodhisat, and the land of Joss, and the
place of the Moslem’s prayer;
By Christian spire ; he did not tire, but ever he
sought a sign,
Saying, “Never I rest till I find my quest, a
proof of the Myth Divine.”
They came to the place of the lonely hill at the
noon of a dreary day,
Anri they spoke the tribe of the Native Bear;
eaters of men were they !
And the three knew the place was an evil place;
they woke to a dreadful fear,
For they saw in the ebony faces there the old
man-hungering leer.
Now, sudden and swift each man was seized and
haled to a lonely height—
The tribe of the Bear had arms of steel and his
withes are fast and tight;
To three high stakes on a ledge of rock they were
bound and left alone.
For that is the rite of the white man’s meat, and
sweetens the blood and bone.
With faces turned to the lonely peak, they hung
in a ghastly row,
And they saw the sun sink into the West, and it
sank in a lurid glow ;
Throughout the day they had gasped and groaned
and writhed in his burning beams,
Till their eyes grew blurred, and their senses dim,
and they woke the stars with their screams.
The chill of the night went through their bones as
a languid moon arose;
The dews but moistened their burning lips and
lengthened their dreary throes ;
Till the long night passed, and the East grew
grey, and the wings of the morning stirred,
When a miracle came to their dying eyes, for a
wonderful thing occurred.
The clouds drew back, like a misty veil, and away
from the mountain rolled ;
The first rays struck on the lofty peak, and
turned it to living gold ;
And one cried out, “Behold my quest —the moun-
tain of gold at last! ”
His tortured face transfigured with joy, he sank
in his bonds and passed.
The beams came down to a waterfall and lit it
with rainbow hues,
Till it leapt like a myriad living things in a
shower of fairy dews.
And one cried out, “’Tis the Spring of Youth—
the Hope of the World is found ! ”
And his ghost went out exultingly, leaving his
carcase bound.
Then the sun looked over the mountain rim
and into the third man’s eyes—
Eyes that were blurred with the mists of death—
and he said, “It is Paradise !
The gates of Glory are opened wide; a sign ! a
sign ! ” he cried.
And with that splendor upon his face he drooped
his head and died.
For a thousand miles as the black crow flies, and
a full three-score beside,
I went nor’-west by the Wallaby Track, and
over the Great Divide.
And came to a high and lonely bill with never a
shrub or tree.
Where three grim posts on a ledge of rock bore
witness of this to me!
TIC,
C.G.A. COLLES.

A Great Sufferer Permanently Cured.

supports the claims made for Bile Beans —that they cure promptly, positively,
and permanently. Some four years ago this gentleman forwarded us a

Bile Beans, and he sends the letter below to inform us that he still enjoys
good health, and has had no return of his old complaints. Such testimony
is of the utmost value to the general public,
because the need of a remedy devoid of harm-
ful drugs, gentle in action, certain in effect,
and which has stood the test of time, is as
great to-day as it ever was. Such a medicine
is Bile Beans, the world’s greatest specific for
