The Hat.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Twelve millions of the people of Britain are paupers.—
Campbell-Bannerman'.
The income of the English landlords amounts to
£150,000,000 per annum. — Statistics.
Take off the lid of England—let us gaze in the
seething vat;
And what is England’s secret —the secret of
England’s fat ?
Behold the stewing paupers—behold them in their
plight;
And these are the pride of Britain —of the proud
old race and white !
These are the living secret, these anguished things
that crawl
Through the rain-swept streets of London by the
Fat One’s palace wall.
These have produced her stoutness —aye, these
have produced her fat —
These have supplied their Master with his wealth
and his Shining Hat!
Turn back to the South Sea atoll, where the tiny
polypes toil;
These are the English people—these insect things
that moil,
These are their polype-symbols —like their medul-
lary pulp
Are the blood and the grief and labor that the
English landlords gulp !
So low that one may not tell them .from proto-
plasmic forms—
Aipt building their insect-Empire beneath the
y pacific’s storms;
So "'Britain’s paupers labor —poor Sandy, and
Hodge, and Pat—
For the sake of England’s fatness and the shine
on Balfour’s Hat!
The polypes toil and perish—they spend their
strength and die;
And the worn-out paupers sicken in the noisome
English sty.
Like zoophytes true they labor, these pale
cephalopods
Who bow their knees in worship to Britain’s
crazy gods!
As the builders of the atoll feed the cruising bird
of prey,
So the paupers bred in England feed the land-
lord race for aye—
Yea, they bow to the landlord fetich, and it
towereth like Ararat;
So hooray for England’s fatness, and the shine on
Cohen’s Hat !
There’s another Thames of sorrow —it flows to
that Sluggish Sea
That is fed from the eyes of paupers, grown old in
their misery.
There’s a Bridge that spans that River —the
River of Paupers’ Tears—
And an army marches o’er it with its face to the
hopeless years.
Lo ! a chariot thunders after, and Mammon sits
on high,
While the paupers groan hosannas as the Empire-
sham rolls by.
Aye, they cheer for the good King Cohen —they
huzza for the plutocrat
Who feasts on England’s fatness, and struts in a
Blatant Hat !
Ye prate of England’s glory, but what of Eng-
land’s shame,
Since ye bow to a tawdry greatness that is great-
ness but in name?
For what is her vaunted freedom but freedom to
oppress—
To wring the landlord’s tribute through blood and
bitterness 1
Far better that Saxon England, ere the million-
aires and sots
Had risen from slums like London’s, where the
heart of a people rots !
Far better the feudal baron who midst his vassals
sat,
Than the pauper-fattened landlord, who props up
a Garish Hat.
Art proud of the Newer Conquest —of Cohen s
diadem ?
Go ask of the English paupers—win England
back for them !
The “precedents may broaden,” but heavier grow
the gyves
That fetter England’s millions and crush their
stunted lives !
Dost wait for a bloodier Senlac—when the hungry
hosts shall rise,
With the grief and the pain of ages in their
anguished, sunken eves ?
Beware of the Day of Spurning, O lisping Aris-
tocrat,
When the English serfs shall trample on thy
Symbolistic Hat !
Huzza for the Time of Spurning, when they tear
each fetich down —
When the paupers smite their landlords with the
sceptre and the crown ?
When they heap up age-worn parchments in a
mighty pile and high.
And the deeds of a dukeling’s freehold go blazing
’thwart the sky !
Far better the days of Chaucer than Austin’s
pinchbeck times.
When the English landlords batten on the fruit
of a thousand crimes !
So huzza for that Day of Spurning, when the
landlord-joss lies flat,
And the pauper welts the landscape with his
owner’s Burnished Hat!
Replace the lid of England—we have gazed in the
seething rat;
We have spied out England's secret —the secret
of England's fat!
We have seen the starving paupers who shiver
athwart the night;
And these are the pride of England—of the proud
old race and white ?
Aye, these are the ghastly secret —these homeless
forms that crawl
Through the windswept streets of London on the lee
of the palace wall.
Lo ! these hare produced her stoutness—yea , these
have supplied her fat—
How long will they feed the landlord who struts in
a Heavenly Hat ?-
vtc.
GRANT HERVEY.
A Bit of London
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WE saw It from the river as we
steamed slowly up. The Kentish
coast was in our wake, and 600
Australians streamed on deck and looked fixedly
at the sky above.
It was not fog nor smoke we beheld, neither
was it river haze. It seemed to hang from the
sky in a solid, elephant-colored mass. A pair of
strong field-glasses revealed what lay beyond—-a
vast, illimitable expanse of roofs and bridges
overhung by a canopy of livid fumes.
“Surely this must be Etna or Pompeii,” said
someone. “That stuff isn t smoke. It s ashes!
“ It’s London all right! ” a big-voiced bushman
pushed forward through the crowd. “You
couldn’t ringbark the blamed atmosphere with an
axe.”
Here it was at last, sprawling east and west,
clinging like an unclean devil to the heels of
Father Thames. London ought to be painted in
masses. You must speak of its legions, not its
types; audit is dirty beyond the dreams of the
wildest Bohemian.
Think of it, comrades outback, a city scoured
by the ashes of a million hearths and smoke
stacks—a city where the black grime settles on
your window pane at dawn and stifles your
breath. If one lived for a year under the hip of
Stromboli or Etna one might acquire a taste for
London. For weeks after landing we felt no
movement in the air—only the pressure of the
elephant-hued atmosphere.
And the metal-eyed little cockney woman who
steals our coal leans over the back-yard fence,
and asks us whether we are enjoying the really
beautiful weather.
In Australia, while travelling from Menindie
to Broken Hill, I met children who had never
seen rain. In the narrow lanes within the heart
of the East end of London it is possible to meet
men who have never felt the sun on their pallid
faces.
And yet the smoke-kippered little cockney
possesses a low-grade vitality that pulls him,
coughing and sneezing, through his horrible
winter. Wherever you turn there are hospitals,
black-faced and smoke-charred. Wait for a
moment outside, and watch the procession of
casualties streaming in. Here’s a man from the
Bakerloo tube almost cut in halves by the wheels of
a car. Five passengers from a capsized motor-car
are rushed in. Then comes the city ambulance
with the latest horror—three men asphyxiated in
the giant sewers.
A moment later a troop of hussars amble
towards Piccadilly, their wine-red cloaks floating
about their saddles, their plumes aslant in the fog-
smeared sunlight. Reefing and plunging, they go
through Leieester-square towards Mew Bond-
street, where the beautiful, tight-booted women
limp from their victorias, kissing their silken-
haired spaniels.
Many Englishwomen are beautiful even when
they are old, but these do not belong to London.
They come from the provinces, where the white
winters have reddened their blood and prepared
them for the ogres who live in the grey-faced
mansions beyond Westminster.
The Londoner is the most tolerant man on
earth. He is more tolerant than the Kanaka or
the Hebrew, more indifferent to your affairs than
a Chinaman or a whale. In Australia I have
been insulted and twitted a dozen times in one
morning on account of my negligent tie or the tilt
of my perspiring hat. In the Strand and Hyde
Park, London, I have seen Frenchmen from
Algiers wearing balloon-hipped trousers drawn
tight about the ankles, and as they strode along
no one turned or cast a look in their direction.
An Egyptian official, in a frock-coat and scarlet
fez, walks through the city unnoticed. Big-
beamed Dutchmen, fresh from the Ostend rabbit
boats, collide with the kippered cockney ; a swift
apology happens, and they pass.
Mew shapes and sensations stalk through
London every day, new forms of journalism, new
styles of cross-heading and type-display. To-day
they are producing “The Typhoon” at the
Hippodrome, a whirlwind play that smashes
steamboats and roots up the river from its bed.
To-morrow the heart of London will ache for a
portable Niagara or an African sirocco served up
behind the footlights, with acres of real Sahara
sand blowing into the orchestra.
Move from the city and you meet the kindly,
red-cheeked English whose hearts are strangely
indifferent to the bawbees or the clank of the
bullion-heap.
The streets are crowded with unemployed
artisans discharged from the Woolwich Arsenal
under Haldane’s new scheme of retrenchment.
Hundreds of them are finding billets in Germany
at the big gun-factories and depdts. Every dis-
charged Woolwich workman carries to Germany
the brains of a new torpedo or some secret of a
Dreadnought’s gun-room or structural defect.
If the Germans bombarded London, their siege-
guns would kill a Hebrew every nine seconds, a
Dago every twenty minutes, an Englishman every
hour, and a Scotchman once a week. At the end
of a’six months’ siege there would be 859,753
Hebrews left to claim damages for the loss of
their loan-offices and whelk-shops.
Statisticians may demur as to the correctness of
the Scotch fatalities in the above statement, but
the majority will agree that my Hebrew figures
are bed rock.
The legs of the average London horse appeal to
the sympathetic Australian visitor. The ceaseless
hammering along macadamised streets causes the
knees to “ mushroom ” and curve outward. I
recently attended an auction sale of ’bus horses
displaced by the London poison-car companies.
The sale was held in a reeking shed at the back
of St. Martin’s Lane. The horses, 800 in number,
were drafted in lots of 100 each. First lot offered
had evidently been drawn by Frank Mahony in
an embittered mood, and their front legs bulged
like the sinews of Achilles. To give the auc-
tioneer heart, I bid £5 for the first lot—one hun-
dred in number. A terrible silence fell on the
crowd as the auctioneer threw down his hammer
and looked at me.
“ For God’s sake, don’t come here blighting our
sales!” he said. The first lot, mostly wind-
broken and leg-smashed, went for £2O per head,
while many of the better stamp of ’bus haulers
fetched £4O.
“ Look at that lot! ” The auctioneer indicated
a troop of sore backs and swollen knees triumph-
antly. “ Look at ’em, gentlemen ! They’re fit
for the Household Cavalry.”
And nobody laughed. _
